# El Eco de la Nieve (Copia) (Copia)

## Prologue: Prólogo

```json
{
  "translated_text": "# Chapter 0: Prologue\n\n**AIGÜESTORTES, BOÍ VALLEY**\n**December Twentieth, Twenty Years Earlier**\n\nThe air wasn't breathed; it was chewed. It was a solid mass of ice needles that pierced Isabel's throat, tearing the soft tissue of her lungs with each desperate inhalation. There was no sound in the world, save for the roar of her own blood pounding inside her ears, a frantic, erratic drumming that threatened to burst her eardrums.\n\nShe ran. Or at least, her brain tried to, sending urgent commands to legs that had long since ceased to respond like flesh, behaving instead like waterlogged wood. The white mantle reached her knees, an immaculate and virgin ocean plotting to devour her. Each step demanded lifting a dead weight, breaking the frozen crust of the surface, and sinking into the treacherous powder beneath.\n\nThe forest was a cathedral of shadows. The fir trees, century-old giants laden with winter, leaned over her like silent judges, their branches bowed by the storm's weight. There was no moon. The sky had been swallowed by a mass of clouds so dense it blurred the border between earth and firmament. Only gray, black, and the dirty white of the ground existed, stretching infinitely in all directions.\n\nIsabel stumbled.\n\nIt wasn't a dramatic fall, but a clumsy collapse. Her hands, gloved in cheap wool that no longer protected anything, sank into the icy tapestry, searching for a handhold that didn't exist. The cold bit at her wrists, an icy burn that climbed her forearms like an invisible parasite. She tried to get up, pushing herself with a moan that died, drowned out by the vastness of the valley, but the ground gave way.\n\nThe slope was steeper than her eyes, blinded by frozen tears, had calculated. She rolled. The world became a blender of shadows and blunt impacts. A root hidden beneath the white mantle, hard as wrought iron, trapped her right boot while the rest of her body continued to spin from inertia.\n\nThe snap sounded like a dry branch breaking in the silence of a library.\n\nThe suffering wasn't immediate; first came the nausea, a dizzying wave that blurred her vision, and then, the fire. A liquid fire erupted from her ankle, radiating agonizing cramps that made her arch her back on the icy bed. Isabel's scream tore through the night, but the snow cover, that impassive sponge, absorbed the sound before it could travel more than three meters.\n\nShe lay there, supine, gazing into the unfathomable blackness as the flakes fell upon her face. They were enormous, soft, oblivious to her suffering. They settled on her eyelashes, on her parted lips, melting with the residual heat of her life.\n\n“Please…” she whispered. The word crystallized into a cloud of ephemeral vapor.\n\nNo one answered. Only the wind, whistling through the treetops with an ancient, cruel melody. Isabel tried to crawl. She dug her elbows into the icy mantle, ignoring the protests of her exhausted muscles, and pulled her body forward. Her right leg was a useless burden, an anchor of agony that carved a deep furrow behind her.\n\nShe advanced half a meter. Then another. Her breathing was a broken bellows.\n\nThen, the wind shifted.\n\nIt stopped carrying the scent of resin and damp earth. Suddenly, in the midst of the wild, an impossible scent emerged. It was clinical. Aseptic. A pungent fragrance of mint and hospital disinfectant that had no right to exist in that primordial forest.\n\nIsabel stopped. Instinct, that primitive animal dwelling at the base of the brain, made the hairs on her neck stand on end. The agony in her ankle faded into the background, eclipsed by a far older terror. She turned her head, her neck stiff from the cold, and looked towards the top of the slope she had fallen down.\n\nThere he was.\n\nIt wasn't a monster with sharp teeth, nor a beast of legend. It was a human silhouette, outlined against the faint luminosity reflected by the white shroud. A man. Motionless. The storm whipped at his coat, but he seemed oblivious to the weather's violence. He didn't pant. There was no urgency in his posture. He watched her with the patience of one who observes an hourglass, knowing the last grain is about to fall.\n\nIsabel wanted to scream again, but her throat had closed up, a pipe choked by panic.\n\nThe figure began to descend.\n\nHe wasn't running. He didn't rush with the fury of a hungry predator. His steps were methodical, calculated. He sank his boots into the immaculate tapestry with almost choreographic precision, advancing effortlessly, as if levitating above the disaster. As he drew closer, Isabel could make out disjointed details: an impeccably tailored wool coat, black leather gloves, a face partially hidden by a gray scarf.\n\nWhat terrified her most wasn't that he carried a weapon —his hands appeared empty— but his eyes.\n\nWhen he was close enough for her to see their color, she realized they were two pools of blue ice. There was no anger in them. No feverish glint of lust nor the darkness of hatred. They were empty of human passion, yet filled with a terrible purpose. He looked at her as a sculptor gazes upon a rough block of marble, evaluating veins and fractures.\n\n“No…” Isabel tried to pull back, kicking with her good leg, uselessly throwing snow at him. “Don’t come closer!”\n\nThe man stopped a step away from her. He knelt with fluid elegance, his knees not touching the damp ground. The mint fragrance intensified, enveloping her, suffocating the stench of her own fear.\n\n“Shhh,” the sound was soft, almost maternal.\n\nHe extended a gloved hand. Isabel braced for the blow. She closed her eyes, preparing for the impact that would end everything. But the blow never came. Instead, she felt a delicate touch on her cheek, the cold leather brushing against her fevered skin. He brushed aside a strand of wet hair that had stuck to her forehead with a tenderness that chilled her blood more than the icy environment itself.\n\n“You’ve done very well, Isabel,” he said. His voice was a soft, cultured baritone, without any harshness. “You’ve fought bravely. That’s important. The struggle preserves the vitality of color in the cheeks.”\n\nShe opened her eyes, confused, trembling uncontrollably.\n\n“What…?” she stammered, her teeth chattering.\n\n“But it’s over now. The chaos has ended,” the man slid his hand down, tracing her neck, her shoulder, until it reached her left arm. His movements were those of a veteran doctor, sure and efficient. “Look at your leg. It’s broken. The asymmetry is… regrettable, but correctable. Suffering is disfiguring your expression.”\n\nIsabel tried to pull her arm away, but he held her. He didn't grip with brute force; he merely applied the exact pressure at the precise points to nullify her resistance. With his other hand, he extracted something from the inner pocket of his coat. A metallic case gleamed fleetingly.\n\n“I’m not going to harm you, my dear,” he whispered, bringing his face closer to hers. His breath smelled of nothing, as if his interior were sterilized. “Suffering is vulgar. Fear is transient. I offer you something better.”\n\nA syringe appeared in his palm. The liquid inside was transparent, viscous.\n\n“No! Help!” Isabel gathered what air remained in her lungs, but he had already acted.\n\nWith surgical dexterity, he slid the needle through the layers of clothing, seeking the deltoid muscle with a precision that spoke of infinite practice. The prick was insignificant compared to the agony of her ankle.\n\n“Permanence,” he said as he pressed the plunger. He did it slowly, savoring the moment, ensuring the fluid entered at the correct pace. “I offer you the perfection of stillness.”\n\nIsabel felt the liquid enter her system. It didn't burn. On the contrary, it was as if she had been injected with liquid sun. A golden warmth began to spread from her shoulder, flowing down her arm, rising towards her neck, enveloping her heart.\n\nThe man withdrew the needle and put away the instruments with methodical calm. He gently stroked her face again, wiping away a tear that had frozen on her cheekbone.\n\n“The world is dirty, Isabel. Time corrupts everything. Old age, sickness, oblivion…” He spoke as if revealing the secret of the universe to her. “But not you. You will be immaculate.”\n\nThe throbbing in her ankle began to fade. It didn't disappear; it simply ceased to matter. It felt distant, as if it were happening to someone else miles away. The trees no longer seemed like threatening judges and became benevolent witnesses. The icy bed was no longer cold; it was a down blanket, soft and cozy.\n\nIsabel's eyelids weighed tons. She tried to keep them open, to fix her gaze on those blue ice eyes, but the world was becoming blurry, soft, beautiful.\n\n“Sleep,” he commanded. It wasn’t a suggestion.\n\nIsabel’s breathing slowed. The drumming in her ears faded, replaced by an absolute, perfect silence. Her body relaxed completely, surrendering to gravity and the icy bed. The last sensation she had wasn't fear. It was an absurd, chemical, and artificial gratitude towards that dark angel who had stolen her suffering.\n\nThe Sculptor watched consciousness leave the girl's eyes, leaving behind a glassy, empty gaze, ready to be immortalized. He nodded to himself, satisfied with the blank canvas that destiny had delivered to him.\n\nThe storm raged, covering them both, but he was in no hurry. Art required time. And now, they had all eternity.\n",
  "source_language": "es",
  "target_language": "en",
  "notes": "Key translation decisions aimed at preserving the literary style and natural flow in English include:\n- **"Boí Valley"**: Adapted 'Valle de Boí' to common English usage for geographical names.\n- **Figurative Language**: Maintained strong imagery and metaphors, such as "air wasn't breathed; it was chewed," "cathedral of shadows," "icy tapestry," "liquid fire," "impassive sponge," "pools of blue ice," and "liquid sun."\n- **Word Choice**: Selected evocative verbs and adjectives (e.g., "plotting to devour," "blunt impacts," "supine," "nullify her resistance," "surgical dexterity," "transient," "corrupts," "stolen her suffering") to match the original's rich prose.\n- **Pacing**: Ensured sentence structure and rhythm contribute to the building tension and eventual serene, yet sinister, conclusion.\n- **Cultural Adaptation**: Converted "kilómetros de distancia" to "miles away" for a more natural English expression of distance, while retaining "meters" for the precise measurement as it felt less disruptive to the overall tone."
```

## Chapter 1: El Silencio Blanco

Aigüestortes, Catalan Pyrenees
December 20, 2018

Ice possesses a brutal honesty that people lack. It doesn't pretend, doesn't manipulate, and, above all, it doesn't forgive. If you make a mistake in the city, you apologize; if you make one up here, you die. That binary simplicity was the only thing that kept my demons at bay.

I adjusted my ice axe's leash and dug my crampons into the vitrified surface of the north glacier. The sound was a dry crunch, like a bone snapping, which the wind instantly devoured. At three thousand meters up, oxygen is a luxury and silence, a sentence.

I looked at my wrist. Dad's old analog watch read ten twelve. The face was scratched, but the ticking remained constant, a metallic metronome synchronizing my breathing with the mountain. I took off the thermal glove from my left hand for just a few seconds, exposing my skin to the glacial air. The crescent-shaped scar crossing my palm seemed to contract with the cold, an old friend reminding me why I'd traded police interrogations for glaciology.

People lie. Ice strata do not.

"Let's see what you have for me today," I whispered, and my voice sounded alien, small against the white immensity.

I knelt beside the main crevice. My hand drilling equipment rested beside me. My job was to document a slow tragedy: the glacier's retreat. Global warming was flaying the mountain, stripping away the white layer meter by meter, year after year, exposing rocks that hadn't seen the sun in centuries. The earth was vomiting its history.

I inserted the auger. The metal bit into the surface. I turned the crank, aiming to extract a clean core sample. I needed air bubbles trapped decades ago, tiny time capsules that would tell me what the atmosphere was like before the world decided to slowly commit suicide.

I liked this. The order. The predictable chemistry of solid water. Here there were no hidden motives, no psychopaths with God complexes, no victims whose names you had to forget to sleep at night. Here, there was only physics and thermodynamics.

Until the sun shifted its angle.

An unusual glint caught my peripheral attention, about five meters below, on the slope descending towards the lateral moraine. It wasn't the specular gleam of quartz, nor the typical bluish refraction of compressed ice.

It was something matte. Opaque.

I straightened up, feeling my thigh muscles protest the effort. I walked carefully, making sure the steel points of my boots bit firmly before taking the next step. The wind blew from the north, bringing invisible needles seeking any gap between my scarf and my goggles.

As I approached, the chromatic anomaly became more evident. It looked like a pinkish stain buried under a translucent layer about ten centimeters thick.

"Trash," I muttered. Probably some plastic wrapper left by an irresponsible climber in the nineties, that the thaw was now deciding to return.

Professional indignation prompted me to clear the area. I hated to see my sanctuary desecrated by human negligence. I knelt again, this time with the adze of my ice axe.

I tapped gently.
*Clack.*
Shards of ice flew up like broken diamonds.

I tapped again.
*Clack.*

The surface layer gave way. I swept my gloved hand over it to clear away the resulting snow dust. What was exposed wasn't plastic. Nor was it synthetic fabric faded by UV rays.

I froze. The air caught in my throat, refusing to enter or leave.

The texture beneath the remaining ice had pores. It had lines. It had a biological geography that my brain, trained for years in the behavioral analysis unit, recognized before my eyes were willing to accept it.

I cleaned with frenzy, forgetting scientific caution, scraping the surface with the desperation of someone trying to erase a stain that won't come off. The ice, now polished by my glove, became a perfect window, a macabre museum display.

It wasn't trash.

It was a hand.

A human hand, pale, with fingers slightly curled in a gesture of eternal rest, not agony. The nails had a purplish hue, but the skin... the skin was immaculate. Perfectly preserved by the sub-zero temperature.

I fell backward, sliding a couple of meters until my boots arrested the descent. Bile rose in my esophagus, acidic and hot.

No. Not here. Not now.

I had fled to the top of the world to escape death, to avoid looking another corpse in the eye, to avoid asking myself *why*. And yet, the mountain had betrayed me. The glacier, in its retreat, wasn't just releasing gases and rocks. It was releasing secrets.

I forced myself to look again, fighting the tremor that rose from my gut and spread to my fingertips. That hand wasn't alone. It plunged into the darkness of the deep ice, connected to an arm, a shoulder, a body that the mountain had held in a jealous, glacial embrace.

There, beneath my feet, someone was waiting.

And by the position of the fingers, relaxed, almost elegant, I knew with the certainty of old instincts that this hadn't been an accident. The mountain kills with violence; it breaks, tears, and twists. But this... this had an artificial serenity.

The silence of the valley, which only minutes before seemed a balm, suddenly transformed into a silent scream. I was alone. Completely alone with something that shouldn't exist.

I looked at the hand trapped beneath the glass. It seemed to be waving to me from the other side of the mirror, inviting me back to the hell I thought I'd escaped.

## Chapter 2: Ecos de Cristal

CHAPTER 2: Crystal Echoes

Aigüestortes, Vall de Boí
December 20, 2018

The descent from the glacial moraine was not a journey, but a geological escape. Gravity tugged at Elena Vives' boots with the insistence of an anchor seeking the seabed, while the wind howled a warning that only fools would ignore. Every step on the crusted snow was a crunch that reverberated in her spine, a sonic reminder of human fragility before the mountain's indifference.

She drove the Land Rover around the tight bends of the service road, gripping the steering wheel so hard her knuckles turned white. The vehicle's heater roared at full blast, spitting out dry, synthetic air that irritated her nostrils, but the cold she felt had nothing to do with the ambient temperature. It was an internal chill, a seed of ice planted in her stomach an hour ago, up there, where the world lost its colors to become white and blue.

She glanced sideways at the passenger seat. There rested her digital camera, a black, compact object that now weighed more than all her climbing gear. It held the proof. The anomaly. The reason why the tremor in her right hand, the one holding the gear stick, didn't obey the diesel engine's vibration.

Upon reaching the village, normalcy struck her with the violence of a slap. Christmas lights twinkled in stone shop windows, tourists unloaded skis from family cars, and smoke from chimneys drew gray columns against a sky that was starting to darken prematurely. No one knew what the glacier had spat out, nor did they suspect that, just a few vertical kilometers away, the beauty of the landscape concealed a crystal tomb.

Elena parked in front of the local police station, a slate and wood building that tried to blend in with the valley's traditional architecture, though the radio antenna on the roof broke the rustic illusion. She switched off the engine. The sudden silence in the cabin allowed the ticking of her wrist to become audible. She looked down at the analog men's watch, too large for her arm, its leather strap worn by use and time. The hands advanced relentlessly, oblivious to the horror.

“Calculation and evidence,” she murmured to herself, reciting the mantra she had abandoned three years ago.

She rubbed the crescent-shaped scar on her left palm with her thumb, an automatic, almost superstitious gesture, before opening the door and facing the bureaucracy.

The interior of the police station smelled of synthetic pine, old paper, and over-brewed coffee. The thermal contrast fogged her ski goggles, which hung around her neck, forcing her to blink to adjust to the yellowish fluorescent light. It wasn't a modern big-city station; there were no bulletproof windows or metal detectors at the entrance here. Only a varnished wooden counter and, behind it, a man who seemed carved from the same rock as the surrounding mountains.

Lucas Riera looked up from a grease-stained file. His black hair was flecked with premature gray at the temples, as if the snow from outside had decided to make its home there. His deep brown eyes carried that kind of weariness that isn't cured by sleeping, but by forgetting.

“Can I help you?” he asked. His voice was deep, a baritone resonance that filled the small reception area.

Elena approached the counter, ignoring the pang of claustrophobia that always assailed her in enclosed, unfamiliar spaces. She needed logic to be her shield.

“I’m Dr. Elena Vives, a glaciologist from the National Center. I'm conducting a recession study on the north face of the Perdiguero.”

Lucas placed his pen on the desk with deliberate slowness. He observed Elena's technical clothing, the ice axe still hanging from her backpack, and the tension in her jaw.

“I know who you are,” he said, leaning back in his chair. The wood creaked under his weight. “We received notification of your permit last week. Though I didn't expect to see you down here so soon. The big storm isn't supposed to arrive until tomorrow night.”

“I didn’t come down because of the weather.”

Lucas arched an eyebrow, a minimal gesture that denoted a patience trained for dealing with nervous outsiders.

“If it’s about stolen gear, I warn you that during high season it’s difficult to keep track of every hiker who…”

“I’ve found a body.”

The three words dropped into the room with the weight of stones thrown into a still pond. The hum of the refrigerator in the corner seemed to pause for a second. Lucas straightened up, losing that relaxed posture of a rural official. His expression shifted, moving from bored courtesy to professional alert, though tinged with skepticism.

“A body?” he repeated, as if tasting the word. “An alpinist? We’ve had a couple of rescues this season, but no one’s gone missing this week. Perhaps a lone climber who fell…”

“It wasn’t a fall,” Elena interrupted. Her voice sounded metallic, devoid of the emotional inflection one would expect from someone who had just seen a corpse. It was the tone of the forensic pathologist she had been, not the scientist she was. “The position is incorrect. There are no visible contusions on the exposed dermis, nor fractures on the visible extremities. The body isn’t at the foot of a cliff, officer. It’s in the ice.”

Lucas stood up. He was tall, over six feet, and his broad shoulders blocked much of the light coming from the back window. As he moved, Elena noticed a slight irregularity in his gait, an almost imperceptible limp in his right leg that he tried to disguise by shifting his weight to his left. The humidity from the incoming storm must have been affecting his joints.

“In the ice,” he repeated, coming out from behind the counter and approaching her. Now that he was close, Elena could smell stale tobacco on his uniform. “Listen, Doctor. The glacier moves. It expels remains from decades ago. Last year we found a Civil War boot. Sometimes, the thaw brings ancient accidents to light. If it’s as deep as you say…”

“It’s not ancient.”

Elena abruptly took off her backpack and placed it on a plastic chair. Her hands trembled slightly, but her movements were precise. From it, she extracted the camera.

“The clothes are modern. Synthetic fibers, not wool or leather. And the preservation…” Elena swallowed, feeling a rough knot in her throat. “Its condition was anomalous. Ice usually crushes, deforms. The pressure of tons of frozen water crushes bones. This… this seems cured.”

Lucas stared at her for a moment, assessing her sanity. He saw a woman with eyes injected with adrenaline, brown hair plastered to her forehead with dried sweat, and a scar on her hand that she compulsively rubbed. In his experience, city folk tended to exaggerate nature’s brutality. They saw a fox and cried wolf. They saw a deer bone and cried murder.

“Look,” Lucas said, softening his tone, trying to handle the situation like one calms a frightened child. “I understand it’s been a shock. I’ll go up with a team once the storm passes. We’ll formalize the discovery, call the duty judge, and…”

“You’re not listening to me,” Elena hissed. The condescension in the officer’s voice lit an old fuse in her chest, the fury of not being taken seriously when the data was irrefutable. “If you wait for the storm to pass, the snow will cover the crevasse entrance. The barometric pressure will change. We could lose the exact location. I need you to mobilize scientific personnel now. I need a chain of custody.”

“The scientist?” Lucas let out a short, dry, humorless laugh. “Dr. Vives, this is Aigüestortes, not Madrid. ‘The scientist’ is a guy in Lleida who takes three hours to get here if the road is clear. And believe me, with what’s coming from the north, the road won’t be clear for long.”

He turned, ready to return to his desk and bury the matter in a ‘remains discovery’ form until the weather improved. To him, it was a formality. An unfortunate soul claimed by the mountain, now revealed. Tragic, yes, but part of the cycle of that place.

“See for yourself.”

Elena switched on the camera and extended her arm, interposing the digital screen between the officer’s face and his comfortable denial.

Lucas sighed, frustrated by the insistence, and looked down at the small luminous rectangle.

The image was sharp, despite the meager light from the crevasse. The flash had captured the deep, almost black, blue of the ancient ice. And trapped within that glassy matrix, like an insect in amber, was a face.

Lucas’s world stopped.

It wasn't a poetic metaphor. It was a physiological interruption. His breathing hitched, his diaphragm paralyzed by an urgent command from a brain that didn't know how to process the visual input. The sound of the heater, the hum of the refrigerator, the distant traffic… everything vanished, absorbed by the sepulchral silence emanating from that photograph.

The eyes closed. Eyelashes frosted. The skin pale, marble-like, yet disturbingly intact. And that hair… that unruly lock he had so often brushed from a warm, living forehead.

He felt the blood drain from his face, receding to his heels, leaving him with a sensation of vertigo. His large, calloused hands, which had held weapons and cradled children, closed over the edge of the counter, seeking support, because the ground beneath his feet seemed to have turned to liquid.

Elena observed the transformation. She watched the police chief’s mask of authority crack and crumble, revealing something raw and devastated beneath. It wasn’t a professional’s reaction to an anonymous corpse. It wasn’t disgust or forensic curiosity.

It was recognition.

Lucas extended a trembling finger towards the screen, but didn’t quite touch it, as if fearing the cold of the image might burn his skin.

“No…” The sound scraped from his throat, a denial that held no power over reality. “It can’t be.”

He looked up at Elena. His brown eyes, once tired, were now wells of absolute panic. The barrier between them, the distance between the local officer and the foreign scientist, had evaporated, replaced by a terrible gravity that bound them together.

“Who is it?” Elena asked. There was no demand in her voice now, only the cautious softness of one who knows they are stepping on a landmine.

Lucas didn’t answer immediately. He couldn’t. His mind was struggling to reconcile two impossible realities: the woman he had seen buying bread two days ago, smiling with that red scarf she always wore, and the serene, eternal, and dead figure looking at him from the ice.

“Isabel,” he finally whispered. The name broke on his lips. “It’s Isabel.”

Elena’s watch continued to tick away the seconds, indifferent. Tick-tock. Tick-tock. But time in the police station had changed its texture. It was no longer a winter afternoon awaiting a storm. It was the beginning of a nightmare.

Elena slowly withdrew the camera, putting away the image like one holstering a weapon.

“Then it’s not an ancient accident,” she said, and the phrase hung in the charged air, a sentence that closed all exits. “We have to go back up. Before the snow erases her from the map.”

Lucas nodded, though his gaze remained lost in the void where the screen had been. Outside, the wind began to lash at the windows with greater force, making the panes vibrate. The White Beast was coming, and they had just unearthed something far worse than a storm.

## Chapter 3: La Morgue Improvisada

# Literary Style Guide: Javier Castillo

## The Master of Contemporary Psychological Thriller

Javier Castillo has revolutionized the Spanish suspense landscape with a formula that combines the dizzying pace of the American *bestseller* with emotional depth and a fragmented puzzle structure. This guide details the pillars of his style.

### 1. Narrative Architecture: The Temporal Puzzle

Castillo's most recognizable characteristic is the break from linearity. His stories are not told from beginning to end, but are assembled.

#### A. Multilayered Structure

*   **Intertwined Timelines:** The plot is usually divided into at least two or three timeframes (e.g., the distant past of trauma, the recent past of a disappearance, and the present of the investigation).
*   **Precise Headers:** Each chapter must begin with a location and a date.
    *   **Example:** New York, 1998 / Mississippi, 2020.
*   **Puzzle Effect:** Information is dosed in such a way that a revelation in the past explains strange behavior in the present, keeping the reader in a constant state of "reconstruction."

#### B. Pacing and the Cliffhanger

*   **Short Chapters:** Facilitate quick, compulsive reading ("just one more page").
*   **Suspenseful Endings:** Practically all chapters end with an impactful sentence, an unanswered question, or a revelation that changes the perception of what came before.

### 2. Tone and Atmosphere

Castillo builds an atmosphere of melancholic tension. It's not just suspense; it's the weight of accumulated pain.

*   **Textual Cinematography:** Descriptions are conceived as camera shots. The focus is on what the character sees and physically feels (the cold, the racing pulse, the dim light).
*   **Aesthetic "Americanization":** Setting his works in the U.S. (especially the East Coast or the Deep South) allows him to use archetypes of the classic thriller (the FBI, major newspapers, towns with secrets) that are familiar and universal.
*   **Emotional Darkness:** The environment often reflects the characters' internal state: rain, basements, dense forests, or empty rooms that emphasize loneliness.

### 3. Language and Stylistic Devices

His prose is direct, yet it is sprinkled with very powerful and visceral lyrical images.

#### A. Sensory and Abstract Metaphors

Castillo has a unique way of describing feelings. He avoids clichés and seeks physical images for pain or fear.

*   **Device:** Comparing an emotion to a sharp object or a destructive natural phenomenon.
*   **Example:** "Guilt was a hungry animal gnawing at my nerve endings every time I closed my eyes."

#### B. Diction and Recurring Vocabulary

He uses words that evoke fragility and mystery:

*   *Pieces, glass, broken, abyss, darkness, heartbeat, destiny, chance, pieces, truth, lie.*

#### C. The Introspective First Person

He often gives voice to the protagonist through a very intimate first person. This allows for:

*   **Unreliable narrator:** The reader only knows what the character remembers or chooses to tell.
*   **Emotional immersion:** Feeling the character's confusion and anxiety as your own.

### 4. Character Construction: "Broken People"

In Castillo's universe, no one is "fine." Everyone carries emotional baggage.

*   **Trauma as the Driving Force:** The central conflict always stems from an unhealed wound from the past.
*   **Professions of Truth:** His protagonists are often journalists (like Miren Triggs), writers, or investigators. People whose social function is to ask questions, which justifies the search for clues.
*   **Moral Duality:** He explores the boundary between good and evil. His "heroes" often commit morally questionable acts to achieve an end.

### 5. Central Themes

To write with Castillo's signature style, the plot must pivot on these axes:

*   **The Disappearance:** The void left by someone who vanishes without a trace.
*   **Destiny vs. Chance:** The idea that an insignificant decision can destroy an entire life years later.
*   **Family Secrets:** The family as a place of love, but also as the repository of the darkest lies.
*   **Identity:** People who discover they are not who they thought they were.

### Checklist for the "Castillo Style"

*   [ ] Have I started the chapter with Date and Location?
*   [ ] Have I ended the chapter with a shocking twist or revelation?
*   [ ] Are there at least two intertwining timelines?
*   [ ] Have I used a visceral metaphor to describe fear or sadness?
*   [ ] Is my protagonist a person "marked" by a past event?
*   [ ] Is the pace fast enough to prevent the reader from stopping?

---

# CHAPTER 3: The Improvised Morgue

**Aigüestortes, December 20, 2018**

The journey from the police station to the Primary Care Center was an exercise in silent restraint. The snow, which hours earlier had fallen with the delicacy of a warning, had transformed into solid curtains battering the patrol car's windshield. The wipers moaned rhythmically, struggling against the white accumulation, a desperate metronome marking the time slipping through their fingers.

Elena Vives watched the landscape blur on the other side of the glass. The valley closed in on itself, swallowing the pines and roads beneath a gullet of fog and ice. Beside her, Lucas Riera drove, his white knuckles clenched on the steering wheel. The police chief hadn't uttered a word in the ten-minute drive, but the cabin smelled of his fear; an acrid, metallic aroma, distinct from the stale tobacco ingrained in the upholstery. Every time he braked on a curve, his right leg trembled slightly, an involuntary spasm he tried to disguise by clenching his jaw. The change in atmospheric pressure must have been tormenting his old injury, though physical pain was probably the least of his burdens now that Isabel's body traveled in the forensic van behind them.

The vehicle stopped in front of the emergency entrance, a stone and slate structure that looked more like a beleaguered fortress than a place of healing.

"Arnald is waiting for us downstairs," Lucas said. His voice sounded sanded, rough from disuse and excessive emotion.

They descended to the basement via a service ramp, pushing the gurney covered by a black vinyl bag. The wheels creaked on the linoleum, a sharp sound that echoed off the bare concrete walls. This was no real morgue; Aigüestortes didn't have enough dead people to justify one. It was a refrigerated storage room, hastily set up, where boxes of medical supplies were stacked in the corners like mute spectators.

Doctor Arnald Soler waited in the center of the room, under the flickering light of two fluorescent tubes that bathed the room in a sickly, greenish hue. The doctor adjusted his latex gloves with dry, precise movements. Despite the ambient temperature, which hovered around five degrees Celsius, a thin layer of sweat beaded on his broad forehead.

"Lucas," the doctor greeted, ignoring Elena for a moment. His tone lacked the paternal warmth he usually dispensed. "I regret that we have to do this here. The conditions are... suboptimal."

"Do what you can, Arnald," the policeman replied, staying by the door, as if an invisible barrier prevented him from approaching the metal table.

Elena took a step forward. The air smelled of a disturbing mixture of subterranean dampness and that pungent mint-and-disinfectant aroma that seemed to emanate from Doctor Soler's very skin.

"We need to document every inch before the ambient temperature alters the tissues," she interjected, her voice devoid of emotional inflection. It was the profiler's voice, just another tool in the room. "The chain of custody begins and ends with what we see now."

Soler looked at her then. There was a resistance in his eyes, a defensive opacity.

"I know how to do my job, Miss Vives. I've signed more death certificates in this valley than you've read in your reports."

Without waiting for a reply, the doctor unzipped the bag. The sound, similar to the ripping of thick fabric, seemed to absorb all the oxygen from the basement.

Isabel appeared before them not as a corpse, but as a toppled statue. The freezing had halted decomposition, maintaining her features in a frightening suspension. Her skin had the color of blue-veined marble, and frost formed tiny crystals on her eyelashes, granting her a cruel, artificial beauty. She didn't look dead; she looked paused.

Elena felt a familiar pressure in her chest, the claw of emotional claustrophobia scratching from within. *Order*, she told herself, seeking the weight of her father's watch on her left wrist. *Find order in the chaos*.

She approached the table, invading the doctor's personal space. Lucas let out a choked sigh from the doorway, but didn't move.

"There are no evident livor mortis marks," Soler murmured, shining a flashlight over the girl's torso. "The cold stopped circulation before the blood could settle. Probable cause: severe hypothermia after an accidental fall."

"Too fast, Doctor," Elena corrected. Her green eyes scanned the body not as one looks at a person, but as one reads a topographical map. "Look at her hands."

Isabel's hands were crossed over her chest. Not in the defensive position of someone trying to protect themselves from a fall, nor cramped, seeking warmth. They were placed. Set there with intentionality.

Elena took out her digital camera and began to shoot. The flash exploded, bouncing off the cerulean skin, revealing details that the naked eye might miss.

"Let's remove her clothes. I need to see the skin," Elena ordered.

Soler hesitated for a second, his gloved hands hovering over the victim's synthetic coat.

"She's stiff as a board. We'll have to cut the fabric."

With trauma shears, the doctor began to cut through the layers of clothing. The sound of the fabric tearing was violent in the sepulchral silence. When Isabel's torso was exposed, a dense, leaden silence fell over the three of them.

There were no disordered bruises. No open fractures with splintered bone breaking through the dermis.

What there was, was a map.

Elena leaned in, holding her breath. On Isabel's abdomen, and climbing towards her ribs, there were lines. These were not scratches caused by rocks or branches in a desperate fall. They were incisions. Clean, surgical cuts, traced with a precision that chilled the blood more than the room's temperature.

"What is this?" Lucas asked, his voice broken from the gloom. He had approached without realizing it, drawn by the magnetic horror of the scene.

Elena ran a gloved finger close to one of the lines, without touching it.

"The edges are perfect," she whispered, feeling a knot of intellectual revulsion form in her stomach. "There's no vital reaction. No inflammation, no tissue retraction."

She looked at Soler. The doctor was pale, his eyes fixed on the marks with an indecipherable expression.

"Doctor, these incisions were made *post-mortem*," Elena affirmed, and the sentence hung in the stale air. "Someone opened her, or tried to, after her heart stopped beating. And then... closed her again with almost invisible intradermal sutures."

Soler shook his head, a spasmodic movement.

"Impossible. That... that could be the effect of ice expanding the skin, or friction with a sharp surface of the glacier. Nature can be capricious, Elena. You see intentions where there are only geological accidents."

"Nature doesn't use a scalpel, Arnald," she retorted, her tone hardening. She pointed to a Y-shaped incision over the sternum. "This is embalming technique. Or preservation."

"That's absurd!" the doctor exploded, stepping back as if the physical accusation of the cuts personally offended him. "Who would do such a thing in the middle of a storm, on top of a mountain? You're seeing ghosts, girl. You're projecting your big-city traumas onto our misfortune."

Lucas looked alternately at Elena and the body, lost in a labyrinth of pain and confusion.

"Elena..." he began to say, "Arnald has been taking care of us for thirty years. If he says the ice..."

"Ice preserves, Lucas. Ice tells the truth men want to hide," she interrupted him, turning to face the doctor. "These marks are art. Someone took the time to work on her while she was freezing. It wasn't a fall. It was a staging."

A subtle, almost imperceptible sound stopped the argument.

*Drip.*

*Plink.*

A drop of condensed water slid down Isabel's arm and fell onto the metal table. Then another. The heat generated by the living bodies and the precarious lighting was beginning to win the battle against the cold that preserved the evidence.

Isabel's skin, until now immaculate porcelain, began to shine with a thin film of moisture. Time, that relentless enemy Elena tried to control with her father's watch, had started again.

"She's defrosting," Elena said, feeling the urgency rise in her throat like bile. "If these incisions soften, we'll lose the geometry of the edges. We need molds. We need macroscopic photography. Now."

Soler crossed his arms, stepping between Elena and the instruments.

"I will not allow you to desecrate the body of a girl I watched grow up based on conspiracy theories. We'll wait for the investigating judge. We'll wait for the storm to pass."

"By the time the storm passes," Elena said, pointing to the tiny puddle beginning to form under the victim's elbow, "the evidence will have dissolved in dirty water. And the killer knows it."

Lucas looked at Isabel's body, the girl he perhaps loved, or perhaps protected, now becoming an enigma dissolving before his eyes. The water continued to fall, slow, inexorable, marking the rhythm of a countdown that no one knew how to stop.

## Chapter 4: Fronteras Cerradas

**C-28 Road, Access to Bohí Valley**
**December 20, 2018**
**5:45 PM**

The journey from the village to the mountain pass wasn't a trip; it was a battle against the whiteness. Lucas Riera felt the Jeep's tires chewing on the virgin snow, searching for traction on asphalt that had ceased to exist hours ago. The windshield wipers fought a losing battle, drawing frantic arcs that barely granted seconds of visibility before the storm blinded the glass once more.

His right knee throbbed with a rhythm of its own, a biological alarm more precise than any barometer. Atmospheric pressure plummeted, and with it, his ailing joint reminded him of his own fragility. Lucas gritted his teeth, clinging to the steering wheel with his large, calloused hands, forcing himself to ignore the throbbing pain climbing up his thigh. There was no time for weakness.

In the distance, amber strobe lights stained the fog a sickly orange. It was the only color in a world that had turned monochrome.

Lucas stopped the vehicle beside the Civil Guard patrol car blocking the way. The wind howled, a sharp lament that seeped even through the car's insulation. As he opened the door, the cold slapped him with the violence of a solid object. The icy air snaked into his lungs, burning on entry, with that pure, metallic taste of high altitudes.

Sergeant Miralles stood beside the snowplow, a yellow hulk of heavy machinery lying inert, twisted at an unnatural angle against the guardrail, like a prehistoric animal felled in the middle of the tundra.

Lucas limped forward, sinking his boots into twelve inches of powder snow. The gale tried to push him back, but his broad build served as an anchor.

“Chief!” Miralles shouted to be heard over the blizzard’s roar. His breath instantly crystallized in his beard. “This is goddamn madness!”

Lucas didn’t respond immediately. His dark eyes, accustomed to reading scenes of misfortune, scanned the surroundings. Visibility was no more than five meters. Beyond that, the road disappeared into a white abyss. The snowplow, his only hope for keeping the artery connecting Aigüestortes to the civilized world open, was dead.

Beside the driver’s cabin, a man in grease-stained reflective overalls gesticulated desperately. It was Pau, the municipal worker. His face was distorted by cold and fear.

“It stopped dead!” Pau bellowed, waving an unlit cigarette between his gloved fingers. “I swear the engine was perfect this morning! I did the check myself!”

Lucas approached the side of the machine. The smell of diesel and hydraulic fluid was pungent, an olfactory stain on the sterile purity of the mountain. He crouched down, ignoring his knee’s protest, and directed his flashlight beam into the mechanical entrails of the vehicle.

What he saw sent a colder void through his stomach than the outside temperature.

On the immaculate snow, beneath the chassis, a dark, viscous puddle spread. It looked like black blood gushing from an open wound. Lucas followed the fluid’s trail to its source: the main hydraulic system hoses.

They weren’t burst by pressure. There was no chaotic fraying indicative of wear-and-tear.

They were clean cuts. Precise. Surgical.

Lucas removed his right glove and ran his fingertips along the edge of one of the severed tubes. The rubber had been sliced with a sharp tool, an oblique cut designed to drain the system in minutes.

He straightened up slowly, feeling the weight of the police badge on his chest, a weight that suddenly felt useless.

“This wasn’t a breakdown, Pau,” Lucas said, his grave voice cutting through the icy air with an authority he didn’t feel inside.

Miralles approached, wide-eyed.
“What do you mean, Lucas?”

“They cut the brakes and the power steering. Someone got underneath with a knife or a box cutter.”

The silence that followed his words was heavier than the storm. The three men looked at each other, sharing a tacit and terrifying understanding. The machine hadn’t broken by chance. It had been murdered.

Lucas turned toward the road, south, toward where the valley exit should be. The snow was falling more heavily now, large, wet flakes piling up at dizzying speed, erasing any trace of asphalt, turning the mountain pass into an impassable wall.

“How long will it take to send another machine from Vielha?” Miralles asked, though his tone suggested he already knew the answer.

“With this storm…” Pau shook his head, looking at the leaden sky. “Nobody’s coming up today. Not tomorrow. We’re talking meters of accumulation in the next few hours. If we don’t clear this now, the access road will be blocked.”

“It already is,” Lucas stated.

The police chief walked to the edge of the precipice, where the guardrail barely peeked out from under the white blanket. He looked down into the darkness of the valley that stretched out at his feet. The village lights were mere dying fireflies in the distance, swallowed by the blizzard.

The feeling of claustrophobia closed in on him like a fist. It wasn’t just the geographical isolation. It was the certainty of intent. Someone had sabotaged the snowplow on purpose. Someone wanted no one to enter.

But, more importantly, someone wanted no one to leave.

He remembered Isabel’s body in the medical center’s basement, the perfect incisions, the ice preserving the horror. And now this. The puzzle pieces began to fit together with a sinister click. They weren’t dealing with an accident or an isolated crime of passion.

Lucas took out his radio, but only got static, a white crackle that mimicked the visual chaos surrounding them. The valley’s topography, combined with the electrical storm accompanying the snow, had erected a wall of silence.

“Sergeant,” Lucas ordered, putting away the radio and returning to his car. His limp was more pronounced now, but his determination had hardened his features. “Set up the concrete barriers. Block the entrance completely. Don’t let anyone come up here only to get trapped.”

“What about us?” Miralles asked, looking at the disappearing road.

Lucas got into the Jeep and closed the door, isolating himself again from the wind, but not from the cold that had settled into his bones. He looked in the rearview mirror, watching the snow begin to cover the yellow corpse of the snowplow, burying the evidence of the sabotage.

“We’re going back down,” Lucas said to himself, starting the engine. “To lock ourselves in with them.”

The trap was already laid. Aigüestortes Valley was no longer a tourist refuge; it was a cage of glass and ice, and the key had just been thrown into the abyss.

## Chapter 5: El Huésped Invisible

**Aigüestortes, Boí Valley**
**December 20, 2018**

The journey from Dr. Soler's clinic to the Sanatorio Hotel was no more than three hundred meters, but the storm had erased any notion of distance or geography. The "White Beast" wasn't just a simple snowfall; it was a solid wall, a living organism that pushed Elena Vives to the ground, trying to bury her before she could take another step.

Every inhalation was a punishment. The icy air entered her lungs like ground glass, burning her throat and freezing her eyelashes into heavy needles of ice. She shielded her face with her forearm, fighting against a wind that howled with the fury of a thousand laments. Her mountaineering boots, designed for extreme terrain, slipped on the layer of ice that was already beginning to form beneath the fresh snow.

When the hotel's silhouette emerged from the blackness, it looked more like a giant tombstone than a refuge. The building, a mass of stone and slate from the turn of the century, stood defiantly against the elements, its dark windows watching the valley like empty sockets.

Elena stumbled up the entrance steps, her numb hands fumbling for the brass doorknob. She pushed. The heavy oak door yielded, and she slipped inside, closing it behind her with a dull thud that abruptly severed the roar of the wind.

The silence that followed was absolute, almost liturgical.

She stood there for a moment, leaning against the wood, allowing her breathing to stabilize. The warmth of the lobby struck her not like a caress, but like a change in atmospheric pressure that made her ears ring. She took off her ski goggles; condensation blurred her vision for a second before dissipating.

The Sanatorio Hotel's lobby was a cavern of decadent luxury. High ceilings with plaster moldings depicting hunting scenes, Persian rugs worn by decades of footsteps, and an immense chandelier hanging like a sword of Damocles over the center of the room. No one was there. Only the ticking of a grandfather clock that echoed against the dark wood-paneled walls.

Elena moved forward. Her steps on the marble produced a damp, intrusive echo. She brought her hand to her left wrist and caressed the cold face of her father's watch, an automatic gesture, an anchor when the mental tide rose too high. Quarter past eight.

"Hello?" her voice came out hoarse, small in the immensity of the space.

Behind the mahogany counter, a figure straightened up. She hadn't seen him; he blended with the gloom in the background. He was a gaunt man, of indeterminate age, dressed in a suit that seemed to have been elegant thirty years ago. His skin had the tone of old paper.

"The kitchen is closed, madam," the man said without preamble. His tone was neutral, devoid of hospitality or rejection. He simply stated a fact.

"I'm not looking for food. I need a room."

The concierge—or manager, or the spirit guarding that mausoleum—tilted his head. His watery eyes scanned Elena's technical clothing, her soaked backpack, and the ice axe protruding from one side.

"We have ninety rooms. Eighty-nine are empty. You can choose the view you prefer, though tonight they all look out onto the same white hell."

"Any will do. I just want to get these off," she said, gesturing to her wet clothes.

The man slid a large registration book across the polished surface of the counter. He opened the pages with an exasperating slowness. The paper crinkled, dry and brittle.

"Name and ID, please. I am Mr. Baró."

Elena fumbled in her inner pocket with clumsy fingers until she found her wallet. While Baró transcribed her details in spiky, angular handwriting, her gaze wandered over the open page. Obsession, that old companion that had turned her into one of the best profilers and then into an emotional wreck, took over.

She looked for patterns. She looked for anomalies.

The page was desolate. December was the dead season, the limbo between autumn and the arrival of January's skiers. There was only one recent entry, dated the previous day.

*Room 302.*

The ink was black, fresh, contrasting with the faded blue of November's annotations. But it wasn't the room number that stopped Elena's heart for a beat, but the name.

*Mr. Pygmalion.*

Elena blinked. Read again. The letters were there, firm, arrogant.

"Do you have another guest?" she asked, trying to make her voice sound casual, though the knot in her stomach tightened.

Baró looked up from Elena's ID. He followed the direction of her finger.

"Ah. The gentleman in 302. A peculiar man."

"Peculiar?"

"He arrived yesterday, before the sky fell. Paid cash, a week in advance. Asked for absolute privacy." Baró shrugged, a mechanical movement of his thin bones. "He hasn't even come down to eat. I suppose he's looking for inspiration or a hiding place. In this valley, they're often the same thing."

*Pygmalion.* The mythical sculptor who fell in love with his own creation. A name no one uses in a hotel register unless they want to send a message, or are playing God.

Elena felt a pang at the base of her skull. The scar on her left hand itched, a phantom reminder. In the clinic's basement, Isabel's body presented precise, surgical incisions, as if someone had tried to remodel her. And now, less than a kilometer away, a guest was checking in under the name of a creator of living statues.

Coincidences don't exist. Only pieces we don't know how to fit together.

"Have you seen him today?" Elena insisted.

"No one has seen him. Room service leaves the tray at the door and picks it up untouched. Perhaps he sleeps. Perhaps he writes. It's not my business as long as he doesn't burn the curtains."

Baró turned the book towards her and offered her a heavy pen. Elena signed. Her own handwriting seemed shaky next to the invisible guest's signature.

"I'll give you 204," the manager said, unhooking a heavy key from a wooden board full of empty hooks. "Second floor. Right below him, if that concerns you. The ceilings are high, you won't hear a thing."

Elena took the key. The metal was icy cold.

"Thank you."

She headed towards the grand central staircase. The elevator, a wrought-iron cage, seemed out of order or simply too eerie to try. As she ascended, the sound of her boots on the varnished wood echoed in the lobby's dome.

One. Two. Three steps.

The hotel creaked. The wood contracted from the temperature change, the pipes sang old songs in the walls. These were the normal sounds of an old building settling for the night.

But then, something broke the usual symphony.

She stopped on the first landing, clutching the banister.

Upstairs. On the third floor.

It wasn't a creak. It was a drag. Slow, deliberate. Something heavy being moved across a rough surface. _Ssshhhk. Ssshhhk._

Elena looked up, into the darkness of the upper floor's railing. The lobby light didn't reach there; shadows gathered like black water.

"Mr. Baró?" she called, leaning over the handrail to look down.

The manager was no longer behind the counter. The lobby was empty.

The sound repeated. _Ssshhhk._ Right above her head. A piece of furniture being shifted, or perhaps something inert being dragged towards the edge.

Her breathing quickened. The sense of security promised by the hotel's thick walls evaporated. That place wasn't a refuge; it was a sounding board. And she had just locked herself inside with something that didn't want to be seen, but was definitely awake.

She clutched the key in her fist until the edges dug into her flesh, using the pain to focus her mind. She climbed the next step. She had to reach her room, lock the bolt, and wait for dawn. Or for Lucas to arrive.

But Lucas was outside, in the storm, and inside, in the gloom of the third floor, "Mr. Pygmalion" had just taken a step.

## Chapter 6: Cicatrices en la Memoria

**Hotel Sanatorio, Aigüestortes**
**December 21, 2018**

Dawn didn't arrive as a promise of light, but as a slight variation in the grayscale that choked the valley. Elena Vives opened her eyes, though technically she had never fully closed them. The vigil had been a demanding companion, sitting at the foot of the bed in room 204, watching the sway of the curtains stirred by invisible drafts.

She sat up. The sheets, damp with a cold sweat that contradicted the glacial temperature of the room, clung to her skin. She looked at her left wrist. Her father's watch read six twelve. The second hand advanced with that mechanical indifference she so envied; time felt no fear, it simply passed.

She picked up the digital camera from the nightstand. Her numb fingers clumsily sought the buttons until the small LCD screen illuminated the gloom with a bluish glow.

There it was. The image she had captured before the storm swallowed everything.

It wasn't just a corpse. Elena zoomed in on the photo until the pixels turned into abstract blocks of cyan and white. The victim's posture showed no struggle, nor that rictus of agony that contorts muscles when death arrives violently. The girl seemed to be sleeping within the block of ice, suspended in an eternal parenthesis.

“There’s no chaos,” she whispered, and her own voice sounded strange, hoarse from disuse during the night.

This wasn't a crime of passion. Anger is messy; it leaves stains, breakages, entropic chaos. This was architecture. It was design. Someone had taken the trouble to compose the scene with the precision of a museum conservator.

Elena set her feet on the floor. The wood creaked, a dry groan that resonated in her temples. She needed to understand the setting to comprehend the work. Hotel Sanatorio wasn't a mere building; its walls, covered in peeling wallpaper like dead skin, breathed a history the present tried to ignore.

She dressed quickly, layering wool and technical fabric, and stepped out into the hallway.

The silence on the second floor was dense, almost liquid. She walked towards the staircase, avoiding the central planks she knew would betray her position. Below, the lobby lay in gloom, with the furniture covered by sheets that looked like crouching ghosts awaiting their turn. But her destination wasn't the exit, blocked by tons of snow, but the east wing, where the previous day she had glimpsed a double-leaf door with frosted glass.

*Library*. Or what remained of it.

She pushed the door open. The hinges protested with a sharp screech that raised the hairs on the back of her neck. The scent hit her first: a mix of decaying cellulose, old leather, and that sickly sweet, stale aroma time acquires when stagnant in enclosed spaces.

The room was a mausoleum of forgotten knowledge. Dark oak shelves rose to the ceiling, filled with volumes no one had consulted in decades. The outdoor light, filtered by the storm, barely entered as a dirty haze, insufficient for reading. Elena took a small flashlight from her pocket and traced a beam of light over the spines.

*Treatises on anatomy. Natural philosophy. Climate records.*

Her hands moved with the urgency of someone seeking air underwater. She wasn't looking for fiction. She was looking for logic. If the killer, that supposed 'Pygmalion' from the registry book, was using this place, there had to be a reason. Territorial predators don't choose their lairs randomly; they seek ecosystems that favor their nature.

She passed by the accounting section. Invoices from 1980, electricity bills... too recent. She kept going further back. A cardboard box, damp at the base, contained bundles of documents tied with twine.

1965. 1958.

Elena set the flashlight on a reading table and untied the knot of one of the oldest bundles. Dust rose in a microscopic cloud, making her cough into the sleeve of her sweater. They were medical journals. Logbooks of the doctors who managed the place when the 'Sanatorium' lived up to its name, treating tuberculosis and respiratory ailments of the Catalan bourgeoisie.

She opened a black-covered notebook, its corners eaten away by dampness. The handwriting was sharp, strict, penned with a fountain pen that tolerated no smudges.

*January 14, 1952. Outside temperature: -15ºC.*

Elena turned the pages, her green eyes scanning for keywords, filtering out the administrative chaff: *supplies, morphine, bandages...*

She stopped. Her index finger, marked by the thin crescent-shaped scar, rested on a paragraph that seemed to vibrate with a dark energy.

*“Subject 04. Controlled induction of hypothermia shows promising results in slowing tissue deterioration. Cold should not be seen as an enemy, but as natural formaldehyde. If we stop the flow, we stop time.”*

Her heart gave a painful lurch, beating against her ribs like a trapped bird. She continued reading. These weren't notes on how to cure illness. They were observations on preservation. On how to maintain life's beauty right at the threshold of extinction.

*“The art of rudimentary cryogenics requires patience. The crystallization of water in cells is the artist's failure. The goal is vitrification. A state of perfect suspension.”*

Elena recoiled from the book as if the letters burned. Her breathing quickened, fogging the air in front of her mouth. She looked around, and the library's shadows seemed to lengthen, taking on grotesque forms.

This wasn't new. The crime of the frozen young woman wasn't a macabre innovation by a modern psychopath. It was a citation. A homage.

The hotel had housed an experimental program in the 1950s. A project obsessed not with curing death, but with perfecting the corpse.

“You’re replicating the method,” she whispered to the empty room, feeling the puzzle pieces click into place with a metallic sound in her mind.

The killer didn't just know the hotel. He had studied its archives. Perhaps he saw himself as the heir to that perverse science, the gifted student who had finally achieved what those doctors only dreamed of: eternity through ice.

A dull thud, coming from the floor above, broke her concentration. It wasn't the wind. It was the unmistakable sound of a heavy object being dragged across the wooden floor. Directly above her head.

Elena abruptly switched off the flashlight, plunging herself into darkness, with the absolute certainty that history was about to repeat itself. And she was at the center of the page.

## Chapter 7: Sangre en la Nieve

**Aigüestortes, perimeter of Sant Maurici Lake**
**December 22, 2018, 08:15 AM**

The mountain held no secrets; it simply buried them under layers of white silence.

Lucas Riera limped forward, though he tried to hide it by digging his left heel harder into the crusty snow. His right knee, that old weather station made of cartilage and screws, throbbed with a dull rhythm, announcing what the leaden gray sky was already shouting: the storm was close. The "White Beast" was not a metaphor for the locals; it was a sentence.

Ahead of him, the leather leash tightened like a violin string about to snap. Roco, a German Shepherd with dark fur and a grizzled muzzle, pulled with a determination that bordered on madness. The animal wasn't sniffing the ground; he was drinking the air, capturing invisible molecules that the icy breeze dragged down from the high altitudes.

"Easy, boy," Lucas murmured, his breath crystallizing into an ephemeral cloud in front of his face.

Elena Vives walked two paces behind. The crunch of her technical boots on the ice broke the stillness of the black pine forest. Her hands were stuffed into the pockets of her parka, but Lucas knew that inside, her fingers would be caressing the cold face of her father's watch. A nervous tic. An anchor.

"The tracks disappear here," she said. Her voice sounded small, absorbed by the immensity of the trees.

Lucas stopped and observed the ground. She was right. The wind had begun to sweep the surface, erasing the recent history of the forest. What yesterday were deep boot marks, today were vague depressions, shadows in the snow that could be footsteps or mere whims of the melt.

"The night wind," the officer conceded, adjusting his jacket collar. "But Roco doesn't follow visual tracks. He follows the scent of fear. Or something worse."

The dog let out a low, guttural whimper. It wasn't the usual warning bark, but a wet, primitive sound. He stopped dead in his tracks in front of a rocky outcrop, a vertical granite wall where pine roots twisted like exposed veins seeking the earth.

Elena approached, her green eyes scanning the surroundings with that clinical precision that both fascinated and terrified Lucas. She didn't look at the landscape; she dissected it.

"There," Elena pointed.

A narrow crack, barely a scar on the rock, made its way between two lichen-covered, frost-dusted blocks of stone. It was a natural, shallow cave, but enough to conceal a crouching man. Or to hide his sins.

Roco refused to move forward. The animal sat back on his haunches, the hair on his back bristling, and bared his teeth toward the darkness of the fissure.

Lucas released the safety of his service weapon; the metallic click sounded obscenely loud in the morning stillness.

"Stay behind," he ordered, though he knew she wouldn't.

The officer advanced, switching on his tactical flashlight. The beam of light cut through the gloom of the cave, revealing a space no bigger than a pantry. The air inside was stagnant, protected from the blizzard, and carried an aroma that made Lucas's stomach violently clench.

It didn't smell of dead animal, nor of decaying vegetation.

It smelled of cleanliness.

It was a chemical, pungent stench, a mix of isopropyl alcohol and something sweetish, mentholated, that didn't belong in the wild. It was the smell of hospital corridors at three in the morning.

"My God," Elena whispered behind him.

The flashlight beam illuminated a flat stone surface, a natural slab that had been meticulously cleaned of dust and debris. On it, arranged with a sickly symmetry, several objects rested.

They weren't trash. They were tools.

A scalpel with a stainless steel handle gleamed with a bluish sheen under the beam of light. Beside it, hemostatic forceps and an open roll of gauze, immaculate. And a small, uncapped amber glass vial, from which that synthetic mint vapor emanated.

Elena stepped forward, ignoring protocol, drawn to the scene like a moth to a flame.

"It's not a hiding place," she said, her voice trembling but firm, analyzing the data as her mind processed the horror. "It's a workstation. A field workshop."

Lucas felt bile rise in his throat. The idea that someone had been there, in the middle of nowhere, preparing surgical instruments with the patience of a craftsman, was more terrifying than any corpse. It implied planning. It implied that the pain inflicted was not a means, but an end.

"This is recent," Lucas noted, drawing his hand near without touching anything. "The steel had no frost. Someone was here this morning. Perhaps an hour ago."

The dog, who had remained at the entrance growling toward the darkness of the forest, suddenly changed his demeanor.

His ears perked up, swiveling like satellite dishes to the east, towards the denser thicket where the trees closed in, forming a black wall.

"Roco, stay!" Lucas warned, feeling the tension in the leash slacken an instant before the tug.

The animal barked, a sonic burst that echoed off the stone walls, and then, something broke. Not the leash, but the discipline. An instinct older than police training took control. The dog detected something humans couldn't see or hear.

With a sudden lunge, Roco thrashed; the choke collar dug into his neck, but he didn't care. The wet leather slipped from Lucas's gloved hand, numbed by the cold.

"Roco, no! STOP!" Lucas yelled.

The German Shepherd shot off like a black arrow across the white snow, plunging into the forest, chasing a shadow, a scent, a ghost.

"Lucas!" Elena cried out, watching the officer try to run after him, but his right leg gave out, causing him to stumble over a hidden root.

Lucas fell to his knees, letting out a grunt of pain and frustration, pounding the snow with his fist.

Roco's barks rapidly faded into the distance, mixing with the whistling wind that was beginning to pick up in the treetops. And then, as suddenly as they had begun, they ceased.

An absolute silence, heavy as a marble slab, fell over the forest.

Lucas rose with difficulty, his face pale, his gaze fixed in the direction where his partner had disappeared. The loneliness, suddenly, became physical, tangible. They were no longer the hunters.

In that clearing, surrounded by surgical tools and virgin snow, they had just become the prey.

## Chapter 8: La Lista de Pacientes

**Aigüestortes**
**December 21, 2018**

The descent from the cave was not a retreat, but a slow fall into hell.

The Jeep Wrangler devoured the curves of the forest road with a mechanical violence that made the axles groan, but Lucas Riera barely registered the vehicle's jolts. His world had shrunk to two points of unbearable pain: the agonizing throb in his right knee, which burned as if someone had driven a red-hot nail beneath his kneecap, and the emptiness in the passenger seat.

Roco's absence weighed more heavily than gravity.

Every time he glanced in the rearview mirror, hoping to see his German Shepherd's wet snout or hear that rhythmic panting that had been the soundtrack of his life for the past five years, he only found the empty, mud-stained upholstery. Guilt was an acid eating away at his stomach. He had left him behind. He had prioritized Elena's safety, logic, protocol, and in return, the mountain had swallowed his only family.

The wipers struggled against a mixture of snow and hail that was beginning to congeal on the windshield, a white curtain threatening to bury the entire valley. The White Beast was no longer just a weather warning; it was scratching at the village gates.

Lucas braked abruptly in front of the Town Hall, ignoring the restricted parking area. He got out of the car without closing the door. His right boot hit the icy asphalt and his leg gave way momentarily, forcing him to lean on the warm hood to keep from hitting the ground. He growled, an animal sound lost in the howling wind, and forced his body upright. Limping, dragging a trail of dirty water and dead leaves, he climbed the stone steps two at a time, propelled by a fury that blurred his peripheral vision.

He ignored the receptionist's greeting. He ignored the Christmas carols playing over the hallway speakers. He burst into the mayor's office without knocking, throwing open the double doors with such force that the wood slammed against the wall stop, creating a deafening crash that shook the portraits of former councillors.

Mayor Marta Vallés looked up from her papers. She sat behind her immaculate mahogany desk, dressed in a pearl-grey wool suit that was utterly uncreased. The room smelled of freshly brewed coffee and furniture polish, an obscene contrast to the scent of damp, dried blood, and fear that clung to Lucas's skin.

"Chief Riera," she said, with that rehearsed calm she used in municipal council meetings when the opposition grew difficult. "You look dreadful. Didn't anyone teach you to knock?"

Lucas advanced to the desk, leaving muddy footprints on the Persian rug. He leaned with both hands on the wooden surface, invading the woman's personal space.

"Enough with the formalities, Marta." His voice was hoarse, as if he had gravel in his throat. "I need full access to the Sanatorio's historical archives. Right now."

The Mayor took off her reading glasses and delicately placed them on a budget report. Her dark, calculating eyes swept over the officer's figure, pausing on the tremor in his hands and the waxy pallor of his face.

"The Sanatorio archives are sealed by data protection and heritage laws, Lucas. You know that better than I do. You can't just barge in there like a bull in a china shop because you have a hunch."

"It's not a hunch," he spat. He leaned in closer, lowering his voice to a dangerous whisper. "We found something up there. In the perimeter forest. There's a cave, Marta. A workshop. Someone's been operating there."

Marta didn't bat an eyelid, but Lucas, who had spent half his life reading gestures during interrogations, saw the instant tension at the corners of her lips. A micro-expression of recognition, not surprise.

"Operating?" she repeated, buying time. "You're delirious. The storm is getting to you, or perhaps it's that leg you refuse to treat. You should see a doctor."

"I found an amputated hand, frozen in a block of ice," Lucas blurted out, throwing the words like stones. "And surgical tools. And I know it's related to the renovations made to the north wing five years ago."

The Mayor stood up, smoothing her jacket. She walked towards the large window overlooking the square, turning her back to Lucas.

"Those renovations were necessary for the structure. The building was falling apart."

"Don't lie to me." Lucas slammed his fist on the table. "I checked the public records before I came up. The construction company was a front. And the funding... the funding didn't come from the Provincial Council, Marta. It came from the Town Hall's discretionary fund. Your signature is on the authorization."

Marta turned slowly. The mask of the amiable politician had vanished. Now there was steel in her gaze.

"It was an investment, Lucas. Wellness tourism. A high-level recovery center. The town is dying, we need income. If to secure private capital I had to look the other way concerning certain... antecedents of the building, then I did."

"Look the other way?" Lucas felt bile rise in his throat. "You funded the reconstruction of a slaughterhouse knowing what happened there in the seventies. You knew about the cryogenics experiments. You knew about the disappearances of the 'patients.' And now, someone is using those facilities, or what's left of them, to continue the work."

"Enough!" Marta's shout echoed in the office, sharp as a whip. "You're talking about absurd conspiracies. You're agitated, dirty, and frankly, you seem unhinged. I've been told you lost your dog. It's a shame, but grief doesn't give you the right to accuse me of criminal complicity."

The mention of Roco was a low blow that almost made him lose control. Lucas clenched his jaw until his teeth ached, fighting the urge to grab her by the lapels.

"Give me the archive key," he said, with a coldness that even startled himself. "Or I'll go to the press and tell them what happened to the nursery school money."

Marta sat back down, regaining control of the situation with a glacial smile. She opened a side drawer and pulled out a folder.

"You're not going anywhere, Riera. And you're not getting that key. In fact, if you don't leave my office in ten seconds and go home to sleep it off, I'll consider this gross insubordination. I've had your dismissal letter drafted since you started limping six months ago. All it needs is the date."

The threat hung in the air, tangible and poisonous. Lucas knew it wasn't a bluff. Marta had the power to take away his badge, his weapon, and the only thing that gave meaning to his empty days.

"Are you threatening my career while there's a psychopath loose on the mountain?" he asked, incredulous.

"I'm giving you a direct order. Go home. Wash up. And pray that when the storm passes, I'm in a good mood."

Lucas stared at her for a long second. He saw the arrogance in her eyes, the absolute certainty that she held all the cards. She believed she had won.

"Alright," he said, relaxing his shoulders in a gesture of apparent defeat. He took a step back, pretending his leg gave out again, and leaned heavily on the edge of the desk, near the inbox. "You win, Mayor. I'm leaving."

As she lowered her gaze to her documents, satisfied with his submission, Lucas's left hand moved with the swiftness of a pickpocket. His fingers closed around the brass keyring resting beside the paperweight, partially hidden beneath an open envelope. He recognized the worn label: *Basement / Archives*.

He slid the cold metal into his jacket sleeve in one fluid motion, pushing off the desk as he did so.

"But be clear about one thing, Marta," Lucas said from the doorway, his hand already on the knob. "If one more body turns up, their blood won't stain my boots. It'll stain your immaculate rug."

He stepped out into the hallway before she could respond.

The sound of the door closing was definitive. Lucas walked down the corridor, feeling the weight of the stolen key pressing against his wrist. The pain in his knee was still there, sharp and constant, and the image of Roco lost in the snow still tormented him, but now, mingled with the anguish, a new fuel burned in his chest.

He was no longer the Chief of Police following orders. Now he was a man with nothing to lose, and he had just declared war.

## Chapter 9: Anatomía de una Obsesión

CAPÍTULO: 9 - Anatomy of an Obsession

Hostal del Valle. Room 204.
December 21, 2018.

The return journey from the forest had been a blur of grey pines and thick silence, interrupted only by the groan of Lucas's Jeep engine battling the mud. He had dropped me off at the hostel entrance with a silent promise in his eyes and a strict order to lock myself in until he returned from the Town Hall. Now, alone within four floral-wallpapered walls, the building's security felt fragile, an eggshell beneath the boot of a storm that was beginning to howl beyond the windowpane.

I transformed the cheap wooden desk into a dissecting table. There were no corpses, only images captured with my digital camera and fragments of memory that burned hotter than dry ice. The photographs of the “workshop” in the cave unfolded across the varnished surface, forming a mosaic of madness.

My fingers, still numb from the residual cold of the cavern, traced the outline of the surgical instruments portrayed. Scalpels aligned with a precision that offended the natural chaos of the world. Unlabeled jars. And that tank.

“Order,” I whispered, and my own voice sounded alien in the empty room.

It wasn't a wild animal's lair. It was a temple.

I brought the nightstand lamp closer, forcing the tungsten bulb to reveal the secrets of the textures. The camera screen showed a close-up of the residue found on the cave's workbench. A viscous, translucent substance that didn't behave like water when freezing.

As a glaciologist, I understood the structure of cold. Water, upon solidifying, is brutal; its crystals are microscopic daggers that break cell membranes, tearing flesh from within. That's why bodies frozen in the mountains turn black, brittle. But what I had seen in the forest, and what I intuited about previous victims, was different. It was an immaculate preservation.

Vitrification.

The crescent-shaped scar on my left palm throbbed, a phantom reminder of old wounds. I rubbed my wrist, feeling the weight of my father's watch, its hands advancing oblivious to the horror I was trying to decode.

Someone didn't want to kill. Someone wanted to stop time.

Dry knocks on the door shattered my concentration. Three rhythmic, precise taps.

My hand flew instinctively to my jacket pocket, searching for a weapon I didn't have, before remembering where I was. I forced myself to breathe, filling my lungs with air that smelled of dust and furniture wax.

“Elena?” The voice on the other side was deep, familiar, laden with a paternal concern that, at another time, would have comforted me.

Dr. Arnald Soler.

I hastily covered the photographs with an unfolded tourist map and went to open the door.

Soler was in the hallway, his impeccable grey wool coat defying the wind lashing Aigüestortes. He held a leather medical bag in one hand and a brown paper bag in the other. His face, framed by that carefully trimmed grey beard, showed a practiced weariness.

“Lucas called me,” he said, stepping in almost before I moved aside. “He said he found you wandering near the perimeter, that you were agitated.”

Lucas’s lie to protect me. Clever.
