“I just needed some air,” I replied, closing the door. The slide of the bolt was definitive. “The hotel suffocates me.”

Soler set his things on the bed. The room seemed to shrink with his presence. From the bag emanated the aroma of fresh coffee and croissants, a scent of normalcy that felt obscene given the circumstances.

“You look unwell, my dear.” He removed his leather gloves with slow, almost hypnotic movements. “Dilated pupils, paleness... Post-traumatic stress is a patient beast, Elena. It waits for you to drop your guard.”

“I’m fine, Arnald.” I crossed my arms, creating a physical barrier. “What are you really doing here?”

“Bringing you breakfast and making sure you don’t fall apart.” He opened his briefcase. The click of the metal clasps echoed like the cocking of a weapon. “And perhaps something for your nerves. A mild sedative. You need to sleep before the storm traps us all in.”

He approached the desk. My heart pounded against my ribs. The map covered the worst, but the edges of some photos peeked out.

“I don’t need to sleep. I need to understand.”

Soler paused, his blue gaze sweeping over the disarray of my improvised workstation. There was no curiosity in his eyes, but a clinical assessment.

“Understand what?” he asked, in a soft tone, the same one he used when I was a brilliant but volatile student.

“The physics of this.” I moved closer to the table and deliberately removed the map. I wanted to see his reaction. “Lucas and I found more than just trees in the forest.”

Soler didn’t flinch. He leaned over the images of the surgical instruments. His expression didn’t change, not a muscle tensed, which, paradoxically, was the most alarming reaction of all. A normal person would have asked what they were. An innocent doctor would have been surprised. He simply observed.

“It looks... rudimentary,” he commented, pointing at the photo of the jars.

“That’s what I thought at first.” I leaned against the window frame, letting the storm’s grey light bathe my profile. “But look at the residue on the table. It’s not normal ice.”

“It’s a cold place, Elena. Things freeze.”

“Not like this.” I turned to face him fully. “Water expands when it freezes. It breaks down tissues. This... this is different. It’s as if the water has been substituted.”

Soler straightened up, smoothing a non-existent wrinkle on his coat.

“Substituted? You’re talking about advanced cryobiology. That requires laboratories costing millions of euros, not a cave in the Pyrenees. Your imagination is looking for patterns where there’s only coincidence.”

“What if it’s not money that’s needed, but knowledge?” I threw the question like a hook. “A homemade compound. A mixture of glycerol and... what else? Dimethyl sulfoxide?”

Arnald let out a short, dry laugh, devoid of humor.

“Dimethyl sulfoxide is toxic at the concentrations needed to prevent total crystallization without industrial equipment. The subject would die before freezing, and the tissue would degrade due to chemical toxicity. You’d be left with an amorphous paste, not a statue.”

The answer flowed from him automatically, like a professor correcting a student.

“Then how would you explain that the samples I saw had no surface frost?” I insisted, taking a step toward him. “The skin was smooth. Perfect. Like porcelain.”

For the first time, Dr. Soler hesitated. It was a blink, a fraction of a second where his benevolent mentor mask cracked. His fingers tapped on the leather of his briefcase.

“Perhaps...” he began, choosing his words with surgical care, “perhaps if a modified ethylene glycol solution were used, with a stabilizing agent. Something that would rapidly increase viscosity to prevent ice nucleation.”

“Ethylene glycol. Antifreeze.”

“Refined,” he quickly corrected. “But that would generate a characteristic color. A bluish tint in the extremities.”

My mind clicked. The sound was deafening in my head.

I hadn’t told him anything about the color of the samples. The photos on the table were black and white prints I’d taken from the reception printer before coming up, or simply visualizations on the small camera screen which he hadn’t examined closely. And yet, he was visualizing the clinical result.

“Nobody mentioned bluish tints, Arnald.”

The silence that followed was dense, heavy as a lead blanket. The wind lashed the window, making the glass rattle in its frame.

Soler looked at me. There was no paternalism in his eyes anymore. There was a cold, calculating intelligence, the look of a chess player who realizes they’ve moved a pawn too early.

“It’s a medical hypothesis, Elena.” His voice dropped an octave, becoming dangerously soft. “I deduce based on physiology. If you inject that, cyanosis is inevitable. It’s textbook.”

“It’s not in the textbooks I studied. And I’ve read your complete bibliography.”

He smiled, but the smile didn’t reach his eyes.

“Medicine is a vast art. There are always dark corners not taught in universities, so as not to give ideas to... impressionable minds.”

He closed his briefcase with a dry snap.

“You should eat something. Sugar will help you think clearly. You’re seeing ghosts, Elena. And ghosts, when given too much attention, tend to become dangerous.”

“Is that a clinical or a personal threat?”

“It’s medical advice.” He walked to the door with a firm step. “The storm is going to get worse. Don’t go out. And stop playing detective with things that are beyond your comprehension. Nature has its own methods of preservation; it doesn’t need human help.”

He opened the door and the hallway air rushed in, bringing with it the distant echo of thunder.

“By the way,” he said, stopping in the doorway without turning, “that mixture you’re talking about... the proprietary one. If it existed, it would smell of bitter almonds and mint to mask the solvent. If you ever smell that, run.”

The door closed behind him.

I stood motionless, feeling the cold of the room seep into my bones. Almonds and mint.

I approached the table and picked up the camera. I zoomed in on one of the workshop photos, on a dark corner I had overlooked. There, barely visible behind a jar, was a box of industrial mint lozenges and, beside it, a dark glass container with a hand-engraved chemical symbol.

Soler hadn’t speculated. He had described the recipe.

I brought my hand to my mouth, feeling bile rise in my throat. He knew exactly what mixture was being used because it wasn’t a standard forensic technique. It was an author’s signature.

The “workshop” wasn’t just a place of butchery. It was a test lab for a thesis Arnald Soler had been writing in his head for years, or perhaps, executing with his own hands.

I looked at my father’s watch. The hands pointed to a quarter past one. Lucas was at the Town Hall, walking into the lion’s den with the Mayor, and I had just invited the wolf into my room.

I went to the door and bolted it, then dragged a chair and wedged it under the knob. A futile gesture, I knew. If Soler wanted in, he’d get in. If he was the Sculptor, or his mentor, no rotting wooden door would stop him.

My gaze returned to the tourist map on the table. Aigüestortes wasn’t a refuge; it was a giant Petri dish, and we were the cultures waiting to be frozen.

I had to warn Lucas. But when I took out my phone, the screen showed what I feared most: “No Service.”

The White Beast had arrived, and with it, total isolation. I was alone with the truth, and the truth had a metallic, poisonous taste.

## Chapter 10: La Jaula de Hielo

**Aigüestortes, December 21, 2018**
**8:00 PM**

The storm had ceased to be a mere meteorological phenomenon and had transformed into an entity with a will of its own. Outside, the snow wasn't falling; it was attacking. It hammered against the windows of the Hostal del Valle with the furious cadence of a thousand tiny fists demanding entry, burying the world under a white shroud that erased roads, paths, and any trace of civilization.

In room 204, Elena Vives felt the air thicken. It wasn't just the lack of ventilation, but the plummeting atmospheric pressure, an invisible weight pressing down on her chest and making the scar on her left palm throb with a dull rhythm.

She adjusted the digital camera's focus on the tissue sample. Through the macro lens, the vitrified flesh of the cave cadaver looked like an alien landscape, a geography of perfect crystals that defied biology. There was no cellular breakdown. That was the artist's signature: absolute preservation.

“It’s not natural ice,” she murmured to herself. Her voice sounded strange, absorbed by the wood-paneled walls.

She put the scalpel back into the forensic kit with a mechanical movement. Her fingers were stiff, and not just from the dropping temperature in the room. Doctor Soler’s lie about the chemical supplies floated in her mind like an oil slick on water. He had access. He knew how to stop biological time.

She brought her hand to her wrist and caressed the cold face of her father’s watch. The second hand lurched forward, the only rhythmic sound in that static chaos. Tick. Tock. Tick. She needed to leave that room, but the hallway felt as menacing as the blizzard outside.

***

Two kilometers away, at the local police station, Lucas Riera waged a losing battle against the static.

“Central, this is Aigüestortes. Do you read me? Over.”

The only response was an agonizing screech, a mixture of white noise and distortion that pierced his ear. He dropped the mic in frustration, letting it fall onto the desk covered with topographic maps.

His right leg was a block of pain. The drop in pressure announced disaster in his joints with more precision than any barometer. He massaged his thigh, feeling the tension of the muscles beneath the uniform fabric.

“Chief,” the young officer, Marcos, said, his voice trembling from the doorway. “The emergency generator for the antenna is coughing black smoke. The wind has bent one of the supports.”

Lucas stood up, ignoring the pang in his knee. He walked toward the armored window at the entrance. What he saw outside chilled his blood more than the mountain air.

The trees bent at impossible angles, like reeds before an invisible scythe.

“If we lose the antenna, we lose our eyes and our voice,” Lucas said, more to himself than to his subordinate. “We’re alone, Marcos.”

The young man nodded, pale. The radio emitted one last crack, like a bone snapping, and then fell silent forever.

***

Back at the Hostal, Doctor Arnald Soler crossed the deserted lobby. His footsteps were muffled by the Persian rugs, but the echo of his breathing was deafening to him. He wore his wool coat buttoned up to his neck, an insufficient armor against the cold seeping through the cracks of the old windows.

He stopped in front of the empty reception desk. The wood groaned under the assault of the wind. Arnald looked up the staircase leading to the second floor, where Elena Vives played detective with fragments of death. He knew she suspected. He had seen it in her green eyes, that analytical gaze that stripped intentions bare.

He plunged his hand into his pocket and squeezed the crumpled empty paper bag in his fist. He had to make a decision. The valley was closing in, transforming into a tomb of ice, and the secrets he had kept for decades threatened to surface with the thaw.

But there would be no thaw. Not soon.

***

The sound came first.

It wasn't thunder, but a metallic, sharp, and terrible ripping sound that echoed throughout the valley and overcame the wind's howl. It was the lament of steel yielding to nature.

On the northern slope, the telecommunications tower, the only umbilical cord connecting Aigüestortes to the twenty-first century, succumbed. The base bolts shot off like rifle bullets. The thirty-meter structure swayed once, twice, and then collapsed in slow motion, dragging the main power lines with it in a shower of blue sparks that illuminated the night for an instant, a photographic flash of the catastrophe.

In room 204, Elena’s bedside lamp flickered.

The light wavered, struggling to stay alive. Then, with an electric hum that sounded like a farewell, the bulb went out.

The laptop died.
The hum of the heating ceased.
The mechanical silence was instantaneous and absolute.

Elena stood motionless in the darkness. Her pupils dilated, desperately searching for a visual reference, but the world had shrunk to dense shadows. She rose from her chair, knocking over the forensic kit in her haste. The clatter of metal instruments hitting the floor was an explosion in the stillness.

She groped in the darkness until she found the window.

Outside, the village of Aigüestortes had ceased to exist. The streetlights on the main road, the lights of the houses on the hillside, the neon sign of the pharmacy... everything had been swallowed. There was no moon, no stars; only the grayish mass of the storm and the abyssal darkness that devoured everything.

Lucas, from the police station, watched as the blackness advanced through the valley like a tide of ink, swallowing building after building until it reached them. The emergency light on the ceiling flickered and, with a dry snap, the filament burned out.

“Shit,” Lucas whispered in the gloom.

They were blind. Deaf. Mute.

The “White Beast” had closed the cage.

Elena pressed her forehead against the icy windowpane of her room. Her breath fogged the glass, creating a barrier of mist that she had to wipe away with the cuff of her sleeve. She needed to see something, anything that would confirm she wasn't floating in a void.

And then, she saw it.

There, at the edge of the forest, where the black pines stood like sentinels of a forbidden kingdom.

A light.

It wasn't a streetlight, or a car. It was a fixed point of light, white, surgical. It shone with an unnatural intensity amidst the undergrowth, about three hundred meters from the hotel. It didn't flicker. It was there, static, defying the total darkness that had consumed the rest of the valley.

Elena felt her stomach clench into a fist. A chill that had nothing to do with the temperature ran down her neck, making the hairs on her arms stand on end. That light was not a distress signal.

It was an invitation.

Someone was out there, in the middle of the storm, with their own power source, watching the hotel. Watching her.

The light remained on for ten eternal seconds. Then, with the same abruptness with which the blackout had arrived, it extinguished, leaving a residual image on Elena’s retina and the absolute certainty that the hunt had begun.

## Chapter 11: El Visitante Nocturno

**HOSTAL DEL VALLE, CATALAN PYRENEES**
**DECEMBER 22, 2018**
**12:15 AM**

Darkness was not merely an absence of light; it was a physical entity that had devoured the lobby hours ago. Four hours, to be exact, since the antenna collapsed and the valley's electrical grid drew its last breath, leaving the Hostal del Valle plunged into an abyssal gloom.

Elena Vives adjusted her flashlight beam, watching how dust motes danced in the cone of white light, suspended like dead stars in a miniature galaxy. The cold was no longer a warning, but a tenant that had settled deep in her bones. She rubbed her arms over her technical jacket, feeling the futile friction against the waterproof fabric. The building's silence was absolute, broken only by the distant groan of the wooden structure resisting the assault of the external wind.

Lucas Riera stood by the main entrance, a silhouette cut against the shadows. His posture was rigid, his right hand resting instinctively on the holster of his weapon at his waist. The limp in his right leg was accentuated by the drop in temperature, forcing him to shift his weight from one foot to the other with an almost hypnotic rhythm.

"Did you hear that?" Lucas asked. His voice sounded raspy, devoid of echo in the vastness of the room furnished with leather sofas and Persian rugs that now looked like inkblots.

Elena nodded, though he couldn't see her outside the light's radius.
"Knocking."

It wasn't the wind. The White Beast, as the locals called the storm, howled and scratched, but this was rhythmic. Deliberate. Urgent.

*Thump. Thump. Thump.*

Someone was pounding on the solid wood of the main door with the desperation of one who knows time has run out.

Lucas stepped forward. His police flashlight beam cut through the blackness, illuminating the wrought-iron bolts. Elena followed him, the scalpel she had put in her pocket weighing like a moral anchor; a precision tool in a world that was becoming too crude.

"Step back, Elena," the policeman ordered. There was no doubt in his tone, only the authority of one accustomed to interposing himself between danger and civilians.

Lucas slid the top bolt. The metal screeched, a sharp sound that seemed to tear through the lobby's frozen air. As soon as he turned the knob, nature reclaimed its space.

The door burst open, pushed by an invisible, violent force. The blizzard erupted into the lobby like an explosion of ground glass. The wind howled, extinguishing the sepulchral silence and casting a horizontal curtain of snow that momentarily blinded Elena. The cold, until then passive, turned aggressive, biting into every inch of exposed skin.

A figure collapsed inward, falling onto the slate tiles with the dull thud of a sack of frozen meat.

Lucas struggled with the door, pushing with his shoulder and wincing in pain when his bad leg protested, until he managed to close it again, silencing the storm's roar to a menacing murmur.

Elena was already on her knees beside the newcomer. She focused her flashlight.

It was a man. Or what was left of one.

He wore a navy-blue anorak with the valley maintenance company's logo, torn at the shoulder. His face was a mask of cerulean horror; his skin had that grayish, translucent tone of old wax. Frost had welded itself to his eyelashes and three-day beard, creating an ice crust that gave him an unreal, almost sculptural appearance.

"Lucas, help me!" Elena cried, instinctively searching for a pulse in the carotid artery. Her own hand trembled, but her fingers found the man's skin hard as marble. "He's hypothermic!"

The policeman knelt on the other side, illuminating the body.
"It's Miquel. The antenna technician."

Miquel opened his eyes. There were no pupils, just two dilated wells staring into a terrifying infinity. His lips, cracked and blue, moved without emitting a sound, like a fish out of water gasping for oxygen.

Elena brought her ear close to the man's mouth. She felt his breath, faint and carrying that sweet, metallic odor that precedes the end.
"Miquel, look at me," she demanded, using her professional tone, that voice designed to anchor victims to reality. "You're in the hotel. You're safe."

The technician's body convulsed. It wasn't a shiver from the cold, but the final spasm of a collapsing nervous system. His gloved, stiff hand awkwardly rose to clutch Elena's lapel. The strength of the grip surprised her; it was pure desperation turned into motor action.

"No..." he croaked. The word emerged wrapped in a cloud of vapor. "Not... safe."

Elena searched for visible injuries. Aside from the evident frostbite, she saw bruises on his neck, purple marks that were not the work of the cold, but of human fingers. Someone had held him tightly.

"What happened, Miquel? Who did this to you?" Lucas asked, drawing so close that his flashlight illuminated the dying man's dilated nostrils.

The man coughed, a wet, cavernous sound. Dark blood welled from the corner of his lips, staining the melted snow on the floor.
"The... light..." he whispered, his voice broken like trampled dry leaves. "It saw me."

Elena felt a knot in her stomach, a physical pressure that made it hard to breathe. The light in the forest. The one they had seen from the window before coming down.

"Who saw you?" she insisted, wiping the blood with her sleeve, a useless but human gesture.

Miquel fixed his gaze on the dark ceiling, or perhaps beyond, piercing through the concrete and wood to the overcast sky. His eyes filled with a sudden and terrible lucidity, that final clarity sometimes granted by the last few seconds.

"The Sculptor..." he said, and this time the word was clear, crisp, a death sentence. "He's... in the basement."

The revelation fell upon them with more weight than the hotel's darkness. Elena froze. The basement. Beneath their feet. In the very structure where they sought refuge.

The grip on Elena's lapel loosened. Miquel's hand fell to the floor with a dry thud. The air left his lungs in a long, definitive sigh, taking with it the last spark of heat he had left. His eyes remained fixed, glassy, forever capturing the terror of whatever he had seen in the blackness.

Lucas let out a choked curse and ran a hand over his face, rubbing his eyes hard. "Damn it."

Elena pulled back slowly, crawling backward until her back hit the leg of an armchair. She looked at her hands. Miquel's blood was on her sleeve and the sensation of his death was impregnated in her skin.

"He's dead," she declared. It wasn't a question. It was the clinical confirmation of an irreversible fact.

Silence reclaimed the lobby, but now it held a different quality. It was no longer a void. Now it was inhabited. Inhabited by a corpse at their feet and by a threat breathing beneath them.

Lucas stood up, ignoring the pain in his leg. He shined his light down the hallway that led to the kitchens and, beyond, to the service stairs. The beam of light was lost in the blackness, unable to dispel the fear that had just germinated.

"He said the basement," the policeman murmured, fully drawing his weapon and flicking off the safety with a metallic click that echoed like a gunshot. "Elena, get your gear."

The woman nodded, picking up her flashlight from the floor. Her fingers brushed against the man's watch on her wrist, its mechanical ticking inaudible yet present against her accelerated pulse. The blizzard continued to batter the walls, trapping them in a tomb of ice and stone, but the true danger was no longer outside.

They had let death in, but the killer was already inside.

## Chapter 12: Descenso al Inframundo

**Aigüestortes, Boí Valley**
**December 22, 2018**

The service door, located behind the reception desk, gave way with a groan of swollen wood that shattered the sepulchral silence of the lobby. Lucas moved first, his service weapon extended and the beam of his flashlight cutting through the density of the suspended dust. His right leg, stiff from the atmospheric pressure change brought by the storm, dragged slightly over the tiles, producing a rhythmic hiss, painful to hear.

Elena followed him, clutching her camera with white knuckles. The air there smelled different from the metallic stench of blood they had left behind with Matías's corpse; down here, the atmosphere had the stale taste of stagnant dampness and a chemical undertone, similar to camphor, that stung her throat.

"Mind the stairs," Lucas murmured. His voice echoed off the narrow walls, distorted, as if the building wanted to swallow his words.

The descent was not a simple drop into a storage room. The staircase curved into a narrow spiral, carved into the living rock of the mountain on which the hotel stood. With every step they took, the already freezing temperature dropped another degree. Elena felt the cold pierce through the layers of her technical clothing, searching for the warmth of her vital organs like an invisible parasite. She brought her left hand to her chest, grazing the face of her father's watch, seeking in its mechanical ticking an anchor against the claustrophobia that was beginning to close its claws around her neck.

The exposed brick walls gave way to irregular granite blocks, enormous and primal.

"Stop," she whispered, stopping abruptly.

Lucas turned his body, sweeping the wall with his light.

"What do you see?"

Elena approached the stone surface. It wasn't empty. Someone, a long time ago, had used charcoal or a sharp tool to mark the rock. These were not the urban graffiti she was accustomed to in city alleyways, nor the signatures of bored teenagers.

They were diagrams. Geometric lines that overlapped with frenetic strokes. Elena traced one of the lines with her gloved finger. There were dates, some from the early twentieth century, and phrases in bastard Latin, written in a spiky, furious script. *Natura non facit saltus*. Nature makes no leaps. And below, more recent, almost fresh: *Perfectionis*.

"It looks like a record," Elena said, photographing the wall. The flash exploded, revealing for a fraction of a second the magnitude of the inscriptions: they covered the entire descending passageway, a tapestry of obsession carved in stone. "Or a countdown."

"Whatever it is, it's not Matías's," Lucas resumed walking, but his grip on the pistol had tightened. "This has been here for years."

The passageway abruptly opened into a vast cavern, and Lucas's beam of light got lost in the blackness before finding the ceiling. The sound of their footsteps changed; it was no longer the dry thud on stone, but a hollow, resonant echo, suggesting a cathedral-like vastness.

Elena raised her own flashlight, sweeping the space. Her breath hitched.

They were not in a conventional basement. Before them unfolded a colonnade of semicircular arches, worn by centuries but unmistakable. The floor was covered with fractured mosaics where blue and white tesserae formed wave patterns.

"Good God..." Lucas exhaled, lowering his weapon out of sheer disbelief.

In the center of the subterranean chamber, an immense rectangular pool dominated the space. There was no liquid water. The surface was a slab of black ice, perfectly smooth, reflecting their distorted silhouettes like macabre funhouse mirrors.

"Thermae," Elena deduced, her profiler's mind piecing together the architectural elements. "The hotel was built over ancient Roman baths. That's why they called it 'Sanatorium.' They took advantage of the medicinal waters..."

"Until they froze over," Lucas completed, illuminating the edge of the pool.

The discovery added a layer of oppressive antiquity to the place. They were treading on a forgotten stratum of history, a place designed for healing that now resembled a tomb. The mist of their breaths mingled in the static air.

A noise from the back, something like plastic scraping against stone, made Lucas raise his weapon again.

"Is anyone there?" he shouted. The echo returned his own voice multiplied: *there, there, there*.

No one answered, but Elena's flashlight caught a group of figures on the other side of the frozen pool, clustered in a corner, in the gloom.

There were five. Perhaps six. They stood, motionless, with limbs at unnatural angles.

"Lucas..." Elena felt a pang in her stomach, a sudden nausea that had nothing to do with the smell.

"Stay here."

"No."

They advanced together, circling the perimeter of the ice. The crunch of their boots sounded like gunshots in that mausoleum silence. As they drew closer, the figures gained definition. Pale skin, gleaming under the artificial light. Naked torsos.

Elena braced herself to see cadaverous faces, the empty expression of death that she knew so well. But when the beam of light fully illuminated the group, the reality was more grotesque than the expectation.

They weren't people; nor were they simple store mannequins. They were antique anatomical models, the kind used in medical schools decades ago. They were ravaged. One was missing its lower jaw, violently ripped off, revealing a rusted spring mechanism. Another had its torso ripped open, not with a surgeon's precision, but with an animal's fury, exposing painted plastic organs that dangled like rotten fruit.

They were arranged in a circle, as if holding a silent conclave.

Elena approached the tallest one. Its head was turned one hundred eighty degrees, facing its own back. On its fiberglass chest, someone had used a scalpel to carve into the synthetic material. This time, it wasn't words, but deep cuts, incisions mimicking surgical wounds.

"He was practicing," Elena whispered, feeling bile rise in her throat. The repulsion was physical, visceral. "It's not art. It's rehearsal."

Lucas illuminated the floor around the mannequins. There were fragments of synthetic 'skin' scattered everywhere, and among them, real tools. A rib spreader. Bent hemostatic forceps.

"Rehearsal for what?" the officer asked, though his tone suggested he'd rather not know the answer.

"To preserve," she replied, looking at the glass eyes of a child mannequin that watched her from the floor. "Or to correct what he deems imperfect."

Lucas's flashlight flickered once. Then twice. The battery, affected by the extreme cold, was beginning to die. The darkness at the back of the chamber, where the thermae extended into even deeper tunnels, seemed to advance a step toward them, reclaiming its territory.

## Chapter 13: Fracturas Internas

**Aigüestortes, December 22, 2018**
**Dawn**

The ascent from the depths of the hot springs had been a silent pilgrimage, a procession of two souls carrying the weight of a truth too great to be shared beneath the artificial light of the lobby. The night had dissolved not in a burst of sun, but in a degradation from black to dirty gray, that specific hue of damp ash that announces the outside world has ceased to exist.

Elena Vives watched the steam rising from her empty cup. Her hands, clutching the cheap ceramic, sought a warmth that the hotel's heating system could no longer guarantee. The industrial kitchen was a vast mausoleum of stainless steel and white tiles, a place designed for culinary noise and chaos, now reduced to a liturgical silence, broken only by the asthmatic hum of a dying refrigerator.

She wore her father's watch on her left wrist. The second hand advanced with a cadence that echoed in her temples, a constant hammering against the bone. *Tick*. A life. *Tock*. A death. The crescent-shaped scar on her palm itched, a phantom reminder that danger lay not only in the mutilated mannequins in the basement, but in the static tension that filled the room.

Lucas Riera stood by the frame of the service door, his shoulders propped against the wood as if he were holding up the building's structure by sheer will. His right leg, the bad one, barely grazed the floor; he shifted his weight to the left with a naturalness rehearsed over years of concealing weakness. His eyes, two dark coffee wells clouded by insomnia, swept the space, watching the entrances.

Before them, the hotel staff gathered like a frightened flock anticipating the wolf's imminence.

The manager, a man named Vallés whose bald head gleamed with a fine sheen of perspiration despite the cold, wiped his forehead with a crumpled handkerchief. Beside him, Marta, the angular-faced chambermaid, and Bernat, the burly cook with reddened hands, exchanged glances that needed no verbal translation. Fear has a universal language: dilated pupils and short breaths.

"The situation is unsustainable," Vallés said, his voice trembling, devoid of the feigned authority he usually displayed at the reception desk. "The road is still blocked. The radio is dead. And now… this."

Elena looked up. She met no one's gaze; she focused on the manager's jugular, watching how his pulse hammered wildly beneath the flaccid skin. 110 beats per minute. Contained panic.

"This?" Lucas asked. His voice was low, like sandpaper grinding against stone.

Bernat stepped forward. His apron, usually spotless, showed stains of old grease. He turned towards the large cold room at the back of the space, a metallic beast with a heavy door.

"I went to prepare breakfast," the cook growled. There was a mix of disbelief and fury in his tone. "Rationing, as you said. Something warm to keep spirits up."

He walked towards the dry pantry, an adjoining room with metal shelves, and swung the door wide open. The gesture was theatrical, violent.

Emptiness stared back at them.

It wasn't a total absence, which would have been less disturbing. It was a surgical removal. The canned goods were gone, the cured meats, the boxes of energy foods, the chocolate, the high-proof alcohol. Only open sacks of flour, useless spices, and paper napkins remained. The gaps on the shelves looked like teeth ripped from a diseased mouth.

"When?" The question left Elena's lips before she could filter it. Her analytical mind was already drawing timelines, calculating volumes and escape routes.

The sound of her voice seemed to act as a trigger. Heads turned towards her in unison, a movement choreographed by suspicion.

"Everything was here last night," Marta spat, crossing her arms as if to protect her vital organs. "I locked up myself before going up to the rooms."

"The lock isn't forced," Lucas pointed out, approaching to examine the frame. His calloused fingers ran over the metal without finding any scratches. "Whoever did it either had a key or knew how to bypass the bolt."

"Or perhaps they never went to sleep," Bernat said. The cook turned towards Elena, and his massive body seemed to expand, taking up more space than he was entitled to. "Perhaps someone has been wandering around the hotel while the rest of us tried to rest."

The atmosphere shifted. The temperature in the kitchen seemed to drop ten degrees at once. It was no longer a meeting of survivors; it was an impromptu tribunal.

Elena felt that familiar pressure in her chest, the claw of emotional claustrophobia closing over her lungs. *Don't react*, she commanded herself. *Observe*.

Bernat's knuckles were white. Vallés stared at the floor, unable to endure the confrontation, an accomplice by omission. Marta looked at her with a contempt that went beyond logic; it was the atavistic rejection of the tribe towards the outsider who brings misfortune.

"You've been here two days," the cook continued, stepping towards the table where Elena was sitting. "Ever since you arrived, everything's gone to hell. First the storm, then the noises, now the food."

"That's absurd," Elena said, maintaining a neutral, almost clinical tone. "Physically, I couldn't have moved that amount of provisions alone and without making noise. Besides, where would I hide it? In my room?"

"You have that backpack," Marta interjected, pointing to the mountain gear resting at the foot of Elena's chair. "And yesterday you went down to the basement. Nobody goes down to the basement. It's forbidden. What were you doing there? Hiding what you stole from us?"

The accusation lacked foundation, but in situations of extreme stress, truth is irrelevant; the only thing that matters is finding a culprit to channel the terror. Elena was the perfect scapegoat: distant, strange, a stranger to the valley.

"I was investigating," Elena replied. Her green eyes, the color of frozen moss, fixed on Marta. "And what's down there is much worse than a few missing cans of tuna. You should be worried about…"

"Don't come to us with city tales!" Bernat shouted, striking the steel table with an open hand. The clang echoed like a gunshot, making the crockery vibrate. "We want to know where the food is! I have children at home I don't know when I'll see, and I'm not going to starve to death because of some snobby woman from Barcelona playing detective!"

The cook advanced another step. His invasion of Elena's personal space was a blatant aggression. His body's shadow fell over her, eclipsing the pale light from the window. Elena tensed, her muscles preparing for flight or fight, adrenaline flooding her bloodstream with the metallic taste of ancient fear.

"Bernat, back away," Lucas warned from the pantry.

"You shut up, Riera," the man snapped, blinded by rage. "Always protecting outsiders. Are you screwing the…?"

The movement was a blur.

Lucas didn't run; his limp prevented it. But the speed of his reaction didn't lie in his legs, but in the explosive fury he had been containing. In two long, uneven strides, he placed himself between the cook and the table.

He didn't draw his weapon. He didn't need to.

Lucas grabbed Bernat by the lapel of his apron and shoved him back with a dry, brutal violence. The cook, despite his size, stumbled and crashed into the marble countertop, knocking over a stack of trays that fell with a tortured clatter of metal.

The police chief didn't stop there. He lunged into the space he had just created, cornering the other man. His face was inches from Bernat's. The veins in his neck stood out like cords beneath his skin.

"I said back away!" Lucas roared.

His voice was not that of a neighbor, nor a friend. It was the voice of broken authority, of a man who has seen the abyss and has no patience for human pettiness. The echo of the shout bounced off the tiles, silencing even the refrigerator's hum.

Marta let out a small gasp and clapped a hand over her mouth. Vallés stumbled back until he hit the wall, pale as wax.

Lucas was breathing heavily. The pain in his leg must have been agonizing after the sudden exertion, but he stood tall, a tower of protection between the mob and the woman sitting at the table.

"No one has stolen anything for themselves," Lucas said, lowering his voice but maintaining a lethal intensity. "Whoever took the food doesn't want to eat. They want us to starve. They want us to weaken. They want us to fight among ourselves like animals."

He slowly turned towards the group, releasing Bernat, who was rubbing his chest, dazed by the force of the shove.

"Elena was with me," Lucas lied, or at least, he omitted the part where they had briefly separated. "And what we saw downstairs… Believe me, she's not the problem. The problem is that we're not alone in this hotel."

A thick, almost solid silence settled in the kitchen. The accusation against Elena had dissolved, replaced by a latent hostility, colder and more dangerous. The group had fractured. They were no longer six people trapped by the snow. They were two sides: Lucas and Elena against the rest.

Elena slowly stood up. Her legs trembled, but her face remained impassive, a mask of cracked porcelain. She picked up her empty cup.

"Thanks for the trust," she said, her voice sharp as the wind howling outside the window. "If you'll excuse me, I have work to do before the light fades."

She left the kitchen without looking back, feeling the weight of hateful and fearful stares on the back of her neck. Lucas stayed for another second, holding his ground, making sure no one followed her, before limping after her.

The fracture was made. And through the crack, the cold began to seep in.

## Chapter 14: El Diario del Dr. F.

**Aigüestortes, December 22, 2018**
**Room 204**

The ascent from the kitchens was a pilgrimage through a dense, almost liquid silence. Elena Vives climbed the three flights of stairs, feeling the wood creak under her boots with a sound that recalled fracturing bones. She left behind the chaos of the ransacked pantry, the staff's mute accusations, and Lucas's wounded-dog look. She needed isolation. Her mind, that machine trained to categorize horror, demanded a sterile environment to process the next piece of the puzzle.

Upon entering Room 204, the stale air hit her with the smell of confinement and dampness. She closed the door and threw the bolt. It wasn't enough. She dragged the heavy oak chair and wedged it under the doorknob, building a physical barricade against a fear that was, in essence, invisible.

She pulled off her gloves with her teeth, anxious, and sat on the edge of the unmade bed. From her backpack, she extracted the object that burned through the fabric: the black leather-bound diary she had taken days earlier. The cover's leather was cracked, resembling the texture of an elderly hand left exposed to the elements.

She turned on the bedside lamp. The bulb flickered, struggling against the electrical storm besieging the valley, and cast long, convulsive shadows on the walls.

Elena opened the diary.

The smell of mold and formaldehyde rose from the pages, an olfactory mix that activated in her brain the muscle memory of morgues and autopsy rooms. She didn't read immediately. First, she observed the handwriting. It was a spiky script, slanted to the right with a manic aggression, where the accent marks looked like daggers on the page.

"Let's see who you were," she whispered, and her own voice sounded strange in the gloom.

She turned past the first pages, filled with dates from 1948. The author, who signed only with a stylized F, didn't write standard medical logs. They were manifestos.

"The corruption of flesh is biology's original sin. Heat is the enemy. In heat, bacteria thrive, tissues soften, morals dissolve. Heat is accelerated putrefaction."

A tightness settled in her temples. He wasn't a doctor seeking to cure tuberculosis; he was a fanatic obsessed with stopping time. She continued reading, her green eyes scanning paragraphs where scientific terminology mingled with mystical delusions.

"I have observed that ice does not destroy. Ice purifies. It suspends decay. My patients do not die in the snow; they transcend their own mortality. By lowering the body temperature below twenty-five degrees, the soul crystallizes, unblemished, perfect for eternity."

Eugenics. The word floated in Elena's mind, heavy as a tombstone. Dr. F. believed he was ‘saving’ his patients through methodical freezing.

An uninvited memory assailed Elena, violent and sudden. She wasn't in the hotel anymore. She was back in the Aragonese Pyrenees, six months ago. The case that broke her. She saw the face of that child, pale and bluish, preserved under a layer of morning frost. The static beauty of death that so horrified the living and which, it seemed, fascinated the author of this diary. Her breathing became shallow. Her chest tightened as if iron ribs were squeezing her from the outside.

"Focus," she ordered herself, digging her nails into the palm of her left hand, right over the crescent-shaped scar. The sharp pain served as an anchor.

She returned to the diary. She turned a page and paused at a detailed anatomical drawing with an almost obscene precision. It showed a human body submerged in what looked like an immersion tub, with notes on the freezing times for each organ. The liver resisted longer than the kidneys. The brain had to be cooled first to preserve the ‘essence.’

And then, she reached the list.

It occupied the last pages, written with that antique fountain pen. The header read: *CANDIDATES FOR THE MAGNUM OPUS*.

They weren't names of sick patients. They were descriptions of traits.
"Subject 1: Male, robust build, perfect facial symmetry. High pain resistance."
"Subject 4: Female, fertile age, light hair. Aesthetic purity is vital for preservation."

Elena understood with a vertiginous nausea that the Sanatorium hadn't been a hospital, but a macabre art studio where the materials were flesh and bone, and the chisel was absolute cold. The ‘Sculptor’ who now hunted them wasn't just any imitator; he was a disciple, a perpetuator of this frozen theology.

Her fingers turned the page with a tremor. The paper crackled, dry and brittle.

In the right margin of the last page, the texture of the handwriting changed.

It was no longer the sepia and faded ink of 1948. There was no oxidation of time in the strokes. It was blue ink, bright, chemical. Modern ballpoint ink. A recent addition, scribbled with such force that it had embossed the paper.

Elena brought the diary closer to the light, refusing to accept what her retinas were sending to her brain. Her heart pounded against her sternum with the violence of a trapped bird.

There, among the notes from seventy years ago, was a new list. Three names crossed out with a furious line. And below, the only intact name, written with handwriting that denoted possession and hunger.

*Elena Vives*.

Next to her name, a note in the same vibrant blue: *‘Intellectual symmetry. The centerpiece. Ready for collection.’*

She dropped the diary as if it were a burning ember. The notebook fell to the floor with a dull thud, lying open on that accusing page.

Elena backed up until she hit the cold wall of the room. Reality fractured. She wasn't there to solve a crime. She wasn't the hunter chasing a beast in the mountains. All this time, since she received the first letter, since she set foot in this cursed valley, she had been nothing more than livestock willingly walking into the slaughterhouse.

The wind howled outside, rattling the windowpanes, but the true cold, the one Dr. F. promised would purify the soul, had just settled into her own blood.

The enemy didn't just know who she was. It had been waiting for her for decades.

## Chapter 15: Avalancha

Aigüestortes, December 22, 2018
Noon

The valley wasn't silent; it held its breath.

Lucas Riera adjusted the collar of his parka, feeling the synthetic fabric scrape against the three-day stubble shadowing his face. The air had a metallic taste, like an old coin sucked under the tongue, a taste that preceded snowfalls erasing paths and rewriting geography. But this wasn't just meteorology. It was an artificial stillness, tense, like a violin string stretched to its breaking point.

His right leg, the one burdened with the memory of old injuries and the secret of a degenerative diagnosis he hid from the world, throbbed with a dull rhythm. It was his personal barometer. The pain wasn't sharp, but a constant pressure, a reminder that time was slipping away from him, not just in the investigation, but in life.

"I don't like the look of those cornices, Chief," said Marc, one of the village volunteers. The young man, barely a twenty-something with a face reddened by the biting wind, pointed towards the western ridge of the peak rising above the hotel.

Lucas followed the direction of his finger. The mountain leaned over them with the arrogance of an ancient god. Large accumulations of unstable snow hung like marble balconies about to give way.

"Keep people away from the northern perimeter," Lucas ordered, his voice hoarse, swallowed by the white immensity. "If that falls, it won't give any warning."

"Do you think it will hold until the snowplow arrives?"

Lucas didn't answer immediately. He observed the sky, a flat, gray mantle, without nuances, that seemed to have descended to crush the tops of the fir trees. There were no birds. There was no wind. Only that unbearable wait.

"No one is coming, Marc. We're alone."

The statement wasn't meant to be cruel, just honest. Lucas turned towards the facade of the old sanatorium converted into a luxury hotel. The building, an imposing structure of stone and wood, looked like an intruder in that realm of ice. On the second floor, a window reflected the leaden light of the day. Room 204.

Elena was there.

Thinking of her caused an involuntary contraction in his stomach. The professional admiration he felt for the profiler had mutated, quickly and dangerously, into a territory he couldn't afford to explore. She was a woman made of broken glass and sharp edges, seeking patterns in chaos, while he was a man trying to maintain order as his own body crumbled.

He took a step towards the main entrance, intending to regroup the volunteers in the lobby, when the world shattered.

It wasn't a roar at first. It was a snap. Dry. Violent. Unnatural.

*Crack.*

The sound reverberated through the valley with the clarity of a rifle shot, but magnified a thousand times. It wasn't the lazy creak of melting ice. It was a detonation.

Lucas stopped dead, his police instinct screaming before his brain processed the visual information. *Explosives*. Someone had detonated a charge on the ridge.

"Run!" the cry tore from his throat. "Inside! Everyone inside!"

He looked up. What he saw froze his blood faster than any blizzard. The mountaintop seemed to dissolve. The cornice Marc had pointed out was no longer a solid structure; it had transformed into a liquid wall, a wave of white foam and fury beginning to descend, devouring the hillside.

Gravity did the rest. Tons of snow, ice, and rock rushed down towards the valley, gaining speed with every meter, becoming an amorphous beast that obliterated the forest in its path. Centuries-old trees exploded like toothpicks before the mass's thrust.

From the window of Room 204, Elena Vives watched the end of the world.

She held the doctor's diary in one hand, the other pressed against the cold glass. Her mind, trained to dissect static crime scenes, struggled to process the kinetic violence unfolding before her moss-colored eyes. There was no beauty in that destruction, only a brutal, indifferent, and colossal force.

She made out the tiny figures below. Black dots on a white canvas. Among them, she distinguished Lucas.

She recognized him by his posture, by that way he shifted his weight onto his left leg. He was pushing others towards the doors, gesticulating, shouting words that the double glazing silenced.

"Lucas!" she cried, hitting the glass with an open palm, a useless, absurd gesture.

The white tide, a leviathan's tongue, reached the forest's edge and lunged at the hotel clearing. Elena recoiled, stumbling over the chair blocking the door, her heart hammering against her ribs like a trapped bird.

Below, time dilated. For Lucas, every second stretched into an eternity of hyper-realistic details. He perceived pure terror in Marc's eyes as the young man lunged into the lobby. He noticed how the pulverized snow, the 'aerosol' preceding the avalanche, obscured the sun, turning noon into twilight.

He tried to run. His brain sent the order to his legs, demanding speed, power, escape. But his right knee gave way. A sharp jab, like a burning nail, made him stumble.

He wouldn't make it to the door.

Understanding came with a strange calm. There was no panic, just an arithmetic acceptance of distance and speed.

He turned, turning his back on the white death bearing down on him, and threw himself into the recess protected by one of the stone buttresses of the entrance, covering his head with his arms.

The impact wasn't sound; it was pressure.

The world turned white, then gray, and finally, absolutely black.

The building groaned. The hotel's structure, designed to withstand rigorous winters but not the direct assault of the entire mountain, shook to its foundations. The roar was deafening, a cacophony of shattering glass, splintering wood, and twisting metal.

The snow didn't enter the lobby; it invaded it. It smashed the ground-floor windows as if they were made of sugar paper, inundating the reception, the lounge, and the corridors. The force of the avalanche dragged furniture, lamps, and works of art in a torrent of frozen debris.

Then, the lights flickered once. Twice. And died.

The mountain's roar ceased as abruptly as it had begun, leaving behind a dense, heavy silence, an acoustic void that hummed in the ears.

Elena lay on the floor of her room, hands over her head, breathing in dust and fear. The floor had stopped vibrating. She slowly pushed herself up, her muscles trembling from the adrenaline rush. The light coming through her window was diffused, sickly.

She approached the glass, or what remained of the view.

Below, there was nothing. The main entrance, the porch where Lucas had been moments ago, the access road... everything had vanished under an uneven blanket of dirty snow, mixed with branches and stones. The snow was piled halfway up the first-floor windows.

"Lucas..." the name left her lips like a broken prayer.

Beneath the surface, reality was very different.

Lucas Riera was alive, but he didn't know for how long.

He woke, or regained consciousness, in absolute darkness. He couldn't move. He felt an oppressive weight on his chest, an invisible slab preventing him from expanding his lungs beyond short, shallow breaths.

Panic, a reptilian creature, tried to take control. *I'm buried. I'm going to die here.*

*No.*

He forced his mind to work. He was a cop. He was a man of procedures. Step one: Assess.

He couldn't feel his legs, but he could slightly move the fingers of his right hand inside his glove. His mouth was full of icy grit. He spat, coughing, and the effort cost him a sharp pain in his ribs.

The cold wasn't ambient; it was direct physical contact. The snow enveloped him like a rapidly setting plaster mold. He knew, from mountain rescue training, that avalanche snow hardens like cement within minutes once the movement stops.

He had to move. Now.

He tried to arch his back. Nothing. He was immobilized. Claustrophobia began to close his throat, more effective than any human hand. The image of an ice tomb, perfect, immaculate, flashed through his mind. The 'Sculptor's' dream. Was this how it ended? Turned into a statue under the snow?

*Elena needs you.*

The thought was a lash. If he died, she would be helpless with the killer. Defenseless in a hotel that was now a death trap.

Anger gave him the strength that fear denied him. He grunted, an animal sound in the darkness, and pushed with his whole body, ignoring the pain in his leg, disregarding the lack of air. He felt something give way to his left. A void.

He was near the building wall. The buttress. If there was an air pocket, it was there.

He freed an arm, scratching at the compacted snow with desperation. His fingernails, protected by leather gloves, scraped against something hard. Stone. He followed the wall upwards. The snow was looser in that direction.

He dug relentlessly until his shoulder muscles burned. Snow fell on his face, seeping down his neck, melting against his skin hot from the effort, but he kept pushing towards what he hoped was the surface.

His hand broke through the crust of ice and found emptiness.

Air. Icy air, but breathable.

Lucas emerged from the snow like a castaway from a solid ocean, gasping, coughing violently. He dragged himself to a sitting position on the white debris, wiping his eyes.

The sight was Dantean.

He found himself inside the lobby, or what was left of it. The avalanche had blown out the main facade and filled the ground floor almost to the ceiling. He was sitting on a mountain of snow that reached the hall's chandeliers. He could touch the ceiling with his hand if he stretched.

He looked around, searching for the others.

"Marc?" his voice sounded weak, absorbed by the muted acoustics of the snow.

A movement to his right. An arm emerged from the whiteness. Lucas dragged himself over, his legs sinking into the unstable snow, and pulled. He helped the young volunteer out, trembling and pale, with a nasty cut on his forehead that was bleeding profusely, staining the snow a brilliant crimson.

"Chief..." Marc stammered, his teeth chattering. "What... what happened?"

"They've trapped us," Lucas murmured, looking towards where the reception desk should have been.

Everything was buried. The counter, the switchboard, the access to the basements.

The basements.

A new wave of horror struck Lucas.

"The generator room," he said, the word escaping with a cloud of vapor. "The emergency generators are on the lower level."

Marc followed his gaze. There was only compacted snow blocking the descending stairs. Tons of it.

The hotel was dark. No electricity. No heating. In the midst of a storm promising twenty below zero temperatures. And the only source of power was buried under ten meters of triggered avalanche.

Lucas looked towards the main staircase, which miraculously still stood, ascending from the mountain of snow into the darkness of the upper floors.

Upstairs, on the first-floor railing, he saw a figure silhouetted against the gloom. The unmistakable glint of a man's watch on a slender wrist.

His eyes met Elena's. There was no relief in her gaze, only a terrifying understanding of the new reality.

It was no longer about solving a crime. Now, the game had changed. The board had been overturned.

The killer didn't just want to kill Elena. He intended to freeze them all. To turn the entire hotel into his masterpiece, his ice mausoleum. And he had just laid the first stone.

Lucas looked at his hands. Large, calloused, trembling uncontrollably, not from the cold, but from fury. He clenched his fists until his knuckles turned white.

They were trapped in the belly of the white beast. And the temperature was starting to drop.

## Chapter 16: Hipoxia

**Aigüestortes, Lleida Pyrenees**
**December 22, 2018**

The silence after the roar weighed heavier than the rubble. It wasn't an absence of sound, but a physical pressure in her eardrums, a dense substance that replaced the air in Elena Vives's lungs.

From the first-floor railing, she observed the hotel's new geography. The lobby had ceased to exist. Where Persian rugs and leather armchairs once stood, now stretched a white dune, compact and violent, which had burst through the main doors and devoured the reception area. The snow wasn't the innocent powder of postcards; it was frozen concrete, soiled with splinters and pulverized glass.

Masonry dust floated in the anemic beams of light that filtered through the upper cracks, dancing like specters over the ground floor's grave.

“Lucas?” her voice sounded alien, raspy, as if she had swallowed sand.

A movement under the main staircase's arch broke the stillness. A figure emerged from the side gloom, shaking a layer of plaster from his shoulders. Lucas Riera coughed, a cavernous sound that echoed through the building's hollow structure.

Elena felt the knot in her diaphragm loosen, only to tighten again as she watched him walk. The police chief limped forward, dragging his right leg with a stiffness that went beyond his old injury. Each step over the rubble was a negotiation with pain.

“Up. Everyone up. Now,” Lucas ordered. His tone brooked no argument, though he was out of breath.

Survivors began to emerge from the rooms along Elena's corridor. Doctor Arnald Soler, his pristine gray wool coat immaculate despite the chaos, adjusted his glasses with trembling hands. A young couple of guests clung to each other against the wall, eyes wide with terror, staring at the white void that had swallowed the exit.

Elena looked at her wristwatch, the large, masculine face that had belonged to her father. The hands pointed to twelve twenty-five. Time kept ticking, indifferent to the world having stopped.

“The structure is holding,” Elena said, forcing her mind into analytical mode. It was her defense mechanism: converting terror into data. If she analyzed, she didn’t feel. “But we’ve lost the heating. The generators were in the basement.”

Lucas reached the first-floor landing. His face was ashen, smudged with soot, and a line of dried blood crossed his forehead. He leaned against the wall, avoiding putting weight on his bad leg. Their gazes met. In his dark eyes, Elena read the confirmation of her worst fears: this had not been a natural accident. But Lucas remained silent. It wasn't the time.

“We need to take a headcount,” he said, panting slightly. “Who’s missing?”

“Everyone on this floor is accounted for,” Arnald replied, assuming a fragile authority. “The kitchen staff... they were downstairs.”

A deathly silence followed the declaration. No one needed to verbalize the fate of those on the ground floor. They were beneath tons of compacted snow.

“We can’t go down,” Elena took charge, her voice gaining firmness. “And no one is coming to look for us. The road must be wiped off the map.”

She walked to the end of the corridor. The cold was already starting to claim the building. Without the constant hum of the heating, the hotel was rapidly transforming into a stone freezer. The temperature dropped by the minute, an invisible tide numbing fingers and slowing thought.

“We need a shelter within the shelter,” Elena declared. She turned to the group. “The library. It’s the innermost room, it has a fireplace and double doors. We need to concentrate body heat.”

“And stay cooped up in there?” protested the man from the young couple, his voice cracking with hysteria. “We have to try to dig out, get out through a window!”

Lucas stepped forward, interposing his massive body between the young man’s panic and reality.

“If he opens a window, it will kill us all,” the policeman said. His right hand rested near his waist, where he kept his weapon, an unconscious gesture of control. “Outside it’s twenty degrees below zero and dropping. With this wind, the wind chill is lethal in minutes. The hotel is the only thing separating us from freezing to death.”

Elena nodded, validating the order.

“Gather everything that can burn. Books, wooden chairs, blankets from the rooms. Take all the food you find in the mini-bars to the library. Let’s go.”

The group dispersed with clumsy movements, stunned by the shock. Elena approached Lucas, lowering her voice to a harsh whisper.

“Your leg.”

“I’m fine,” he lied, clenching his jaw. Cold sweat beaded on his temple despite the decreasing temperature. “It’s just the knock.”

“Don’t lie to me, Riera. I need you whole.”

“I need you to keep these people sane, Elena. That’s what I need.”

They headed to the library. It was a spacious room, paneled in dark wood, with shelves that reached the ceiling. Arnald was already there, piling leather-bound volumes inside the large stone fireplace. The doctor acted with an automated efficiency, burning culture to buy survival.

When the fire caught, flames licking at the ancient pages, a false sense of security flooded the room. The six survivors huddled near the hearth. They closed the double doors and sealed them with rolled towels at the base to prevent drafts.

The heat began to rise, but with it, something else.

An hour passed. Then two.

Elena sat in a wingback armchair, reviewing the contents of her forensic kit. Scalpel, evidence bags, luminol. Useless tools to fight the cold. Suddenly, she felt a throbbing behind her eyes. A dull, rhythmic pain.

She looked up. The young woman was rubbing her temples. Lucas, leaning against the bookshelf, blinked heavily, as if his eyelids were made of lead. The fire, once vibrant, had taken on a lazy, orange hue.

The air.

Elena stood up abruptly, and the sudden movement made her dizzy, almost knocking her over. The world swayed.

“The fire,” Elena gasped. Her own voice sounded distant. “Put out the fire.”

“What are you saying?” Arnald looked at her with disbelief, holding another book. “We’ll freeze.”

“We’re suffocating!” she shouted, advancing toward the fireplace.

She grabbed the poker and scattered the burning books, stomping on the embers that rolled across the rug. Acrid smoke filled the room, making everyone cough.

“The room is airtight,” Elena explained, feeling adrenaline momentarily clear the fog from her brain. “We’ve sealed the entrances. The fire consumes oxygen faster than we do. We’re creating a gas chamber.”

Lucas reacted, limping toward the double doors. He tore the towels from the floor and swung the leaves wide open.

The corridor’s air, icy and sharp as a blade, invaded the library. It was a brutal blow, but it brought with it the gift of oxygen. The survivors greedily inhaled, coughing, as the room’s temperature plummeted in seconds.

The dilemma presented itself to them with the clarity of a death sentence.

Elena hugged herself, trembling. She looked at Lucas, then at Arnald and the rest. Their breaths formed white clouds every time they exhaled.

“If we close up and light the fire, we die of hypoxia,” Elena said, with the certainty of a forensic pathologist dictating the cause of death before it happens. “If we ventilate, we die of hypothermia.”

No one responded. The truth floated among them, invisible and toxic. They had to choose between breathing or warming up. They couldn't have both.

Lucas closed the doors, but this time he didn't seal them. He left a crack open. An invitation to the cold, the price to pay for staying conscious. He returned to Elena, his movements growing slower, weighed down by the pain he refused to admit.

“Shifts,” he said. “We’ll take shifts to monitor the ventilation. Ten minutes closed, two minutes open.”

They sat on the floor, in a circle, like a primitive tribe surrounding a fire they could no longer feed with joy. The afternoon light died in the windows, giving way to a bluish darkness that erased their faces.

In that moment, Elena understood the true nature of their prison. The Sculptor didn’t need a weapon. He only needed time and physics. The hotel wasn’t a refuge; it was an icy mausoleum waiting for its occupants to stop moving.

Silence fell upon them again, but this time it wasn’t empty. It was filled with synchronized breaths, the sound of chattering teeth, and the absolute certainty that the coming night would be the longest of their lives.

## Chapter 17: La Sombra en el Pasillo

**Hotel Aigüestortes, West Wing Hallway**
**December 22, 2018**

Leaving the relative safety of the library felt like shedding a second skin. I left behind the dying glow of the fireplace and Lucas, whose restless sleep filled the room with an unbearable fragility. I needed to move. Immobility was a breeding ground for the ghosts that haunted my memory, and tonight, the hotel seemed determined to summon them all.

The hallway stretched before me like the gullet of a dead beast. The cold here was different from outside; it didn't have the wind's fury, but the patience of ice seeping through the seams of mountaineering clothes. I turned on my flashlight. The beam of light cut through the darkness, revealing suspended dust particles dancing in the stagnant air, tiny satellites in a sealed universe.

My boots barely made a sound on the frayed carpet, but the building's silence was so absolute that even the rustle of my jacket sounded like a gunshot. I clutched the scalpel I had taken from the forensic kit in my pocket. The cold metal against my palm, just over the crescent-shaped scar, anchored me to reality.

Then I heard it.

It wasn't the creaking of the building settling under the weight of the snow, nor the groan of the beams contracting with the temperature. It was a rhythmic sound. Deliberate. The soft impact of a rubber sole against creaking wood, about twenty meters ahead, right where the corridor turned towards the service rooms.

I stopped dead in my tracks. My breath caught in my throat, a ball of ice I couldn't swallow or expel. Someone was walking. It wasn't Lucas; his limp dragged with a different rhythm. And Doctor Soler had been lightly snoring when I closed the library door.

I switched off my flashlight.

Darkness swallowed me instantly, dense and liquid. My pupils dilated, desperately searching for any nuance in the blackness. I sharpened my hearing, becoming pure perception.

*Creak. Crack.*

Footsteps. They receded, but without haste. It was the gait of someone who knows they own the ground, who isn't afraid of being discovered because they are the predator.

I forced myself to move forward. Every step required a negotiation with my survival instinct, which screamed at me to turn back and entrench myself by the fire. But the profiler who still lived in some corner of my brain, buried under layers of trauma and snow, took control. I needed to see. I needed to know what we were up against.

I moved forward, pressed against the wall, feeling the rough wallpaper beneath my fingertips. Reaching the corner, I peeked out with the caution of someone looking into an abyss.

A shadow stood out at the end of the hallway.

It was barely a silhouette, an absence of light deeper than its surroundings. Tall, indistinct. It stopped in front of the door to room 314. It didn't open it with a key, nor did it force it. It simply vanished inside, as if the wood were mist.

My heart hammered against my ribs with the violence of a caged bird. I crept forward, counting the seconds, calculating the distance. Fear transformed into an icy clarity. I was cornered.

I reached the door. It was ajar, barely a black sliver. I pushed the wood with my shoulder, switching on my flashlight and raising the scalpel in one fluid motion, ready to slash, to scream, to kill if necessary.

"Stay still!" my voice came out hoarse, a croak in the gloom.

The beam of light frantically swept the room. The bed was unmade, but empty. The wardrobe, open, showed bare hangers swaying slightly, like mocking pendulums.

No one.

I spun on my heels, illuminating every corner. Under the bed. Behind the heavy curtains that hid the storm outside. Nothing. It was impossible. I had seen him enter less than 10 seconds ago. The room had no balcony, and the windows were sealed by ice and the tempest.

Then, a metallic sound above my head stopped my search. A soft *click*, followed by a muffled scrape.

I raised my flashlight towards the ceiling. The ventilation duct grille was not in place. It hung from a single screw, swaying slightly. The black, rectangular opening stared back at me like an open maw.

I understood the architecture of our prison with renewed horror. The hotel wasn't just hallways and rooms; it had a hidden circulatory system. A network of metal tunnels where stale air traveled... and where he moved. While we were trapped on the surface, guarding the doors, the Sculptor crawled through the building's entrails, invisible, omnipresent. He could be anywhere. He could be above us right now, watching through the vents.

I lowered my gaze, dizzy from the revelation. My flashlight beam bounced off the dressing table mirror at the back of the room.

I approached, drawn by something that didn't fit the thermal logic of the place.

The glass was fogged. A patch of condensed mist in the center of the mirror, an island of warm breath in an ocean of cold. Someone had been there, standing before the reflection, exhaling their own life against the glass seconds before I entered.

And in the midst of that ephemeral cloud, a gloved finger had traced some words.

The condensation was already beginning to evaporate, devoured by the dryness of the freezing air. The letters lost their edges, turning into water, blurring before my eyes like a secret the universe refuses to keep.

I read the message just before the last vowel disappeared:

"You're making too much noise, Elena."

The mist completely dissipated, leaving me alone before my own reflection. My green eyes, wide and gleaming with panic, stared back at me from the glass. But I was no longer alone in that room. The sensation of being watched, not from a corner, but from above, from the walls, from the very air I breathed, lodged itself in the back of my neck.

It wasn't a hunt. It was a game. And he had just changed the rules.

## Chapter 18: Fiebre de Cabaña

**Aigüestortes, Catalan Pyrenees**
**December 23, 2018**

Dawn did not arrive like a promise, but like a grayish whisper, a corpse of light that barely managed to pierce the crust of ice accumulated on the library's windows.

I had returned to the common refuge shortly after my foray into the west wing, with terror still clinging to my skin like a second layer of thermal clothing. No one asked me where I had been; misery is a potent narcotic, and most of the guests were too busy trying not to freeze to notice my absence or return. I curled up in a corner, away from the mahogany shelves, hugging my knees, watching reality fray at the edges of the room.

The air had grown dense, almost chewable. It smelled of stale sweat, of damp clothes that never dried, and, beneath it all, the metallic tang of stagnant fear. The ventilation continued to spit out inadequate gusts of heat, but it brought something else with it: a constant hiss that burrowed into the eardrums, a low frequency that made teeth vibrate.

“They’re there…” a voice whispered to my left.

I turned my neck, feeling the stiffness of my vertebrae. He was a young man, perhaps thirty years old, wearing an electric blue anorak that now looked dull with grime. He stared fixedly at an empty spot on the wall, right where the wallpaper was peeling from the damp.

“Who?” I asked, more from inertia than curiosity.

“The birds,” he replied, without blinking, his pupils dilated to devour his irises. “They have fire in their beaks. They want to burn the books.”

I tensed. There were no birds. Only elongated shadows and the silence of the snow burying us alive. Hypoxia. Or perhaps carbon monoxide slowly seeping in, an invisible killer competing with the one hiding in the vents. “Mountain madness,” the locals called it. The mind, deprived of stimuli and subjected to perpetual cold, began to invent its own monsters so as not to feel so alone.

Across the room, a middle-aged woman rocked rhythmically, murmuring a lullaby to a velvet cushion. The group's cohesion, that fragile social glue that had kept us civilized for the first forty-eight hours, was evaporating. We were no longer people waiting for a rescue; we were caged animals measuring the thickness of the bars.

The trigger was absurd, as tragedies often are.

A blanket. A simple piece of Scottish wool, frayed at the edges, resting on the back of a wingback armchair.

The man in the blue anorak, the one who saw fire birds, rose with spasmodic movements. His hands, purple from the cold, closed around the fabric.

“It’s mine!” The shout ripped through the library's lethargic atmosphere.

There was no warning. A second man, burly and with a beard full of frost, lunged at him. The clash of bodies sounded hollow, unpleasant, flesh hitting bone without the cushioning of courtesy.

They rolled on the floor, knocking over a pile of encyclopedias that fell open, like dead birds with broken wings.

“Let it go, you thief!” bellowed the bearded man, throwing a clumsy punch that landed on the other’s ear.

Chaos erupted. The screams of the woman cradling the cushion joined the cacophony. Violence, contagious and primitive, electrified the air. I saw blood splatter the Persian rug, bright red drops that looked obscene against the dark fabric.

“Stop!” The command tried to assert itself, but it came out fractured.

Lucas Riera advanced from his position near the door. He moved poorly. The limp in his right leg was more pronounced today, a painful burden that robbed him of physical authority. His face was pale, waxy, and the weapon at his hip seemed a dead weight he had no intention of using.

He tried to grab the bearded man by the shoulder, but his grip lacked strength. The combatant thrashed with the fury of desperation and pushed the officer. Lucas stumbled, his boots slipped on an open encyclopedia, and he fell to one knee with a choked grunt that betrayed his fragility.

The police chief, our designated protector, was failing. The illness devouring him from within, the one he tried to hide behind a mask of stoicism, was winning the battle against his will.

The individual in the blue anorak took advantage of the distraction. His fingers sought his opponent's neck, squeezing the windpipe with lunatic force. His eyes bulged from their sockets, bloodshot, seeing enemies that only existed in his oxygen-deprived brain.

He was going to kill him. Right there, over a piece of cloth.

I straightened up, searching my pockets for the scalpel I had stolen, though I knew I wouldn't make it in time. The distance was an unbridgeable chasm.

Then, a white shadow slid into my peripheral vision.

He didn’t run. He didn’t scream. Doctor Arnald Soler moved with the fluidity of a specter passing through a wall. His wool lab coat rippled slightly around him, not like clothing, but like an extension of his own coldness.

He reached the tangle of limbs struggling on the floor. He didn’t try to separate them by force. He didn’t waste energy on useless struggles.

He knelt beside the aggressor, the one in the blue garment. His right hand descended, quick and precise like a viper’s strike. There was a metallic flash, brief, almost imperceptible in the morning’s dim light.

A sharp moan escaped the man’s lips.

Soler withdrew his hand.

One, two, three seconds.

The individual blinked, confused. The tension in his arms dissolved like a sugar cube in hot water. His eyelids fluttered, heavy, and he slumped to one side, rolling away from his victim’s body with the inert grace of a sack of sand.

The silence that followed was absolute, heavier than the storm raging outside.

The bearded man scrambled backward, gasping, touching his reddened throat. He looked at Soler not with gratitude, but with the pure terror of one who has just watched a larger predator devour a smaller one.

Lucas got to his feet with difficulty, leaning on a table. His gaze traveled from the unconscious man to the doctor’s right hand.

Soler straightened up slowly. He adjusted his glasses with a mechanical gesture, devoid of any emotion. In his hand, he held an empty syringe. The plunger was pressed all the way down. With a casual, almost bored movement, he put it into the deep pocket of his lab coat.

“He was wasting oxygen,” Soler said. His voice was flat, without edges, a straight line on a heart monitor. “And disturbing the others.”

I felt a new cold, distinct from the mountain's, spread in the pit of my stomach.

He hadn't hesitated. He hadn't assessed the situation. And the most terrifying thing: he had the syringe ready. Loaded. Ready in his pocket before the fight even began. As if he had been waiting for an excuse, any excuse, to use it.

My gaze met Lucas's. In his dark, tired eyes, I saw my own conclusion reflected, a certainty that chilled the blood more than any blizzard.

The doctor wasn't taking care of us. He was managing us. Like cattle. Or worse, like specimens in a Petri dish that must be kept still to be studied.

No one said anything. No one claimed the blanket. The sedated man snored softly on the floor, and the rest of us drew away from Arnald Soler, creating a circle of emptiness around him. He simply sat back in his armchair, opened a book, and continued reading, as if he had just squashed an annoying insect.

The library was no longer a refuge. It was a waiting room. And the doctor decided who moved on to the next phase.

## Chapter 19: El Paciente Cero

**Aigüestortes, December 23, 2018**
**East Wing of the Grand Hotel**

The corridor connecting the main building to the closed-off wing was no mere hallway; it was a gullet of rotten wood that threatened to swallow them whole.

Elena Vives advanced, hugging the wall where the main beams offered greater support. Each step was a negotiation with gravity. The floorboards groaned with the whine of a dying animal beneath her hiking boots, releasing clouds of dust and spores that danced in the beam of her flashlight. Behind her, Lucas Riera's breathing was heavy, rhythmic, marked by the effort of dragging a right leg which the storm's humidity had turned into a block of concrete and pain.

“Step where I step,” she whispered, though the advice was unnecessary.

Lucas didn't respond. He merely swept the darkness with the barrel of his service weapon, an extension of steel that trembled imperceptibly in his calloused hand. The cold here was different from that of the library; it wasn't the crisp chill of snow, but stagnant, dense air, laden with the cloying scent of dampness and oblivion.

The east wing had been condemned decades ago, long before the hotel tried to reinvent itself as a luxury retreat. The doors on both sides of the corridor hung from their hinges or were boarded up with planks that time had warped. The wallpaper peeled in long, grayish strips, revealing the cracked plaster like the skin of a leper.

Elena stopped before a fork where the ceiling had partially given way, allowing snow to seep in and form a white mound on the threadbare carpet.
