“According to the evacuation plans I saw in the lobby, the main suites were at the far end,” she said, her voice barely a wisp of vapor. “If someone wanted to hide something valuable, or shameful, they would do it far from the eyes of guests and staff.”

“Or it’s just a ruined area, Elena.” Lucas wiped the sweat from his forehead with the back of his left hand. “If we take one wrong step here, we’ll fall two floors down to the boiler room.”

“It’s not total ruin. Look.”

Elena pointed at the floor. Amidst the layer of dust and debris, there was a clean path. Someone had walked there recently. The footprints weren't clear; they had been deliberately erased, but the pattern of “cleanliness” in the grime was undeniable. A trace of order in the chaos.

They advanced. Tension coiled at the nape of Elena's neck, a physical pressure she knew well. Her profiler's mind, trained to dissect crime scenes, began cataloging the anomalies. There were no rats. The silence was absolute, a vacuum bell that isolated them from the howling wind outside.

They reached the end of the corridor. There, where logic dictated there should be a wall or a broken window, stood a solid oak door. Unlike the others, this one didn't have peeling paint. The brass knob gleamed, polished with obsessive care.

Lucas stepped forward, ignoring the protest of his knee, and placed his hand on the knob. It was locked, but the wood around the frame showed signs of having been forced and repaired multiple times, like a wound that opens and is stitched up again and again.

“Cover me,” the officer ordered.

With a dry thud of his shoulder, Lucas forced the mechanism. The door yielded with a hydraulic sigh, revealing a darkness far deeper than that of the corridor.

Elena entered first, raising her flashlight. The beam of light cut through the gloom and what it revealed stole her breath.

It wasn't a hotel room. It was a sanctuary.

The windows were hermetically sealed with metal sheets, preventing a single ray of natural light or snowflake from entering. The air inside was glacial, much colder than outside, artificially maintained by a ventilation system that hummed at an almost inaudible frequency. But what struck Elena wasn't the cold, but the smell.

Mint and antiseptic.

The scent was so strong it pricked at her throat. It was the same smell that, according to the files, permeated the killer's letters. The smell of clinical sterility.

“My God!” Lucas murmured, lowering his weapon.

In the center of the room, a 1970s hospital bed dominated the space. The sheets were stretched with military precision, not a single wrinkle. Beside it, a surgical metal table held a collection of amber glass bottles, antique glass syringes, and a black leather notebook.

The walls weren't adorned with paintings, but with anatomical diagrams and hand-drawn industrial refrigeration schematics.

Elena approached the table, feeling her pulse throb in her fingertips. She placed her flashlight on a flat surface to free her hands and picked up the notebook. Her green eyes scanned the pages. The handwriting was spiky, nervous.

““Day 450. Cellular degradation has stopped. Beauty remains.”” she read aloud. Her voice sounded strange in that acoustic chamber. ““Ice does not destroy; ice suspends. It is the only way to love something forever: by preventing it from changing.””

Lucas walked the perimeter of the room. He stopped before a low bookshelf.

“Elena, you have to see this.”

She closed the notebook and joined him. On the dark wooden ledge, there was a series of silver-framed photographs. They were old, black and white or sepia, but perfectly preserved.

The first showed a man in that very bed. He was emaciated, skin clinging to his skull, eyes sunken in dark sockets. A degenerative disease consumed him.

The second photo was what chilled the blood.

It showed the same man, but this time he wasn't alone. A boy, about ten years old, sat at the foot of the bed. The boy didn't look at his father with sadness, nor with fear. He looked at him with an analytical, almost scientific intensity. In the child's hands rested a block of ice, and his clear, inexpressive eyes seemed to study how the solid water melted between his fingers.

Elena picked up the photo. Turning it over, she found an inscription in faded blue ink: ““For my son, the only one who understands that death is merely a technical failure. 1982.””

“Patient Zero,” Elena whispered, feeling the puzzle pieces click into place in her mind. “All this... the hotel, the isolation, the obsession with cold. He’s not a random serial killer.”

She turned the photo so Lucas could see it clearly in the flashlight's beam.

“This man died here,” she continued, pointing to the empty bed. “Or at least, his body stopped functioning. But his son didn’t accept the end. He turned this room into a laboratory to try and preserve him.”

Lucas leaned closer to the image, his tired eyes narrowing.

“The Sculptor…” the officer said, comprehending the magnitude of the horror. “He doesn’t kill for pleasure. He’s practicing.”

“He’s perfecting the technique,” Elena corrected, looking around with new terror. “The diagrams on the wall weren’t theoretical; they were instructions. Each victim is a rehearsal. An attempt to achieve what he couldn’t with his father: perfect preservation. Immortality through ice.”

A dry crack, like wood splitting, echoed in the corridor outside.

Lucas spun around, pointing towards the open door. Elena instinctively extinguished her flashlight, plunging the room into a grayish gloom.

“Someone knows we’re here,” Lucas whispered.

Elena pressed the photograph against her chest. The Sculptor’s legal identity remained a mystery, a name in a census, but now they knew his soul. They knew where his monster came from. He was an orphaned child who refused to bury his father, and who had decided to freeze the entire world so he would never have to say goodbye again.

The vengeance wasn’t against people; it was against time itself. And they had just desecrated his church.

## Chapter 20: El Chivo Expiatorio

There was no surprise on his face, only an unsettling placidity, like the surface of a frozen lake hiding dark depths.\n\n\"Mr. Blasco,\" Lucas said, positioning himself to his right, his hand near the holster of his service weapon. \"Stand up. Slowly.\"\n\n\"Is something wrong?\" Blasco asked. His tone was flat, lacking the nervous vibration any innocent person would have in that situation.\n\n\"Your suitcase,\" I demanded, pointing to the brown leather luggage at his feet. \"Open it. Now.\"\n\nA murmur ran through the room. The rest of the survivors stood up, forming an irregular circle, drawn by the magnetism of the conflict. Human curiosity is a parasite that feeds on the misfortune of others.\n\n\"This is absurd,\" Blasco protested, but his hands obeyed.\n\nThe metal clasps snapped open. The sound was dry, definitive, like the trigger of an unloaded gun. He lifted the lid.\n\nClothes folded with military precision. A gray toiletry bag. Nothing at first glance.\n\n\"Step aside,\" Lucas gently pushed him with his shoulder and knelt.\n\nMy profiler's eyes scanned Blasco. I looked for signs of escape, tension in his jaw, pupil dilation. Nothing. Either he was innocent, or he was a psychopath with absolute emotional control. Doubt, that corrosive acid, began to drip into my stomach.\n\nLucas rummaged through the shirts. His large, calloused hands probed the lining. Suddenly, he stopped.\n\n\"Elena,\" he called, without taking his eyes off the inside of the luggage.\n\nI approached. Lucas extracted an object wrapped in a hotel towel. He unwrapped it on the floor with ceremonial care.\n\nThe silence in the room turned solid.\n\nOn the white terry cloth lay a set of surgical scalpels, shiny, immaculate. And next to them, a small, unlabeled pharmaceutical glass bottle, filled with a clear liquid. But what stole the air from my lungs wasn't that.\n\nIt was the photographs.\n\nThree Polaroids scattered like cards from a macabre deck. They weren't landscape photos. They were close-ups. Open, glassy eyes covered in frost. Blue lips stitched with fishing line. The Sculptor's \"work,\" documented with the pride of an artist cataloging his collection.\n\nThe menthol scent enveloped me. It came from the clothes in the suitcase. That antiseptic, clean scent that had permeated Patient Zero's room.\n\n\"My God!\" someone exclaimed behind me. I think it was one of the young tourists.\n\nBlasco took a step back, hitting the wall. For the first time, the mask of calm cracked. His eyes widened disproportionately, two wells of genuine panic.\n\n\"That's not mine,\" he stammered. His voice rose an octave. \"I swear it's not mine! Someone put it there. I haven't touched that suitcase all day!\"\n\n\"He has photos of the corpses, for God's sake!\" roared Dr. Soler, stepping forward with a fury that struck me as strangely theatrical. \"You killed them! You froze them as if they were pieces of meat!\"\n\n\"No!\" Blasco waved his hands. \"I'm an accountant! I'm from Madrid! I know nothing about this!\"\n\nLucas didn't wait any longer. Police instinct took over any reasonable doubt. He lunged at Blasco, twisting him against the wall with a dull thud.\n\n\"Hands behind your back!\" Lucas yelled.\n\n\"They're setting me up!\" the accused shrieked, struggling weakly. He didn't have the physical strength to move bodies, I thought fleetingly. How could he have dragged the victims? But the adrenaline of the moment swept away my logical analysis like an avalanche.\n\n\"Hold still!\" Lucas twisted his arm. Blasco whimpered in pain.\n\n\"Arnald, the rope,\" I requested, pointing to the supplies we had stacked near the fireplace.\n\nDoctor Soler handed me a roll of nylon cord with trembling hands. I approached Lucas, and together we tied Blasco's wrists. The nylon dug into his skin, white and soft, nothing like the hands of a craftsman or a killer who works with ice.\n\nWe seated him in a sturdy wooden chair, away from the exit, and secured his ankles to the legs. Blasco was crying now. Thick, silent tears rolled down his cheeks and died on his shirt.\n\n\"Why?\" Lucas asked, standing in front of him, his chest heaving. \"Why do something like this?\"\n\n\"It wasn't me...\" Blasco sobbed, his head bowed. \"You have to believe me.\"\n\nI stepped away from the circle. The room seemed to exhale a collective sigh. The shoulders of those present dropped. The threat had a name and a face. It was tied. It was no longer a ghost lurking in the blizzard, but a pathetic man strapped to a chair.\n\nI walked to the window. My reflection in the glass showed a woman with deep dark circles and matted hair. Behind my reflection, the storm still roared, indifferent to our small victory.\n\nI looked at the Polaroids that had been left on the floor. Something didn't fit. The Sculptor sought perfection, eternity. These photos were out of focus, poorly framed. They were vulgar. And the suitcase... it wasn't locked, accessible to anyone in the common room while Blasco went to the bathroom or slept.\n\n\"We got him, Elena,\" Lucas said, standing beside me. He put an arm around my shoulders. I felt the warmth of his body, and for a second, I wanted to fall into him, forget logic, forget the scar on my palm, and simply accept that the horror was over.\n\n\"Yes,\" I lied. My voice sounded hollow. \"We got him.\"\n\nDoctor Soler had approached the suitcase and was looking at the scalpels with morbid fascination.\n\n\"Precise work,\" he murmured to himself, though I heard him perfectly. \"Very precise.\"\n\nOutside, the wind howled with renewed force, rattling the windows. The White Beast pounded the sanatorium walls, furious that we had snatched away its servant. Or perhaps, it was laughing.\n\nI looked at Blasco, huddled in his chair, defeated. And then I looked at the rest of the room, at the shadows dancing in the corners where the firelight didn't reach. The feeling of relief flooding the room seemed like a dangerous narcotic. We had swallowed the lie because we were thirsty for security.\n\nMy analog watch read a quarter past eleven. The second hand advanced relentlessly, oblivious to justice or error.\n\nTick. Tock.\n\nWe had caught the scapegoat. But the wolf was still at large, and now, we had all let down our guard.",

We had a face from the past, a child, but we needed a culprit in the present.\n\n\"Are you sure you want to do it this way?\" Lucas whispered. ## Chapter 21: Inocencia Congelada

Aigüestortes Refuge, Catalan Pyrenees
December 24, 2018. 3:15 a.m.

The silence was not an absence of sound, but a physical presence that pressed against the eardrums until they ached. Outside, the White Beast had decided to take a breath, a deceptive truce in which the wind ceased its howling to allow the cold to do its work in solitude: to petrify the world.

Elena Vives observed the phosphorescent hands of her analog watch. The second hand advanced with that military cadence her father had taught her to respect and fear in equal measure. A quarter past three. The wolf's hour. The hour when human biology is most vulnerable and the spirit's defenses surrender to fatigue.

She couldn't sleep. The image of Javier Blasco's suitcase, filled with scalpels and macabre Polaroids, continued to dance behind her eyelids every time she tried to close them. It was too perfect. Too symmetrical. In nature, nothing fits with such precision unless it has been forced by the hand of man. Glaciology had taught her that ice is chaotic in its microscopic structure; it only appears smooth from a distance. And that case, seen up close, had cracks.

She sat up on the cot in the common room. The air was stale, heavy with the deep breathing of the survivors who slept the sleep of the just, or of the condemned, believing the monster was caged. Lucas Riera dozed in a chair by the door, his chin pressed to his chest and his right hand resting on the holster of his service weapon. Even in sleep, the police chief maintained the posture of an exhausted sheepdog.

Elena slipped out from under the blankets. The wooden floorboards creaked under her boots, a dry groan that made Lucas instantly open an eye.

“Where are you going?” he whispered. His voice was gravel and fatigue.

“I need to see him,” Elena adjusted the collar of her fleece jacket. The cold from the hallway was seeping in under the door. “There’s something in his statement… the way he was screaming that it was a trap. Narcissistic psychopaths don’t usually plead, Lucas. They manipulate, seduce, or remain silently superior. Blasco was terrified.”

Lucas rubbed his face with his calloused hands, stretching the skin to chase away sleep.

“The evidence is solid, Elena. He had the tools. He had the photos.”

“The tools were clean. Too clean for someone who’s supposedly been operating in this valley for years.” Elena already had the flashlight in her hand and the key to room 204 in her pocket. “I’m just going to check that he’s still there. I need to see his eyes one more time without the clamor of the crowd accusing him.”

Lucas stood up, ignoring the pang in his right leg that always warned him of pressure changes.

“You’re not going alone.”

They stepped into the hallway. The temperature there dropped drastically, turning their breath into small, ephemeral clouds that vanished before touching the ceiling. The corridor was plunged into liquid darkness, barely broken by the white beam of Elena's flashlight. The shadows of the beams stretched and contracted like the ribs of an immense animal breathing slowly.

They reached door 204. The wood was robust, old pine.

“Do you have the key?” Lucas asked, his hand already on the doorknob, verifying it was still locked from the inside.

Elena nodded and pulled out the cold metal. She inserted it into the lock. The *click* of the mechanism turning sounded like a gunshot in the refuge's stillness.

“Stay back,” Lucas ordered, regaining his role, gently moving her aside with his shoulder to enter first.

He pushed the wooden door open.

The smell hit them before the sight. It wasn't the stench of putrefaction; that would take hours. It was a ferrous, dense, and warm aroma, mixed with the involuntary emptying of the sphincters that accompanies the end of life. It smelled like a freshly hosed-down slaughterhouse.

Lucas raised his tactical flashlight and swept the room.

Elena stifled a scream, bringing the back of her hand to her mouth to block the bile that rose in her throat like boiling acid.

Javier Blasco was still in the chair where they had left him. But his posture had changed. His head hung back at an unnatural, almost obscene angle, defying his cervical vertebrae.

“My God!” Lucas murmured, taking two steps forward, his weapon drawn and pointing at the empty corners of the room.

Elena, driven by a morbid force that overcame her fear, approached. The light from her flashlight illuminated the prisoner's neck.

It hadn't been a struggle. It had been an execution.

A deep, clean, surgical cut ran across his throat from left to right. It had severed the carotid artery and jugular vein with such precision that the edges of the wound looked as if drawn with a ruler. Blood had spurted out forcefully, soaking Blasco's chest, the chair, and creating a black, viscous puddle that crept towards Elena's feet. It was no longer flowing; his heart had stopped pumping a while ago. The blood was beginning to take on that gelatinous texture, similar to blackcurrant jam, indicating that death had occurred at least an hour ago.

“The door was locked…” Elena whispered. Her analytical mind tried to process the horror by converting it into data, into physics, into anything but reality. “Lucas, the door was locked.”

The officer approached the body, avoiding stepping into the crimson lake. He holstered his weapon and put on the latex gloves he always kept in his back pocket, a habit from his days in the city.

“The restraints,” he said, his voice trembling. “Look at his wrists.”

Elena directed the beam of light towards Blasco's hands, tied to the back of the chair. The knots were intact. The climbing rope dug into the pale flesh, taut and cruel. There wasn't a single sign that he had tried to free himself. Whoever had entered had done so with such stealth or authority that Blasco hadn't even had time to struggle.

Or he knew his killer.

“He couldn't have done it himself,” Lucas stated, verifying the incipient rigidity of the fingers. “No one cuts their own throat like this and then ties their hands behind their back. It’s physically impossible.”

Elena felt the floor tilt. The false security of the past six hours crumbled like a powder snow avalanche. They had believed they had the culprit. They had slept, they had let down their guard, they had felt that narcotic relief of justice served.

And while they rested, the true Sculptor had entered this room.

“He’s here,” Elena said. The words left her mouth like cold stones. “Lucas, he hasn’t left. Blasco isn’t the killer. Blasco was the bait.”

Lucas turned towards her. His face, lit from below by the flashlight, looked like a tragic theater mask, accentuating the dark circles under his eyes and the terror in his brown eyes.

“We have to wake the others,” he said. “Right now.”

But before they could take a step towards the exit, a noise in the hallway froze them. It wasn't the wind. It was the creak of a floorboard. Someone had been listening. Someone breathing with the calm of one who has just completed a masterpiece.

Elena looked at the bled-out body, Blasco’s “inocence” written in red on his shirt. The truth hadn't freed them; it had trapped them in a tomb of ice with an invisible predator.

Panic, that creature that had been clawing at the walls of her mind since they arrived, finally broke down the door and flooded everything.

“Close the door,” Elena ordered, her voice broken by the certainty of disaster. “Close it, Lucas. We don’t know who’s on the other side.”

## Chapter 22: La Traición de Lucas

```json
{
  "translated_text": "# CHAPTER 22: Lucas's Betrayal\n\n**Aigüestortes Refuge, Catalan Pyrenees**\n**December 24, 2018, 6:15 a.m.**\n\nThe silence that follows the discovery of a corpse is not the absence of sound, but a high-pitched, almost electric frequency that settles at the base of the skull. Elena Vives walked down the second-floor hallway, moving away from room 204, feeling that vibration crawl up her spine. She left behind the coppery scent of spilled blood, that metallic, sickly-sweet fragrance that clung to the palate like a layer of rancid grease, but the image of Javier Blasco, bound and his throat cut with a butcher's precision, remained seared into her retinas with the fidelity of a forensic photograph.\n\nBeside her, Lucas Riera’s footsteps were uneven. The police chief dragged his right leg slightly, a rhythmic sound—*clack, drag, clack*—that echoed against the polished wooden floor. At another time, that defect in his gait would have evoked compassion; now, it only stirred a clinical suspicion within her, cold and sharp.\n\nThey reached a bend in the hallway, a sort of blind alcove meant for storing linen, far from the curious ears of guests who were sleeping, or pretending to, downstairs. The only light came from a small window through which the bluish, sickly light of dawn filtered, tinged by the storm that continued to rage outside, oblivious to human tragedy, burying the valley under tons of white indifference.\n\nElena stopped dead in her tracks and spun on her heels. The movement was so abrupt that Lucas had to stop short, bracing a large, calloused hand against the wall to keep from losing his balance.\n\n“Shut up,” she ordered before he could utter a word. Her voice was nothing more than a whisper, but it carried the authority of someone who had interrogated monsters and knew the architecture of lies.\n\nLucas blinked, confused. The dark circles under his brown eyes looked like soot craters on a face that had aged ten years in five hours.\n\n“Elena, we have to go downstairs. We have to secure the perimeter and notify—”\n\n“No one comes in and no one goes out, Lucas. We already know that.” Elena took a step towards him, invading his personal space. She needed to see his pupils, to look for the dilation of fear or the contraction of guilt.\n\n“What do you mean?” The officer ran a hand through his black hair, flecked with premature gray at the temples, in a gesture of exasperation.\n\n“Room 204’s door,” she said, enunciating each syllable with surgical slowness. “It was locked. We had to use your master key to get in. But when I checked Blasco’s pockets, there was nothing. Not on the table, not on the floor.”\n\nElena watched Lucas’s Adam’s apple bob as he swallowed. The tension in the small cubicle grew, displacing the air.\n\n“The killer took it,” Lucas ventured, though his voice lacked conviction.\n\n“Correct. The killer locked it from the outside. He turned the bolt, secured the door, and left with the trophy.” Elena fixed her moss-green eyes on his, searching for a crack. “But to lock it from the outside, you need a key. And in this refuge, there are only three sets: the guest’s, which has disappeared; the caretakers’, which hangs at the reception; and the master.”\n\nShe paused, letting the implication float between them like the vapor of their breaths in the frigid hallway air.\n\n“You have the master key, Lucas.”\n\nThe man recoiled instinctively, his back hitting a shelf full of clean towels. The scent of industrial lavender, so domestic and safe, felt obscene in that context.\n\n“What are you implying?” he asked, his tone oscillating between offense and panic.\n\n“I’m not implying. I’m analyzing variables.” Elena crossed her arms over her chest, protecting her vital organs, a habit acquired after years of coexisting with human predation. “Blasco died hours ago. The blood was coagulated, his liver temperature had dropped. It happened during the night. While we all slept. Or while we were supposed to be sleeping.”\n\n“I was in my room,” Lucas defended, but his hands began to tremble. It wasn’t a subtle tremor; it was a nervous shaking he tried to hide by thrusting his fists into the pockets of his worn corduroy trousers.\n\n“Show it to me,” Elena demanded, extending her left hand, the crescent-shaped scar gleaming pale under the spectral light from the window.\n\n“What?”\n\n“The master key. Take it out. Now.”\n\nLucas looked at her, and in that gaze, Elena saw the police chief crumble, the protector of the valley, the man who had maintained his composure when the snow isolated them. What remained before her was an eroded human being, full of cracks through which dignity seeped away.\n\n“I can’t,” he whispered.\n\nElena’s stomach clenched, an acidic nausea that had nothing to do with the corpse upstairs and everything to do with betrayal.\n\n“Why can’t you, Lucas?”\n\nHe took his right hand out of his pocket. It was empty. Then his left. Also empty. He patted his coat, his trousers, with a theatrical desperation that Elena found pathetic. But it wasn’t theater. It was the confirmation of a catastrophe.\n\n“It’s not there,” he confessed, his voice broken. “I had it last night. I swear on my life, Elena. I left it on the nightstand, next to my weapon.”\n\nElena felt the ground beneath her hiking boots lose its solidity. The only alliance she had allowed herself to trust, the sole pillar of authority in that white hell, had just turned to sand.\n\n“Are you telling me you lost the only item that guarantees access to every room in this refuge with a killer on the loose?”\n\n“I didn’t lose it,” he retorted, and suddenly shame gave way to defensive anger. “They took it from me. Someone entered my room while I was sleeping and took it.”\n\nElena let out a dry laugh, devoid of humor.\n\n“They entered your room? You, a trained officer, with the light sleep of someone who’s been patrolling for twenty years, and you heard nothing? Not the doorknob turning, not footsteps, not the breathing of a stranger a foot and a half from your bed?”\n\nLucas lowered his head. His breathing became heavy, noisy. He seemed to be fighting an internal battle, torn between maintaining a facade of steel that was already rusted or letting the truth, however corrosive, come to light.\n\n“I don’t have light sleep, Elena. Not lately.”\n\nHe reached into the inner pocket of his jacket and pulled out an orange plastic vial, the kind dispensed in pharmacies with a strict prescription. He held it in the palm of his hand, offering it as incriminating evidence. The sound of the pills rattling against the sides of the container sounded like a warning rattle.\n\nElena took the vial. She read the label. The chemical name was complex, but she knew the drug family. Dopaminergic agonists. Potent sedatives. Medication for severe neurological disorders.\n\nShe looked up, re-evaluating everything she thought she knew about the man before her. The limp. The chronic fatigue. The large hands he sometimes hid under the table.\n\n“What is this?” she asked, though her analytical mind was already connecting the dots, drawing a straight line between the symptom and the diagnosis.\n\n“I have early-onset Parkinson’s,” Lucas said. The word left his mouth like a stone swallowed years ago. “Early degeneration. The doctors say it’s progressing fast.”\n\nElena’s world tilted. The confession didn’t evoke pity in her. It evoked vertigo.\n\n“I took the nighttime dose,” he continued, speaking disjointedly, as if by shedding the ballast he could avoid shipwreck. “It knocks me out. It’s like my brain gets switched off. I don’t dream, I don’t move. If they’d fired a cannon in the hallway, I wouldn’t have woken up.”\n\nElena squeezed the vial until the plastic creaked. Rage, hot and liquid, began to pump through her veins, replacing fear.\n\n“You hid this from me.”\n\n“I didn’t want you to treat me like an invalid. I’m the authority here. If they knew I was sick...”\n\n“This isn’t about your pride, damn it!” Elena’s shout echoed off the narrow walls, startling both of them. “It’s about safety! We’re trapped with someone who slits people’s throats like livestock. I trusted you because I thought you were capable of watching, of reacting. And it turns out you drug yourself unconscious every night.”\n\nLucas tried to approach, to place a conciliatory hand on her shoulder, but Elena recoiled as if physical contact were contagious.\n\n“Elena, please. I’m still me. I can still shoot, I can still think...”\n\n“No,” she cut him off, relentless. “Last night, while you slept your chemical sleep, someone entered your room. Someone who knew you wouldn’t wake up. They took the key from your nightstand, walked to Javier’s room, tortured him, killed him, locked the door with your key, and probably either put it back or kept it. And you didn’t notice a thing.”\n\nThe raw truth of the account struck Lucas. He leaned against the wall, sliding down a few centimeters, defeated by the profiler’s crushing logic.\n\n“I’m a hindrance,” he murmured.\n\n“You’re a risk,” Elena corrected. The cruelty was necessary; survival didn’t understand euphemisms. “And the worst part isn’t the illness, Lucas. It’s the lie. You’ve allowed me to operate based on false data. I thought I had a partner.”\n\n“You do,” he insisted, looking up, his eyes moist with despair.\n\n“No. I have a sick man who has lost control of his own safety and, by extension, of mine.”\n\nElena tossed him the vial of pills. Lucas caught it in the air with a clumsy reflex, nearly dropping it.\n\n“Who else knows?” she asked, reverting to her professional, icy tone.\n\n“No one. Only my doctor in Vielha.”\n\n“The killer knows,” Elena declared. She turned towards the window, watching the snow pile up on the sill, layer upon layer, obscuring the world. “Whoever it is, they knew you’d be out of commission. They knew they could enter your room and take the key without risk. That means they’ve been watching you. You. Your trembling hands. Your pills.”\n\nA shiver that had nothing to do with the temperature ran down Lucas’s spine. The idea of having been studied, analyzed, and dismissed as a threat while he believed he was in control was humiliating.\n\n“What do we do now?” he asked, relinquishing command without complaint.\n\nElena looked at the scar on her palm. She recalled the previous case, the one that had forced her retirement. The mistake of trusting that variables were static. Nature was chaos, and human beings, a part of it. She had made the mistake of looking for order in Lucas, and chaos had devoured it.\n\n“You don’t do anything,” she said without looking at him. “You’re going downstairs, you’re going to sit in the common room, and you’re going to watch the others. If you can even keep your eyes open.”\n\n“Elena...”\n\n“I’m going to find that key. And I’m going to find out what else you’ve hidden from us.”\n\n“I haven’t hidden anything else from you.”\n\nElena turned to look at him one last time. The distance between them in that narrow hallway was barely two meters, but emotionally, an insurmountable chasm had opened, a deep and dark glacial crevice. The alliance had fractured. They were no longer “us.” It was “me” and “him.”\n\nElena walked past him, brushing his shoulder with deliberate indifference, and headed towards the stairs, leaving him alone in the hallway’s gloom, with his vial of pills in his hand and the weight of a corpse and a betrayal on his burdened shoulders. The sound of her receding footsteps was the only thing that broke the electric hum of the silence.\n\nLucas stood there, static, as the storm outside battered the windows, demanding entry. He looked at his hands. They trembled. And for the first time in years, he didn’t know if it was from the illness or from the pure and absolute terror of knowing himself vulnerable.",

  "translated_text": "# CHAPTER 22: Lucas's Betrayal\n\n**Aigüestortes Refuge, Catalan Pyrenees**\n**December 24, 2018, 6:15 a.m.**\n\nThe silence that follows the discovery of a corpse is not the absence of sound, but a high-pitched, almost electric frequency that settles at the base of the skull. Elena Vives walked down the second-floor hallway, moving away from room 204, feeling that vibration crawl up her spine. She left behind the coppery scent of spilled blood, that metallic, sickly-sweet fragrance that clung to the palate like a layer of rancid grease, but the image of Javier Blasco, bound and his throat cut with a butcher's precision, remained seared into her retinas with the fidelity of a forensic photograph.\n\nBeside her, Lucas Riera’s footsteps were uneven. The police chief dragged his right leg slightly, a rhythmic sound—*clack, drag, clack*—that echoed against the polished wooden floor. At another time, that defect in his gait would have evoked compassion; now, it only stirred a clinical suspicion within her, cold and sharp.\n\nThey reached a bend in the hallway, a sort of blind alcove meant for storing linen, far from the curious ears of guests who were sleeping, or pretending to, downstairs. The only light came from a small window through which the bluish, sickly light of dawn filtered, tinged by the storm that continued to rage outside, oblivious to human tragedy, burying the valley under tons of white indifference.\n\nElena stopped dead in her tracks and spun on her heels. The movement was so abrupt that Lucas had to stop short, bracing a large, calloused hand against the wall to keep from losing his balance.\n\n“Shut up,” she ordered before he could utter a word. Her voice was nothing more than a whisper, but it carried the authority of someone who had interrogated monsters and knew the architecture of lies.\n\nLucas blinked, confused. The dark circles under his brown eyes looked like soot craters on a face that had aged ten years in five hours.\n\n“Elena, we have to go downstairs. We have to secure the perimeter and notify—”\n\n“No one comes in and no one goes out, Lucas. We already know that.” Elena took a step towards him, invading his personal space. She needed to see his pupils, to look for the dilation of fear or the contraction of guilt.\n\n“What do you mean?” The officer ran a hand through his black hair, flecked with premature gray at the temples, in a gesture of exasperation.\n\n“Room 204’s door,” she said, enunciating each syllable with surgical slowness. “It was locked. We had to use your master key to get in. But when I checked Blasco’s pockets, there was nothing. Not on the table, not on the floor.”\n\nElena watched Lucas’s Adam’s apple bob as he swallowed. The tension in the small cubicle grew, displacing the air.\n\n“The killer took it,” Lucas ventured, though his voice lacked conviction.\n\n“Correct. The killer locked it from the outside. He turned the bolt, secured the door, and left with the trophy.” Elena fixed her moss-green eyes on his, searching for a crack. “But to lock it from the outside, you need a key. And in this refuge, there are only three sets: the guest’s, which has disappeared; the caretakers’, which hangs at the reception; and the master.”\n\nShe paused, letting the implication float between them like the vapor of their breaths in the frigid hallway air.\n\n“You have the master key, Lucas.”\n\nThe man recoiled instinctively, his back hitting a shelf full of clean towels. The scent of industrial lavender, so domestic and safe, felt obscene in that context.\n\n“What are you implying?” he asked, his tone oscillating between offense and panic.\n\n“I’m not implying. I’m analyzing variables.” Elena crossed her arms over her chest, protecting her vital organs, a habit acquired after years of coexisting with human predation. “Blasco died hours ago. The blood was coagulated, his liver temperature had dropped. It happened during the night. While we all slept. Or while we were supposed to be sleeping.”\n\n“I was in my room,” Lucas defended, but his hands began to tremble. It wasn’t a subtle tremor; it was a nervous shaking he tried to hide by thrusting his fists into the pockets of his worn corduroy trousers.\n\n“Show it to me,” Elena demanded, extending her left hand, the crescent-shaped scar gleaming pale under the spectral light from the window.\n\n“What?”\n\n“The master key. Take it out. Now.”\n\nLucas looked at her, and in that gaze, Elena saw the police chief crumble, the protector of the valley, the man who had maintained his composure when the snow isolated them. What remained before her was an eroded human being, full of cracks through which dignity seeped away.\n\n“I can’t,” he whispered.\n\nElena’s stomach clenched, an acidic nausea that had nothing to do with the corpse upstairs and everything to do with betrayal.\n\n“Why can’t you, Lucas?”\n\nHe took his right hand out of his pocket. It was empty. Then his left. Also empty. He patted his coat, his trousers, with a theatrical desperation that Elena found pathetic. But it wasn’t theater. It was the confirmation of a catastrophe.\n\n“It’s not there,” he confessed, his voice broken. “I had it last night. I swear on my life, Elena. I left it on the nightstand, next to my weapon.”\n\nElena felt the ground beneath her hiking boots lose its solidity. The only alliance she had allowed herself to trust, the sole pillar of authority in that white hell, had just turned to sand.\n\n“Are you telling me you lost the only item that guarantees access to every room in this refuge with a killer on the loose?”\n\n“I didn’t lose it,” he retorted, and suddenly shame gave way to defensive anger. “They took it from me. Someone entered my room while I was sleeping and took it.”\n\nElena let out a dry laugh, devoid of humor.\n\n“They entered your room? You, a trained officer, with the light sleep of someone who’s been patrolling for twenty years, and you heard nothing? Not the doorknob turning, not footsteps, not the breathing of a stranger a foot and a half from your bed?”\n\nLucas lowered his head. His breathing became heavy, noisy. He seemed to be fighting an internal battle, torn between maintaining a facade of steel that was already rusted or letting the truth, however corrosive, come to light.\n\n“I don’t have light sleep, Elena. Not lately.”\n\nHe reached into the inner pocket of his jacket and pulled out an orange plastic vial, the kind dispensed in pharmacies with a strict prescription. He held it in the palm of his hand, offering it as incriminating evidence. The sound of the pills rattling against the sides of the container sounded like a warning rattle.\n\nElena took the vial. She read the label. The chemical name was complex, but she knew the drug family. Dopaminergic agonists. Potent sedatives. Medication for severe neurological disorders.\n\nShe looked up, re-evaluating everything she thought she knew about the man before her. The limp. The chronic fatigue. The large hands he sometimes hid under the table.\n\n“What is this?” she asked, though her analytical mind was already connecting the dots, drawing a straight line between the symptom and the diagnosis.\n\n“I have early-onset Parkinson’s,” Lucas said. The word left his mouth like a stone swallowed years ago. “Early degeneration. The doctors say it’s progressing fast.”\n\nElena’s world tilted. The confession didn’t evoke pity in her. It evoked vertigo.\n\n“I took the nighttime dose,” he continued, speaking disjointedly, as if by shedding the ballast he could avoid shipwreck. “It knocks me out. It’s like my brain gets switched off. I don’t dream, I don’t move. If they’d fired a cannon in the hallway, I wouldn’t have woken up.”\n\nElena squeezed the vial until the plastic creaked. Rage, hot and liquid, began to pump through her veins, replacing fear.\n\n“You hid this from me.”\n\n“I didn’t want you to treat me like an invalid. I’m the authority here. If they knew I was sick...”\n\n“This isn’t about your pride, damn it!” Elena’s shout echoed off the narrow walls, startling both of them. “It’s about safety! We’re trapped with someone who slits people’s throats like livestock. I trusted you because I thought you were capable of watching, of reacting. And it turns out you drug yourself unconscious every night.”\n\nLucas tried to approach, to place a conciliatory hand on her shoulder, but Elena recoiled as if physical contact were contagious.\n\n“Elena, please. I’m still me. I can still shoot, I can still think...”\n\n“No,” she cut him off, relentless. “Last night, while you slept your chemical sleep, someone entered your room. And you didn’t notice a thing.”\n\nThe raw truth of the account struck Lucas. ## Chapter 23: Termodinámica del Miedo

**Aigüestortes, December 24, 2018**
**Boiler Room**
**12:15 p.m.**

The descent into hell didn't smell of sulfur, but of stale diesel and stagnant damp.

Elena Vives left the second floor behind, carrying the weight of Lucas's betrayal as if it were a backpack full of rocks. Each metal step descending into the hotel's sub-basement echoed hollowly, a solitary percussion marking her distance from the rest of the group. The air grew dense and oppressive, a gelatinous mass that made breathing difficult. The disappointment in the officer had formed a hard crust around her emotions, a necessary shield for what her analytical mind called "execution mode."

At the end of the service stairs, a red-painted steel fire door stood ajar, waiting for her. As she crossed the threshold, the temperature plummeted. It wasn't the clean, crisp cold of the mountains; it was an industrial, dirty chill that clung to her skin beneath the layers of thermal clothing.

"Mrs. Vives?" The voice echoed off the concrete walls.

Ramón, the maintenance supervisor, emerged from the shadows of an immense cylindrical structure. He was a fifty-year-old man with perpetually grease-stained hands and blue overalls two sizes too big for him. He held a monkey wrench with the same reverence a soldier holds his rifle.

"You told me to come down if I found anything strange," the man said, wiping cold sweat from his forehead with the back of his wrist. His voice trembled, not from the cold, but with that instinctive vibration animals feel before a storm.

Elena stepped forward, illuminating with her flashlight the labyrinth of pipes that crisscrossed the ceiling like the exposed veins of a metal giant.

"What have you seen, Ramón?"

The mechanic pointed to the main boiler's control panel. The machine, a cast-iron behemoth that should have been roaring to heat the building, remained in sepulchral silence, as dead as the corpse they had left upstairs in the snow.

"It's not a malfunction," Ramón whispered, approaching the panel cautiously. "I tried purging the system, thinking it was an ice blockage. But look at this."

Elena leaned closer. She followed the man's index finger, which pointed to a series of brass valves at the base of the unit.

"The safety valves didn't trip. Someone bypassed them. And here..." Ramón illuminated a section of the intake pipe. "This isn't wear and tear. There are tool marks. Someone injected liquid nitrogen into the water circuit before blocking the flow."

Elena felt a knot in her stomach. The implication was terrifying in its precision.

"Sabotage?"

"Worse," the mechanic corrected, turning to her with wide eyes. "It's a technical execution. Whoever did this knew exactly where to strike so that the system wouldn't just fail, but would be beyond repair. They turned the hotel into a sealed freezer. They didn't want us to feel cold; they wanted to freeze us."

The revelation hit Elena with the force of a physical certainty. It wasn't chaos; it was design. The killer wasn't improvising. He was sculpting the environment to suit his work.

"Can it be fixed?" she asked, though she already suspected the answer.

Ramón shook his head and turned towards the back of the boiler, where the darkness was thickest.

"I need to see if the damage reached the heat exchanger. If I'm lucky, maybe I can rig a manual bypass, but..."

The man moved deeper into the gap between the machine and the brick wall. Elena stood still, analyzing the geometry of the space. The place had too many blind spots, a profusion of shadows where the flashlight's beam died without return.

A sharp, dry metallic sound clacked in the gloom.

"Ramón?"

"I've got it!" he exclaimed from the darkness. "There's an auxiliary lever, it looks like they left it..."

The sentence cut short.

There was no scream. Only a wet, visceral sound, like a boot sinking into mud, immediately followed by the clang of a heavy tool hitting the floor.

"Ramón?" Elena repeated, taking a step forward. Her right hand instinctively reached for the scalpel in her pocket.

The silence that followed was absolute, but it lasted barely a second. Then, the mechanic's body was flung from behind the boiler. He didn't walk; he fell. He collapsed at Elena's feet with the inert heaviness of dead flesh.

Her flashlight's beam swept over the body. Ramón's eyes were open, fixed on nothingness, and a steel rod—likely part of the boiler's mechanism itself, sharpened like a stiletto—pierced his throat from side to side. Blood gushed out, black under the artificial light, soaking his blue overalls.

Elena recoiled, her heart pounding against her ribs like a trapped bird.

A figure was silhouetted in the darkness, right where Ramón had been.

She didn't see its face. She only saw a silhouette shrouded in dark clothing, camouflaged against the basement's blackness, and the fleeting gleam of eyes that reflected her flashlight's beam with the indifference of an arctic predator.

The smell reached her then. It wasn't diesel.

Mint. And something chemical. Antiseptic.

The killer hadn't fled. He had been waiting for her. Using the mechanic as bait to lure her into the bowels of the building.

The figure took a step towards her. The movement was fluid, without urgency, with the terrifying calm of someone who controls time and space.

Elena didn't think. Her body, trained by trauma and survival, took command.

She spun on her heels and ran.

"Elena!" a voice whispered behind her. It wasn't a cry of threat, but an invitation. A taunt.

She lunged towards the pipe-lined corridor they had come from, but a metallic crash stopped her. An enormous stopcock, thrown with inhuman force, smashed against the wall inches from her head, showering sparks and blocking her direct route to the door.

She changed direction. She plunged into the labyrinth of auxiliary machines, towards the generator area.

She extinguished her flashlight.

Darkness instantly swallowed her. It was like going suddenly blind, but it was her only advantage. If she couldn't see, neither could he.

She moved by touch, hands extended, grazing cold metal and rough walls. Her boots slipped on oil slicks. Every breath was a thunderous roar in her own ears.

*Crack.*

A step. Behind her. Slow. Deliberate.

Elena ducked, sliding under a low pipe. She felt a sharp pain in her right forearm as a rusty iron scraped her skin through her jacket, but she bit back a scream. The pain was information; it told her she was still alive.

She crawled forward, feeling the warm blood trickle down her wrist, mixing with the grime on the floor. The mint smell seemed to hang in the air, drawing closer, defying the physics of ventilation.

She reached a fork. To the left, the distant hum of generators. To the right, a narrow corridor that smelled of dust and oblivion.

She chose the right. She straightened up and ran, scraping her shoulders against the narrow walls.

Her fingers fumbled for the doorknob at the end of the corridor. It was locked.

Panic, that animal she had kept at bay, clawed at the door of her consciousness.

She heard the sound of something dragging across the floor a few meters away. Metal against concrete. The killer wasn't running. He was herding her.

Elena forced the handle, pushing with her injured shoulder. The wood groaned, resisting.

"There's no escape, Elena," the voice said, much closer this time. Right behind her.

With a grunt of effort, she charged the door one last time. The rotten frame gave way. Elena fell forward, rolling onto a cold tiled floor, and kicked the door shut just as a gloved hand tried to slip through the opening.

She scrambled to her feet, gasping, and looked for something, anything, to barricade the access. She shoved an old metal filing cabinet against the wooden leaf.

She leaned against it, trembling, her arm throbbing to the rhythm of her racing blood. On the other side of the door, there was only silence. No banging, no attempts to force it open.

Only a soft laugh, barely a breath of air, before the sound of footsteps receded, rhythmic and calm, back into the darkness of the boiler room.

## Chapter 24: Ratas en el Muro

**Aigüestortes. Day 5. Afternoon.**

The world had narrowed to a sixty-centimeter-wide galvanized steel duct.

Elena Vives crawled forward on her elbows, transformed into a blind animal creeping through the guts of a metal beast. The darkness was not merely the absence of light; it was a physical, heavy entity, a leaden blanket that clung to her skin and seeped into her nostrils with a rancid smell of dust accumulated over decades and industrial grease.

Every movement was a negotiation with pain. The crescent-shaped scar on her left palm throbbed against the cold rivets of the floor, sending electric shocks up to her shoulder. Her unprotected knees struck against the duct's seams with a dull, cruel rhythm. *Clack. Drag. Gasp. Clack.*

She didn't know how long she'd been in there. The concept of time had dissolved into the blackness, leaving only the primitive urgency of escape. Behind her lay confinement; ahead, an uncertain promise of freedom or suffocation.

The glaciologist tried to focus her mind, that tool she used to dissect criminal profiles with surgical precision, but the environment conspired against logic. The air was stale, hot, and thin. She felt her lungs having to work double to extract the necessary oxygen, a mechanical task that was beginning to fail. Claustrophobia, that old enemy she kept at bay with open spaces and icy mountains, clawed at the back door of her consciousness.

“It’s just a tunnel. It has an exit. Every ventilation system obeys a logical architectural design.”

She repeated the technical lie like a mantra. But her body, wiser and more terrified, knew she was buried alive.

She paused for an instant to wipe away the sweat stinging her eyes. The silence of the duct was broken by a distant sound. It wasn't the wind, nor the settling of the building. It was voices.

The sound traveled through the metal with treacherous acoustic clarity, bouncing off the tin walls and amplifying as if inside a macabre cello. Elena held her breath, ignoring the burning in her chest. She crawled two meters further, to where a rectangular grate cut through the darkness below with sickly yellow stripes of light.

The light drew bars on her soot-streaked face. She peered out cautiously, pressing her ear to the cold metal.

“...that’s not what we agreed on.”

The voice chilled her blood more than any mountain blizzard. It was unmistakable. Grave, paternal, tinged with that medical authority that had so often comforted her.

Doctor Arnald Soler.

Elena felt the sheet metal floor vanish beneath her stomach. Arnald? Here? Her mind struggled to find an innocent explanation, but the doctor’s tone exuded a clandestine urgency that didn’t fit with any routine round.

“The process has accelerated,” another voice replied. This one wasn't human, or at least it didn't sound like it. It was a metallic whisper, devoid of inflection, as if the words were cut with a scalpel before being spoken. She couldn't see the speaker, only the elongated shadow it cast on the white tiles of what appeared to be a storage room.

“There are no more vials!” Soler’s voice broke, losing composure. “If the storm doesn’t abate, the generators will fail. And if they fail, the temperature will rise. You know what that means. They’ll rot. All your 'art' will turn into decomposed flesh.”

“Beauty requires sacrifice, Doctor. You know that better than anyone.”

“Don’t talk to me about sacrifice!” Arnald hit something, perhaps a metal table. The clang resonated through the duct, rattling Elena’s teeth. “I’m doing it for her. Only for her. If it weren’t for Julia...”

Elena bit her lower lip until she tasted the coppery tang of blood. *Julia.* Arnald’s daughter’s name. The missing piece of the puzzle, the leverage. The image of the benevolent doctor, her mentor, the man who had offered her refuge when her police career crumbled, shattered into a thousand unrecognizable pieces.

He wasn’t an ally. He was a pawn. Or worse, an architect of horror.

“Julia is safe as long as you fulfill your function,” the faceless voice said. There was a nuance in that tone, a sadistic satisfaction that reeked of antiseptic and madness. “Keep the bodies cold, Doctor Soler. And keep the profiler away from the truth. She is... astute. It would be a shame to have to add her to the collection prematurely.”

“Elena knows nothing,” Soler lied, and his voice trembled. “Keep her out of this.”

“That depends on you. And on the storm.”

Footsteps were heard, the creak of a heavy door opening and closing, and then, silence reclaimed the room below. Only Soler’s ragged breathing remained, as he seemed to sob in solitude.

Elena wanted to move, wanted to keep crawling away from that poisonous truth, but her body rebelled.

The attack came without warning, brutal and absolute.

The duct walls seemed to suddenly contract, as if the metal throat decided to swallow her. The air became solid. Elena opened her mouth to take a breath, but nothing came in. Her throat closed, a noose tightened by invisible hands.

“I can’t get out. I’m going to die here. Like her.”

The memory of her last case, the victim she couldn’t save, who died suffocated in a crawlspace while she analyzed clues kilometers away, superimposed itself on reality. She was no longer in the ducts of the Aigüestortes hospital. She was in that wooden box underground. The darkness was the same. The smell of fear was the same.

Her heart, a runaway war drum, pounded against her ribs with such violence that she feared they would break. Cold sweat drenched her back, mixing with the grime. Her limbs went numb, a painful tingling that heralded the paralysis of panic.

She wanted to scream, but the sound died in her larynx. If she screamed, Soler would hear her. And if Soler heard her, the man with the icy voice would return.

She was trapped between death by suffocation and death at the hands of a monster.

Her vision filled with black spots that danced like carrion flies. She felt gravity invert, the duct spinning on itself. She clawed at the metal with her nails, breaking them, searching for an exit that logic told her existed but terror concealed.

“Breathe. Elena, breathe.”

She couldn’t. The weight of the entire mountain was on her chest.

Amidst the sensory chaos, a rhythmic sound, tiny but constant, broke through the roar of her own blood in her ears.

*Tick. Tock. Tick. Tock.*

Her left wrist was pressed against the floor, near her ear. Her father’s watch. That old analog mechanism that had survived time, death, and oblivion. The hands, coated with a luminescent material that barely glowed anymore in the gloom, kept moving forward. Indifferent to fear. Indifferent to Soler’s betrayal. Indifferent to death.

One second. Another. Yet another.

Time didn’t stop. Order existed in that mechanism of gears and springs. If the watch could keep working in the darkness, so could she.

Elena closed her eyes and focused on that sound. She visualized the gears turning, tooth by tooth, with mathematical precision. She let the rhythm of the watch replace the chaotic rhythm of her heart.

*Inhale.* The air entered, painful and thin, but it entered.
*Exhale.* The fear left, not all of it, but enough to make space for survival.

The glacier doesn’t panic when pressure increases; it compacts, becomes harder, bluer, stronger. She had to be ice.

She opened her eyes. The darkness was still there, but it was no longer a monster; it was merely an absence of photons. The walls were still narrow, but they were just physical limits, not a coffin.

“Move,” she whispered, her hoarse voice sounding strange in the tube.

She forced her cramped muscles to respond. One knee forward. An elbow. Again. The pain was information: she was alive.

She crawled away from the grate where Soler wept for his sin. She advanced five meters, 10, 15. The duct took a ninety-degree turn to the right. She felt a current of fresher air, a faint thread that smelled of bleach and clean laundry.

At the end of the section, another grate. This one wasn’t screwed in as firmly. The light entering was white, sterile.

Elena turned onto her back with difficulty, braced her boots against the grate, and pushed. The metal creaked, resisting. She gritted her teeth and unleashed all the frustration, all the anger over Arnald’s betrayal, in a single kick.

The grate gave way with a metallic crash that echoed like a gunshot.

Elena dropped down.

The impact against the tiled floor was hard. Pain exploded in her hip and shoulder, but it was the pain of freedom. She rolled onto herself, coughing, spitting out the gray dust that coated her tongue.

She lay there for a moment, looking at the white, immaculate ceiling. She was covered in soot, sweat, and dried blood. She looked like a specter risen from the underworld to stain the asepsis of that place.

She slowly got up, checking for broken bones. She was in the hospital laundry room. Large white sheets hung like ghosts on the shelves. The hum of an industrial dryer masked the sound of her fall.

She was free from the wall. But the prison was much larger than she imagined.

She looked at her trembling hands. Black with grime.

Arnald Soler, the man who had taught her to trust again, was collaborating with the killer to save his daughter Julia. The enemy wasn't just outside, in the storm, lurking in the snow. The enemy had keys, a white coat, and access to every room.

Elena got to her feet, swaying. She wiped her face with her sleeve, leaving a dirty streak on her cheek. Her green eyes, now framed in black, gleamed with a cold and terrible determination.

She was no longer the victim trapped in the duct. Now she was the witness. And ice, when it breaks, cuts.

## Chapter 25: Punto de Congelación

The corridor of the scientific station's north wing stretched like a concrete throat, swallowed by the darkness the storm imposed from outside. The auxiliary generator coughed somewhere in the basement, sending spasms of yellowish light that made shadows dance on the flaking walls. The cold wasn't just a temperature; it was a physical presence, an entity that bit at the skin through layers of thermal clothing.

Elena Vives moved along the wall, her breath held deep in her lungs. Her left hand clutched the grip of the unlit tactical flashlight, while her father's watch, enormous on her slender wrist, ticked off the seconds with a sound that seemed deafening in the sepulchral silence.

“Twenty meters to the generator room,” Lucas Riera whispered. His voice was deep, a vibration Elena felt more than heard.

The police officer limped slightly. The humidity and the barometric drop from the cyclogenesis punished his old injury, forcing him to drag his right leg with a syncopated rhythm: step, drag, step. He walked ahead, his broad silhouette outlined against the intermittent emergency lights, acting as a human shield Elena hadn't asked for but, at that moment, was grateful for with an acidic guilt in the pit of her stomach.

An aseptic, chemical smell pierced the air, stale with dust and humidity: mint and industrial disinfectant.

Elena stopped dead. Her moss-green eyes dilated, catching the detail before her brain processed the threat.

Too late.

A shadow detached itself from the darkness of a side niche. It wasn't a patch of light, but a solid figure, dressed in a fisherman's slicker that gleamed wetly under the electric flicker. The face didn't exist; there was only a modified welding mask, smooth and featureless.

In its hands, a red-handled fire axe was already describing a downward trajectory.

Time didn't stop, but rather accelerated its entropy.

“Get back!” Lucas roared.

The police chief tried to pivot, to interpose his body between the steel and the Profiler. He sought support from his right leg to push himself back, an instinctive defensive movement trained over years. But his knee, stiff from the cold and chronic pain, did not respond to the neural command.

The joint failed. A dry crack echoed as Lucas's support collapsed.

Instead of dodging the blow or blocking it with his forearm, Lucas slipped, falling heavily onto one knee. The change in height was catastrophic. The axe, originally aimed at his collarbone or neck, found a new target.

The tempered steel blade descended with the force of gravity and human malice.

The sound was atrocious. There was no metallic clang of a movie fight, but the wet, dense, and definitive sound of flesh violently separating. The blade bit deeply into Lucas's right thigh, tearing through the tactical pants, slicing the vastus medialis, and seeking the femur.

“AAAGH!”

Lucas’s cry tore through the corridor’s atmosphere, an animalistic shriek that froze Elena’s blood.

The masked figure, with mechanical efficiency, pulled the handle back to free the weapon. The extraction was worse than the impact. A gush of dark blood, almost black in the gloom, spurted from his injured limb, splattering the linoleum floor and Elena’s boots.

Lucas collapsed onto his side, his large, calloused hands flying instinctively to the wound, trying to contain the uncontainable. Blood flowed between his fingers with terrifying pressure.

The masked figure raised the axe again. The movement was deliberate, curatorial, like an artist examining where to apply the next brushstroke of red.

Elena reacted. There was no conscious thought, only the activation of muscle memory buried under layers of trauma. The paralyzing fear that usually haunted her transformed into cold fuel.

She lunged forward, not toward the aggressor, but toward a fire extinguisher hanging on the opposite wall. Her fingers closed around the cold metal lever. With a twist of her hip, she ripped the cylinder from its bracket and, leveraging the inertia, threw it with all her might at the figure in the slicker.

The red metal tank struck the aggressor in the chest, making him stagger and recoil two steps. The axe hit the floor, sparking, though he didn't let go of it.

“Come on!” Elena cried, her voice unrecognizable even to herself.

She grabbed Lucas by the lapel of his jacket. The man weighed a ton, dead weight anchored by pain and shock. He looked at her, his brown eyes wide with terror, pupils constricted by agony.

“Leave me…” he gasped, his complexion turning grayish. “Run.”

“Shut up and move,” she ordered.

Elena pulled. Adrenaline lent her a strength her slender muscles shouldn’t possess. Lucas, understanding she wouldn't leave, dug the heel of his good leg into the floor and pushed. They dragged themselves meter by meter, leaving a wide, glistening trail on the dirty floor.

The man in the slicker recovered, straightening up with the slowness of an inevitable nightmare.

Elena saw a metal door to her left: INFIRMARY.

“There!” she indicated.

They lunged against the door leaf. It was open. They tumbled inside, into a space that smelled of alcohol and old bandages. Elena kicked the door shut just as the shadow of the axe fell across the threshold.

She turned the lock and pushed a heavy metal filing cabinet against the entrance. A second later, a brutal blow shook the door, denting the metal from the outside. Then another. And silence.

Elena turned. Lucas lay in the center of the room, his back against a gurney. His breathing was wheezing, ragged. The puddle beneath his right leg grew with an obscene swiftness, devouring the white floor.

She knelt beside him. The wound was an open mouth in his thigh, a deep laceration that exposed muscular tissue and pulsed with every beat of Lucas's heart. It wasn't an injury a bandage could fix. Life was escaping him through that breach.

“Elena…” he murmured, his eyelids heavy, his crimson-stained hand seeking hers. “It’s cold.”

Elena looked around, her green eyes scanning the infirmary with the precision of a computer searching for data. Bandages, sutures… useless with this flow. She needed to stop the bleeding now.

Her gaze fell on a Bunsen burner on the laboratory table and a surgical metal spatula. Then, she looked back at the open wound, the torn flesh that wouldn't stop bleeding.

She understood what she had to do. Bile rose in her throat, but she swallowed it down.

“I’m sorry, Lucas,” she whispered, as she stood up to prepare the fire. “This is going to be hell.”

## Chapter 26: Sangre y Hielo

The infirmary hallway reeked of damp and neglect, but at that instant, the dominant smell was the hot copper of arterial blood.

Elena Vives dragged Lucas Riera’s dead weight. Her boots slid on the worn linoleum, leaving a scarlet trail that shimmered under the dying flicker of the emergency fluorescents. He was a large man, broad-shouldered and heavily built, a burden that strained every fiber of her muscles.

"Hold on!" Elena snarled, kicking open the double doors of the treatment room.

Lucas let out a guttural moan, a wet sound that escaped between his clenched teeth. His face, usually weathered by the mountain sun, was now the color of old wax. Cold sweat beaded on his forehead, mixing with the premature gray at his temples.

She let him drop onto the examination gurney with less gentleness than she would have liked. The police chief clung to the metal edges, his knuckles white, his brown eyes glassy and unfocused, searching the ceiling.

Elena focused on the source of the horror. It wasn't his shoulder, as she had feared in the confusion of the attack. The reality was worse.

His uniform trousers were shredded at the right thigh. The dark fabric was soaked, heavy, stuck to the skin by the viscosity of the vital fluid that gushed out in rhythmic spurts. Elena recalled with surgical clarity the preceding seconds in the corridor: the Sculptor's shadow emerging, the firefighter's axe whistling through the air. Lucas had tried to retreat, but his old injury, that cursed limp that the weather brought on, had betrayed him. His knee had given way and the steel, instead of finding air, had bitten deeply into his vastus medialis.

"Elena..." he whispered. His voice was a thread of sand.

She did not respond. Her analytical mind, trained to dissect the psychology of monsters, shifted gears. Now there was no profile to trace, only fluid mechanics and emergency anatomy.

She searched for scissors on the instrument table. They were rusted, useless. She cursed under her breath and pulled out her own tactical knife. With a sharp motion, she tore the fabric from knee to groin.

The wound opened before her eyes like an obscene mouth. The cut was clean at the edges, but deep, reaching dangerously close to the femoral artery. The blood wouldn't stop gushing, dark and fast, stealing Lucas's life with each beat of his heart.

"Press here," Elena ordered, guiding Lucas's large, calloused hands over the source of the bleeding. "Don't let go. If you let go, you die."

Elena stepped back and began rummaging through the metal and glass cabinets. The clatter of clashing bottles echoed in the small room, competing with the howl of the storm that battered the armored windows outside. The *White Beast* roared with hurricane force, isolating them from the world, from help, from hope.

Gauze. Bandages. A half-empty bottle of ethyl alcohol. No sutures. No anesthetic. No modern hemostatics.

"There's no... not enough pressure," Lucas gasped. His head fell back. The red stain beneath his hands expanded inexorably.

Elena looked at her father's watch on her left wrist. The second hand advanced with cruel indifference. At this rate, Lucas would go into irreversible hypovolemic shock in less than five minutes.

Her gaze swept the room in desperation until it stopped on a Bunsen burner connected to a gas outlet on the adjoining laboratory counter. Beside it, a long-handled surgical steel spatula.

The idea made her nauseous, a churning in her stomach she had to swallow back. It was an atrocity. A medieval butchery.

But it was the only option.

She ran towards the burner, turned the valve, and lit the flame. The blue flame hissed, hungry. Elena took the spatula and placed the flat blade over the tongue of fire.

"Lucas, listen to me," she said, returning to his side. She leaned over him, forcing him to look at her. Her moss-green eyes locked onto his brown ones, trying to anchor him to consciousness. "I'm going to have to close that up. Now."

He turned his head, saw the metal heating in the background, changing from silver to a menacing cherry red. He understood instantly.

"Do it," he said, closing his eyes tightly.

"It's going to hurt. More than you can imagine."

"Better than being dead," he murmured, though a solitary tear escaped his closed eyelid, getting lost in the lines of pain on his face.

Elena searched for something he could bite on. She found a roll of elastic bandage and put it in his mouth.

"Bite down. Don't scream if you can help it. We don't want him to come back."

She was referring to the Sculptor. The predator was still loose in the building, lurking in the shadows, waiting for the moment to claim his artwork.

Elena returned to the burner. The metal was incandescent, radiating a heat that dried her eyes. She wrapped her hand with a cloth to avoid burning herself on the conductive handle and took the spatula.

She walked towards the gurney. Each step was a sentence. The air in the room seemed to have solidified.

"On the count of three," she lied.

She abruptly pushed Lucas's bloodied hands away and, without giving his brain time to process the cruelty of the act, pressed the glowing red steel directly onto the open wound on his thigh.

The sound was the worst. A sharp hiss, like meat thrown into a boiling pan.

Immediately after, the smell. The acrid, dense stench of burning human flesh, mixed with the metallic sweetness of cooked blood and singed hair. It was a primitive aroma that awakened ancestral terrors in the reptilian brain.

Lucas arched on the gurney as if struck by lightning. His muscles convulsed violently. The scream was muffled by the gag, turning into a dull, vibrating howl that seemed to shake the walls of the infirmary. The veins in his neck swelled like ropes about to burst.

Elena maintained the pressure. Tears blurred her vision, but her hand didn't tremble. She had to cauterize every vessel, seal the torn flesh with fire.

"I'm sorry, I'm sorry, I'm sorry," she whispered, a feverish litany he couldn't hear.

It was ten eternal seconds. Ten seconds in which time ceased to exist, and only absolute pain and the smell of death remained.

When she finally removed the metal, now black and smoking, the skin around the wound was puckered, blackened, and hideous, but the blood had stopped flowing. The red channel had become an artificial, grotesque scab.

Lucas collapsed onto the thin mattress. His chest rose and fell in irregular spasms, desperately searching for air. He was unconscious, or on the verge of it. The pain had surpassed the threshold of what the mind could process.

Elena dropped the tool to the floor with a metallic clang that broke the silence. She leaned against the edge of the gurney, fighting the dizziness that threatened to bring her down too.

She looked at her hands. They were covered in Lucas's blood. Dry, dark stains under her fingernails, fresh splatters on her forearms. The crescent-shaped scar on her left palm seemed to glow, a reminder of old violences.

With mechanical movements, she found a thermal blanket in the cabinets and covered the trembling body of the policeman. She checked his pulse at the carotid; it was fast, thready, but steady. He had survived.

Elena slid down the wall to sit on the cold floor, knees drawn to her chest. The adrenaline began to recede, leaving in its place an icy void and a leaden weariness.

Outside, the storm battered the building with renewed fury, as if the *White Beast* was frustrated at not having claimed a life that night. But Elena knew the night was long. The Sculptor was still out there, patient, meticulous. And now, Lucas was dead weight. She was the only barrier between him and eternal ice.

She looked towards the barricaded door. Her watch read three in the morning. Hours remained until dawn, and many more until the storm abated.

No one would come. They were alone in the white hell.

Elena closed her eyes for a moment, visualizing the building's map in her mind, tracing routes, calculating angles. The analyst was back. She had to be ready. Because when Lucas's pain subsided, or when the killer decided to finish his work, she would have to be colder than winter itself.

## Chapter 27: El rastro de la Presa

```markdown
# CHAPTER 27: The Prey's Trail

**AIGÜESTORTES SANATORIUM, DECEMBER 25, 2018**

The smell of cauterized flesh clung to Elena Vives’s palate, a greasy, bitter film that not even the frigid air of the corridor could dissipate. She left behind the infirmary’s barricade, where Lucas’s rhythmic, heavy breathing marked the only beat of life in that concrete mausoleum. He would survive. That certainty was an anchor, but also a burden; now, every step she took into the darkness was not just a flight, but a hunt.

The flashlight in her right hand cast a trembling cone of light that cut through the blackness like a scalpel opening necrotic tissue. She ascended to the third floor. Her hiking boots, stained with soot and dried blood, landed on the tiles with an unnatural softness, learned not in the mountains, but during years of studying predatory behavior. To catch the monster, she had to move like him: without sound, without mercy, without fear. Or at least, pretending that fear wasn't a parasite devouring her stomach.

The building groaned. The beams contracted under the thermal pressure of the storm outside, emitting clicks that echoed like distant gunshots. Elena stopped at the foot of the service staircase leading to the upper levels, the forbidden ones, those not found on the valley’s tourist maps.

She glanced at her wristwach. Her father’s watch face, with its scratched crystal, showed ten o’clock in the morning, but daylight was a distant memory, drowned by the perpetual snowfall that sealed the windows. Inside, time had fractured.

She took the first step towards the fourth level and stopped dead.

Something in the air’s texture had changed. It wasn’t a sound, but an absence of suspended dust at a specific point in the beam of light. She narrowed her eyes, those moss-green irises that had analyzed hundreds of crime scenes, searching for the anomaly in the pattern.

There it was.

A fishing line, translucent and taut, crossed the third step at shin height. It was almost invisible, a ghostly trace awaiting an unwary leg.

Elena held her breath. She followed the fishing line’s path with her gaze, without touching it. The line ascended the banister, coiled into an improvised pulley system of rust and malice, and ended at the ceiling, supporting a block of masonry torn from the wall, bristling with twisted iron and broken glass. A guillotine of rubble.

“Primitive,” she whispered, and her own voice sounded strange, metallic to her.

It wasn't the work of an unreachable genius, but that of an engineer of pain. The Sculptor didn't just want to kill; he wanted to punish the intrusion.

With surgical precision, Elena lifted her left leg, clearing the deadly thread. Her balance wavered for an instant on her right leg, feeling the fatigue in her muscles, but she stabilized herself before placing her sole on the other side of the trap. She repeated the movement with her other limb.

Turning, she observed the mechanism from above. That trap confirmed her theory: he was upstairs. Nobody protects a path that leads nowhere. The predator had retreated to his highest lair.

She continued her ascent. The temperature dropped with each step. The mist from her mouth grew denser, a white cloud preceding her advance. The cold was no longer an environmental nuisance, but a physical assault, a bite on the exposed skin of her face.

Reaching the landing of the top floor, before the access to the clock tower, her flashlight beam swept across the rotten wooden floor.

A flash of color, incongruous in that world of grays and blacks, caught her attention.

She knelt, feeling the scar on her left palm throb, a phantom reminder of old wounds. On the floor, overturned on its side, lay a high-heeled shoe. It was made of fine, elegant leather, in a burgundy shade she had seen days earlier at an official reception.

The mayor’s shoe.

Elena shone her light on it closely. The heel was intact, but the sole showed deep grooves, longitudinal scratches. It hadn’t fallen off while walking. It had been dragged.

The revelation struck her with the force of a hammer blow.

“He doesn't hide them in the basement,” she murmured, and understanding chilled her blood more than the storm. “He elevates them.”

The Sculptor’s logic crystallized in her mind with terrifying clarity. Cold preserves. Ice stops putrefaction. He didn't want his works to rot in the dampness of the foundations; he sought the absolute purity of eternal freezing. And the coldest place in the sanatorium, the point where the wind lashed mercilessly and the temperature dropped below what was humanly bearable, was the clock tower. The attic.

She stood up slowly, leaving the shoe where it was. It was a breadcrumb in a forest of wolves.

Ahead of her, at the end of the corridor, stood a double-leaf door, massive, reinforced with black iron bands. The wood was covered in frost on the inside, proof that on the other side the climate did not obey the laws of central heating, but the tyranny of winter.

The wind howled through the cracks, a funereal chant inviting her in.

Elena advanced. The thirst for revenge that had propelled her out of the infirmary had transformed into something denser, more calculated. It was no longer fire; it was ice. She squeezed the handle of the scalpel she kept in her thermal jacket pocket until her knuckles turned white.

She reached the door.

There were no more visible traps. Only the wood, the iron, and the sepulchral silence waiting behind. She knew that crossing that threshold meant no return. There, logic ended and the artist’s delirium began.

She extended her hand towards the frozen doorknob. The metal burned her skin, a final warning from the physical world before entering the realm of madness.

She turned the handle.
```

## Chapter 28: El Tiempo Detenido

**Aigüestortes, December 25, 2018**
**Clock Tower**

The wind howled beyond the wood, an impatient beast clawing to get in, but the real cold didn't come from the storm. It resided within, crouched in the belfry's gloom. Elena pushed the oak door with her shoulder, keeping the scalpel held high, a trembling extension of her own will.

The screech of the rusted hinges tore through the silence like a choked cry.

No one attacked. No shadow lunged at her. Only dust, suspended in grayish beams of light filtering through the shutter cracks, danced with hypnotic slowness. The clock mechanism dominated the space: a tangle of immense, toothed bronze and iron gears, clattering with a rhythmic cadence, indifferent to human life. *Tick.
