Tock.* The mechanical heartbeat of the village.

Elena took a step. The plank floor groaned beneath her hiking boots. The air smelled of industrial oil, old wood, and something more. A pungent, clean scent, contrasting with the grime of the surroundings. Menthol. Antiseptic. The olfactory signature of the operating room.

She swung her flashlight beam towards the center of the room, where the ceiling beams converged into a dark apex.

Her breath froze in her throat, solidifying into a painful mass that prevented her from swallowing.

The mayor wasn't tied to a chair, nor was she discarded on the floor like refuse. She was displayed.

Suspended by a system of pulleys and fine chains that disappeared into the darkness of the upper rafters, the woman floated half a meter off the floor, slowly rotating on her own axis, propelled by air currents. She didn't look like a murder victim; she looked like a ballerina caught in the precise moment of an eternal leap.

Elena forced herself closer, fighting the primal instinct that screamed at her to turn back and flee into the snow. Her analytical mind, that tool she had honed for years to dissect human depravity, tried to take control. *Observe. Categorize. Don't feel.*

The mayor's skin was no longer human. It had taken on a bluish, translucent hue, similar to fine porcelain on the verge of shattering. Hoarfrost covered her completely, a dress of microscopic diamonds glinting under the artificial light of the flashlight. Her hair, blonde and stiff, formed a crown of ice needles around her head, tilted back in feigned ecstasy.

It was grotesque. And, in a sickening way that twisted Elena's guts, it was beautiful.

The Sculptor hadn't committed a crime of passion. He had curated a work of art. He had eliminated the imperfection of life—warmth, movement, chaos—to leave behind only pure form. Cadaveric rigidity had integrated into the pose: arms extended to the sides, palms open towards the ceiling as if begging or receiving an invisible blessing.

Elena lowered the flashlight, scanning the body. The clothes had been removed. The woman's nakedness wasn't vulnerable, but statuesque. There was no visible blood, no bruises, no signs of struggle. Only that absolute stillness, preserved by the attic's sub-zero temperature, which transformed a person into an object of macabre worship.

"My God..." she whispered, and her voice sounded strange, small against the magnitude of that tableau.

She moved close enough to see the victim's chest. It didn't move. Life had extinguished hours ago. The ice had claimed the flesh, sealing the pores, halting decomposition before it could begin. The glaciologist in her recognized the technique: rapid freezing, controlled humidity. The killer understood the thermodynamics of the human body better than many doctors.

Something white protruded from between the mayor's stiff, dead fingers. It wasn't ice.

It was paper.

Elena swallowed, tasting the metallic tang of fear at the back of her tongue. She tucked the scalpel into her anorak pocket, needing both hands for what was coming. Her gloved, clumsy fingers grazed the victim's skin. The touch was like feeling marble pulled from a freezer. The cold burn pierced through the fabric.

She gently pulled the note. The heavy-stock paper, similar to what would be used for a formal invitation, slid out with a dry rustle.

She brought it closer to the light. The calligraphy was impeccable, firm strokes of a fountain pen in black ink, without hesitation or blemish.

"Almost perfect. You are better."

The world tilted.

The words weren't a confession. They were a promise. An invitation. The mayor was just a draft, a dress rehearsal before the definitive creation. And the raw material for that final work had just voluntarily entered the artist's studio.

The impact of the phrase hit Elena with the force of a physical hammer blow to the solar plexus. The analytical barrier, that wall of tempered glass she had built between herself and the horror, shattered into a thousand pieces.

Biological reality superseded the intellectual.

That menthol scent became unbearable, mingling with the imagined stench of her own future death. She felt a violent contraction in her stomach, a spasm that climbed burning up her esophagus like acidic lava.

She doubled over at the waist, dropping the note.

Violent retching seized her. Elena vomited onto the old wooden floor, expelling bitter bile and her meager breakfast, emptying herself of everything except panic. The sound of her own retching echoed in the belfry, competing with the clock's relentless *tick-tock*.

She coughed, spitting a string of saliva, and wiped her mouth with the back of her hand, trembling uncontrollably. Her eyes, swimming with tears of exertion, fixed again on the suspended figure. The mayor was still there, pivoting imperceptibly, watching her with those open, glassy, empty eyes, as if welcoming her to the museum.

She was alone.
The killer wasn't there, but his gaze filled everything.
And she was the next piece for the collection.

## Chapter 29: Decisiones en Frío

CHAPTER 29: Cold Decisions

Aigüestortes Spa, Catalan Pyrenees
Day 6. 4:45 PM

The outside world had ceased to exist. Beyond the reinforced glass of the infirmary, reality had shrunk to a monochrome concept: white. A voracious, absolute white, devouring the contours of the fir trees and blurring the line where sky should meet earth. The storm wasn't falling; it was pushing. It was a physical beast ramming against the hotel's stone walls, searching for a crack to slip through and reclaim what remained inside.

Elena Vives tore her gaze away from that aggressive nothingness and turned to the bed.

The silence in the room smelled of iodine and stale hopelessness. Lucas Riera sat on the edge of the mattress, his right leg stretched out at a rigid, unnatural angle. The bandage, clean just hours ago, already displayed the dark bloom of a fresh bloodstain, a geographical map of the pain he tried to conceal by clenching his jaw until the muscles in his neck tightened like violin strings about to snap.

"I know where he is," Elena said.

Her voice sounded strange in her own ears, devoid of vibration, as if the words were stones she was dropping into a dry well. There was no triumph in the revelation, only the fatality of a terminal diagnosis.

Lucas looked up. His dark eyes, usually warm, were now two tunnels of weariness. The gray hairs at his temples seemed to have multiplied in the last twenty-four hours, a personal snowfall mimicking the one outside.

"The caves on the north slope," he deduced. It wasn't a question. It was the confirmation of a police officer who knew his territory better than the lines on his own hands. "The old natural ice houses."

"The temperature there is constant. Perfect for preserving... materials." Elena avoided the word *bodies*. Her analyst's mind was already there, visualizing the scene, anticipating the arrangement of horrors the Sculptor would have prepared. Her father's watch, large and heavy on her left wrist, ticked off the seconds with a *tick-tock* that resonated in her bones, reminding her that time was not a renewable resource.

"I'm coming with you."

The phrase left Lucas's mouth with the speed of a reflex, but it lacked foundation.

"No, Lucas."

"I'm not letting you go out there alone. That bastard is waiting for you. It's a trap, Elena. All of this..."—he made a vague gesture encompassing the hotel, the storm, the accumulated bodies—"this is his stage. And you're the lead actress."

"Exactly. That's why I have to go."

Lucas growled, an animal sound born of the deepest frustration, the kind that corrodes the dignity of men accustomed to being others' shields. He pressed his calloused palms against the edge of the mattress and pushed his body upwards.

It was an act of rebellion against biology.

Elena didn't move to help him. She knew he wouldn't forgive it. She watched, with a clinical coldness that stung her chest, as the mechanics of the human body failed.

Lucas managed to stand for an instant. One glorious, pathetic second. Then, he shifted his weight to his right leg.

There was no scream. The sound was worse: a choked gasp, air escaping his lungs as if he'd been punched in the solar plexus. His knee, that complex hinge of tendons and bone, collapsed under gravity. His leg didn't support him; it buckled like a rotten branch.

He fell heavily. The thud against the linoleum floor sounded like defeat.

Elena closed her eyes for an instant, feeling the impact in her own nervous system, a sympathetic jolt of phantom pain. When she opened them, Lucas was on the floor, pounding the floorboards with a clenched fist. One, two, three times. His knuckles turned white.

"Shit," he whispered, his voice broken by humiliation. "Shit, shit!"

Elena approached and knelt beside him. She didn't touch him. Not yet. She respected the sacred space of his helplessness.

"Your leg won't hold up in the snow, Lucas. There's more than a meter out there. You need strength, balance. Right now, you have neither."

He looked at her. There were tears of contained rage in the corners of his eyes.

"If you go alone, he'll kill you. He's stronger. He knows the terrain."

"I know his mind," she retorted, and for the first time, she allowed the arrogance of her old life, the confidence of the profiler who had hunted monsters in air-conditioned offices, to seep into her voice. "I know how he thinks. I know what he needs to complete his work. His narcissism is his blind spot. He expects me to come scared, like a victim. He doesn't expect me to come as an equal."

She stood up, leaving Lucas on the floor, and headed towards the supply cabinet where they had stacked the equipment recovered from the reception area.

The ritual began.

It wasn't dressing; it was armoring herself. Each garment was a layer of insulation not only against the cold, but against fear.

First, the thermal clothing, clinging to her skin like a synthetic second skin. Then, the thick wool sweater, rough, smelling of mothballs. The waterproof pants hissed as she zipped them up, a sharp, definitive sound.

She pulled on her hiking boots. She laced them tightly, constricting her instep until foot and sole were a single entity. Each knot was a promise of no return. Finally, the red anorak. The color of blood, of warning. She pulled up the collar and felt her peripheral vision narrow. Only what was in front existed now.

When she turned, Lucas had managed to drag himself back to the bed. He sat, pale, with cold sweat beading on his forehead despite the low temperature of the room.

"You look like..."—Lucas searched for the word, his gaze tracing her figure—"You look like a soldier."

"It is a war, isn't it?"

Elena approached the bed. The space between them, once filled with unspoken words and professional tensions, had become charged with a different electricity. The imminence of the end has a way of distilling human relationships, evaporating the superfluous and leaving only the pure residue of truth.

Lucas reached under the pillow. The movement was slow, solemn. He pulled out his service weapon. A Glock 19, black, matte, utilitarian. An instrument of death designed with Austrian engineering precision.

He held it in the palm of his enormous hand, offering it to her as one would deliver a sacred relic.

"Take off the safety," he said, his voice hoarse. "Aim for center mass. Don't hesitate. If you see a shadow, shoot. If you hear a sound that isn't the wind, shoot."

Elena took the weapon. The metal was warm from having been under his head. It was heavy. Heavier than she remembered from her shooting practice at the academy, years ago. It was heavy with the responsibility of survival. She checked the magazine with a dry, mechanical motion. Full.

"I will."

She put the pistol in the deep pocket of her anorak, next to the flashlight and the knife. Her right hand brushed the grip, memorizing its rough texture.

Silence fell. The wind howled outside, rattling the window frames, reminding them that time was up.

Lucas reached out and took her hand. His skin was rough, warm. Elena's, cold as the ice she was about to face. He gently pulled her closer. Elena didn't resist. The gravity between them had shifted its axis.

"Elena," he said. Her name sounded different on his lips, stripped of ranks and formalities. "Promise me one thing."

"What?"

"That you won't let him turn you into one of his statues. That if you can't win..."

He didn't finish the sentence. There was no need. The plea hung in the air, brutal and honest. *Don't let him capture you alive.*

"I promise."

Their faces were inches apart. She could see the map of wrinkles around Lucas's eyes, the small scar on his chin, the furious pulse beating in his neck. He leaned in and kissed her.

It wasn't a gentle kiss. There was no romantic movie tenderness. It was a collision. It was desperate, tasting of blood from a bitten lip, of stale coffee and fear. It was the kiss of two people on the edge of an abyss, trying to anchor themselves to each other before the fall became inevitable. It lasted a second and it lasted an eternity. When they pulled apart, both were breathless.

Elena broke away abruptly, severing the physical bond before the temptation to stay, to hide under the blankets and wait for the end together, could paralyze her. The 'paralysis of analysis' was her old enemy, but today there was no room for thought, only for kinetic action.

"Wait for me awake," she ordered, her voice firm, reclaiming her mask.

"Always," he replied. A white lie. With that leg, with infection lurking, he might not wake up if he closed his eyes.

Elena walked towards the infirmary door that led to the side corridor, the service exit connecting to the outside. She placed her hand on the doorknob. The metal was icy, burning her fingertips even through the thin gloves she wore beneath the thick ones.

She looked back one last time. Lucas Riera, Aigüestortes' chief of police, the man who had maintained order in the valley, was now a broken figure on white sheets, a fallen giant watching her with the devotion of one who sees their only hope depart.

"Lock up as soon as I'm out," she said.

"Good luck, Elena."

She turned the knob. Pushed.

The wind didn't enter; it invaded. A blast of icy air, laden with microscopic ice needles, struck her face like a physical slap, stealing her breath instantly. The roar of the storm drowned out any other sound.

Elena Vives stepped forward, crossing the threshold. The door clicked shut behind her with a metallic snap, leaving her alone in the white belly of the storm. There was no turning back now. Ariadne's thread had been cut.

## Chapter 30: El Reino Blanco

**Valle de Boí, Catalan Pyrenees**
**December 26, 2018**

The world had ceased to be a place of shapes and contours, transforming instead into an absolute concept: white.

There was no sky, no earth, no horizon. Only a furious, milky mass swallowing reality whole. Upon leaving the shelter, the transition hadn't been gradual; it was like crossing the threshold of an airlock into the void outside. The wind wasn't blowing; it was pushing with the solidity of a moving concrete wall, an invisible giant trying to knock me down with every step.

I adjusted my blizzard goggles, but the snow embedded itself in the glass with the violence of sand blasted under pressure. Beneath layers of thermal clothing and a technical anorak, my body was a small boiler struggling to maintain its inner fire against an ocean of entropy. Lucas's service pistol, heavy and cold against my hip, felt like a ridiculous talisman. What was I going to shoot at? The tempest? Winter itself?

Nature needed no weapons to kill; its indifference was enough.

I moved forward. Or so I believed. The notion of distance had evaporated along with visibility. My boots sank calf-deep into the virgin snow, and each time I lifted a leg, the effort burned in my quadriceps like boiling lactic acid. The roar of the air was so deafening that, paradoxically, it created an absolute mental silence. There was no room for analytical fear, only for the mechanics of survival: lift, move forward, step. Lift, move forward, step.

I remembered the topographical map I'd memorized at the shelter's kitchen table. The entrance to the caves in the northern sector should have been less than two kilometers away. On a summer day, a thirty-minute walk. Today, it was an odyssey across a hostile planet.

A side gust of wind, crueler than the previous ones, made me lose my balance. I fell to my knees. The impact wasn't hard; the snow cushioned it, but the cold took advantage of that momentary pause to seep through the seams of my clothing. I felt its icy fingers caressing the nape of my neck, seeking a path to my spine.

“Get up,” I ordered myself, but my voice was lost before it escaped my scarf.

When I looked up, the landmark I'd been following—a cluster of black fir trees standing like deformed sentinels to my right—had vanished.

I turned my head. Nothing. Only that thick, swirling white curtain, blurring the surroundings a meter from my nose. Panic, that creature I'd kept at bay with logic and police procedure, began to claw at the walls of my stomach. Without visual references, human balance fails. The inner ear becomes confused. I could be walking in circles, or worse, heading towards one of the precipices that flanked the invisible path.

I looked at my father's watch on my wrist. The hands advanced with an insulting normalcy. Forty minutes had passed. I should have arrived. Or, at least, I should have seen the rock formations that preceded the entrance.

Doubt settled in my lungs, heavier than the freezing air. I pulled off a glove with my teeth—an immediate tactical error—to take out the compass from my pocket. The needle danced erratically, as if the mountain's magnetism were drunk.

“North,” I whispered, watching my breath crystalize instantly.

But north was merely an abstract concept when the wind struck you from all directions. I put my glove back on, but my fingers were already numb, clumsy as wet pieces of wood. Hypothermia doesn't strike suddenly; it seduces you. It convinces you that you're tired, that sitting down for a moment isn't such a bad idea.

I kept walking, guided solely by a blind obstinacy and the image of Lucas, wounded and helpless in the infirmary. His limp, his beaten-dog look when I took his weapon. He trusted that I was stronger than this.

Then I saw it.

About ten meters away, a dark silhouette stood out against the infinite whiteness.

I stopped, my heart thudding against my ribs like a trapped bird. My hand instinctively went to the pistol grip beneath my coat. The figure was motionless, tall, with slumped shoulders.

“Lucas?” I yelled, knowing it was impossible. He couldn't walk.

The figure didn't respond. The wind whipped what appeared to be a cloak or a long coat. I took a step, squinting my moss-green eyes, searching for definition in that blur. It wasn't Lucas. The posture was rigid, unnatural.

It was the girl. The victim of the failed case. The one I'd let die by not seeing the signs in time. She stood there, in the snow, pointing into the void with an accusing arm.

I squeezed my eyes shut and shook my head, feeling the sharp ache of cold in my cheekbones. “Hypoxia. You're hallucinating. Your brain seeks patterns where there's only chaos.” When I opened them again, the figure had disintegrated. It wasn't a woman, nor a ghost. It was a dry tree trunk, split by some ancient lightning, protruding from the snow like a broken rib of the mountain.

Disappointment and relief mingled in a bitter cocktail. There was no one. I was alone on that immense snowy canvas. But that hallucination had served a purpose: the trunk marked the forest's edge. If my mental map wasn't deceiving me, the rock face had to be right behind it.

I advanced towards the dead tree, using it as a buoy in the wreckage. Upon reaching it, I touched the rough wood. It was real. Solid. I leaned against it, panting, and looked beyond.

There it was.

It wasn't a majestic entrance. Barely an irregular crack at the base of a gray granite cliff, an open wound in the stone that the snow hadn't quite managed to cauterize. The darkness emanating from within was absolute, a violent contrast to the blinding luminosity outside. It looked like a mouth waiting to be fed.

The physical relief was so intense it almost made me vomit. I dragged myself the last few meters, battling against the snow that had accumulated at the entrance, forming a treacherous dune. My legs no longer responded fluidly; they were dead weights I had to drag with the strength of my hips.

I let myself fall inside, rolling onto the cold, dry stone.

The change was instantaneous. The roar of the wind died down, becoming a distant, muffled howl, as if someone had closed a heavy door. The air inside was still. Not warm, not even temperate, but the absence of the biting wind felt like an embrace.

I stayed on the ground for a moment, my cheek pressed against the dusty rock. It smelled of ancient dampness, of minerals, and something more… a subtle metallic scent that made the hairs on my neck stand on end.

I switched on the flashlight with trembling hands. The beam of light cut through the gloom, revealing limestone walls that sweated dampness. I was inside.

I sat up, hugging my knees to conserve residual heat. I was frozen to the bone. My teeth chattered with a rhythm I couldn't control. But I was alive. I had vanquished the storm, the White Beast guarding the valley.

Now I had to face what dwelled within.

I gazed into the blackness that stretched beyond the circle of my flashlight's beam. The cave's silence was dense, expectant. It wasn't an empty refuge. It felt inhabited, watched.

I drew Lucas's pistol from its holster, released the safety with my numb thumb, and aimed at nothing.

“I'm here,” I whispered into the void, and my voice bounced off the walls, distorted, returning an echo that sounded like a threat.

## Chapter 31: La Galería

North Sector Caves, Valle de Arán
December 26, night

The silence weighed more heavily than the snow.

Elena moved forward, dragging her numb feet across the bare rock. Behind her, the roar of the white beast, that storm scratching at the mountain's entrance like a frustrated predator that had lost its prey, had faded. Inside, the stillness was not peace, but a malevolent expectation. A waiting.

She switched on her flashlight. The beam of light cut through the dense darkness, revealing dust particles dancing in the stagnant air like suicidal moths. Her fingers, stiff inside her technical gloves, closed around the grip of Lucas's pistol. The metal offered a cold comfort, a promise of defense that felt useless against the magnitude of the blackness swallowing the tunnel.

She walked.

Each step echoed with a damp acoustics, amplified by the limestone walls that seemed to close in on her shoulders. Claustrophobia, that old acquaintance nestled in her chest since the Minnesota case, awoke. Not as a scream, but as hydraulic pressure at the base of her throat. She breathed through her mouth, trying to fool the panic, but the air tasted wrong.

It didn't smell of dampness, nor of wet earth, nor of rancid bat guano.

It smelled of mint. And beneath that chemical, aggressive, antiseptic freshness, the cloying stench of flesh, when it ceases to be life and becomes mere matter, crawled.

The tunnel suddenly widened. The ceiling rose until it was lost in the upper shadows, and the temperature dropped sharply, not with the violence of the outside wind, but with the absolute stillness of an industrial cold storage unit. The vapor of her breath froze before her eyes before dissipating.

Elena swept the space with the cone of light.

She stopped dead. Her boots scraped against the floor, which was no longer irregular rock, but had been leveled, polished with manic dedication.

"My God!" she whispered. Her voice had no echo; the ice absorbed everything.

She wasn't in a cavern. She was in a cathedral.

On both sides of a central aisle, arranged with sickening symmetry, rectangular blocks stood tall. Translucent monoliths, two meters high. They gleamed under the flashlight's beam with a bluish, almost radioactive glow.

She forced herself to approach the first one in the left row. Fear tightened the muscles in her neck like steel cables about to snap.

She wiped the frost from the surface with her coat sleeve. Her heart hammered against her ribs, a frantic drum threatening to betray her position. She raised the light.

Inside the ice, suspended in eternal weightlessness, was a hand.

Only a hand.

It was preserved with an obscene perfection. Fingers extended in a gesture of supplication or reach, the skin pale, the nails with a purplish hue beneath the layer of clear polish. The cut at the wrist was surgical, clean, without tears. There was no blood. The ice had cauterized the history, halting decomposition at the exact second beauty met death.

Elena stumbled back, tripping over her own feet. The light swung violently, illuminating the rest of the gallery.

It wasn't the only piece. There were dozens.

She walked down the aisle, bile burning her esophagus. The next block contained a female torso, nude, marmoreal, with the ribcage opened and ribs separated by silver retractors, exposing lungs that would never breathe again. Beyond that, a face. A young man, with wide, blue eyes, injected with a solution that preserved the sparkle of life long after the soul had fled.

It was a museum.

A gallery of atrocities where the artistic medium was not oil or marble, but human biology halted in time. The Sculptor didn't kill for sadistic pleasure; he killed for curation. He sought the preservation of the instant, the Pyrrhic victory over entropy. Each block was either a failure or a success in his quest for physical immortality.

The impact of the vision made her legs falter. Reality fractured. This was not the work of a common serial killer; it was the labor of years, perhaps decades, of technical refinement. The logistics required to maintain that temperature, to transport the bodies, to create the molds... the sheer magnitude of the operation crushed her more than the mountain above her.

She reached the end of the main hall.

There, the space opened into a sort of natural apse. There was no ice in the center, only a raised stone platform, illuminated by a faint light coming from an opening at the back, suggesting an adjoining room with electrical power.

On the platform stood an empty pedestal.

It was a slab of black granite, polished into a dark mirror. It waited. It demanded.

Elena approached, drawn by a morbid gravity she couldn't resist. The flashlight trembled in her left hand, making the shadows of the stalagmites dance like hungry specters.

On the surface of the pedestal, carved with a chisel and filled with gold leaf, was a metal plaque. Small. Elegant. Definitive.

She read the inscription. The air rushed out of her lungs and refused to re-enter.

"ELENA VIVES
The Final Piece"

Reality swayed beneath her feet. She wasn't an intruder. She wasn't the hunter who had followed breadcrumbs to the witch's house.

She was the commission.

Everything—the call, the clues, the storm, the route that had led her there—had been a design. A macabre choreography orchestrated to bring her to this exact spot, in front of that empty pedestal awaiting her form, her final gesture, her frozen eternity.

Her wrist watch, her father's old analog, marked the seconds with a *tick-tock* that sounded deafening in the sepulchral silence. *Tick*. You're here. *Tock*. It's your turn.

An electric hum broke the trance.

At the back, behind the pedestal, a heavy metal door hissed open hydraulically. A white, clinical, blinding light spilled from within, cutting through the gallery's gloom like a scalpel on taut skin.

Elena raised her weapon towards the light, squinting against the sudden glare. Her pupils painfully contracted.

The laboratory was open.

## Chapter 32: Cara a Cara

Central Laboratory, Aigüestortes Valley Cave System
December 26, 2018

It wasn't the light itself that hurt, but its purity.

After hours spent crawling through galleries where darkness had weight and texture, where shadows clung to the skin like tar, the radiance emanating from the central cavern felt like a physical assault. Elena Vives blinked, feeling reflexive tears bathe her retinas, trying to focus on that impossible scene.

There were no stalactites dripping moisture, nor the geological chaos of limestone. The floor had been leveled with a grayish, industrial resin, upon which stainless steel tables rested, arranged with the precision of an operating room. A generator purred in some hidden corner, a mechanical, constant, and deep throb, replacing the mountain's pulse. But what dominated the air wasn't the ozone from the machinery, but that scent. Menthol. Chemical eucalyptus. An aggressive asepsis that tried, unsuccessfully, to mask the ferrous smell of old blood.

And there he was.

He didn't turn when she crossed the threshold. He remained with his back to her, leaning over a workbench, manipulating an object with a watchmaker's delicacy. He wore an immaculate white coat, which contrasted violently with the surrounding black rock. His ash blonde hair, so pale it seemed to lack pigment, was styled with geometric precision.

Elena raised the pistol. Lucas's service weapon weighed more than she remembered, or perhaps her own muscles had lost the ability to hold reality. The barrel's metal was cold, but her palm sweated against the grip. She aimed at the center of that pristine back.

"Don't move," her voice sounded raw, like sandpaper against her dry throat.

The man stopped his hands, but he didn't tense up. There was no instinctive spasm of someone who knows they are hunted. He simply ceased what he was doing with a calm that chilled the blood more than the cave's sub-zero temperature.

"Punctuality was never your strong suit in college, Elena," he said. His voice was soft, devoid of sharp edges, with a baritone cadence that echoed off the stone walls. "But I suppose the weather complicated the ascent."

He turned slowly.

Elena held her breath, searching for the monster's face. What she found was the most terrifying normalcy. Symmetrical, unremarkable features, the kind the mind forgets five seconds after seeing them in a supermarket line. Only his eyes broke that mask of mediocrity: two pools of ice blue, static, devoid of any spark of humanity. They were shark's eyes, made to observe, not to feel.

The Sculptor.

He wore no visible weapons. He only held in his right hand, gloved in blue latex, a small carving tool, similar to a scalpel, but with a curved blade.

"Drop it," she ordered, taking a step forward to steady her stance. Her hiking boots squeaked on the resin.

He smiled, a minimal gesture that barely curved his thin lips. He placed the instrument on the metallic table with a soft clink. Then, he extended his hands, palms up, in a gesture that wasn't surrender, but welcome. Like a host receiving a late guest at a gala dinner.

"There's no need for so much hostility. You're trembling, and that trigger is sensitive. It would be a shame to spoil the moment with a vulgar accident."

"The moment?" Elena felt a wave of bile rise in her esophagus. She thought of the previous gallery, of the ice blocks, of the faces preserved in eternal agony. "You call that carnage a 'moment'?"

The man shook his head, paternalistic disappointment shadowing his gaze.

"Carnage is what nature does, Elena. Putrefaction. Worms. Oblivion. I offer permanence." He walked around the table, approaching her with measured steps. "Lower the weapon. You haven't come all this way to kill me. You've come to understand."

"I've come to put an end to this."

"If you wanted to shoot, you would have already. You're an analyst, a profiler. Your mind needs to complete the pattern before closing it. You can't destroy the unknown without first solving the equation. It's your nature."

Elena kept the gun's sights aligned with the man's chest. He was right, and that terrified her. Her index finger caressed the trigger guard, hesitating. Fear wasn't paralysis, but a high-frequency vibration that ran through her nerves, warning her that she was facing a predator who used words, not claws.

"If you take one more step, I'll blow your head off," she warned.

The Sculptor stopped three meters away. He casually leaned against one of the tables, crossing his ankles. Behind him, Elena could see what he had been manipulating: a cubic ice block, about thirty centimeters. Inside, suspended in the crystalline transparency, was a human hand. The fingers were extended, graceful, captured in a gesture of supplication or surrender. It didn't look dead; it looked paused.

"Why?" she asked, the word escaping her lips before she could censor it.

"The million-dollar question." The man sighed, observing his work. "People fear death, but what truly terrifies them is deterioration. To see beauty wither, skin wrinkle, identity dissolve into a biological soup." He looked up and fixed his blue eyes on hers. "I stop the clock. I turn the ephemeral into the absolute. Isn't that what every artist seeks?"

"You're a psychopath," Elena spat. The clinical label served as a shield, a way to reduce him to a known, manageable pathology.

"Labels are for the mediocre who need to categorize what surpasses them." He made a vague gesture with his hand, encompassing the laboratory. "You're not like them, Elena. That's why you're here. That's why you are the final piece."

The air in Elena's lungs solidified. The 'Final Piece'. The phrase she had intuited, had feared, now spoken with that casual certainty.

"Have you been watching me?"

"For years." The confession landed like a slab. "Not your daily movements, that's vulgar. I've read your work. Your thesis on the morphology of Pyrenean glaciers. Your articles on isotope preservation in permafrost."

Elena felt the ground sway. It wasn't a physical pursuit; it was an intellectual violation. He hadn't chosen her for her appearance, or by chance. He had entered her mind long before entering her life.

"You understand ice, Elena," he continued, his voice taking on an almost religious fervor. "You know that frozen water is the only true memory of this planet. Rock erodes, wood rots, paper burns. But ice... ice remembers. Every air bubble trapped ten thousand years ago is still there, intact. You seek truth in glaciers. I seek truth in flesh. We are colleagues."

"Don't compare me to you," she growled, gripping the weapon until her knuckles turned white. "I study nature. You torture people."

"I save them," he corrected vehemently, losing his icy composure for a second. "I save them from the ugliness of the end. Think of that girl you found in the forest. If I hadn't intervened, she would now be dust and bones. Thanks to me, she will be perfect forever. Incorruptible."

Elena remembered the first victim's body. The unnatural serenity of her face. The absence of visible violence, save for the fact that she was dead. She felt a deep, intellectual nausea. The man's logic was a labyrinth of broken mirrors, where cruelty disguised itself as pity.

"And now you want me," she said, connecting the dots. "Not just for your collection. But because you think... I appreciate it."

"You are the only one who can understand the magnitude of my work." The Sculptor moved away from the table and took a slow step towards her. "The others... they were blank canvases. Raw material. But you... you are a mirror. Your mind is cold, analytical, precise. Like this place. Like me. To preserve you would not just be to keep a body; it would be to crystallize a kindred intelligence. It would be the culmination of my museum."

Elena backed up one step, colliding with the edge of an empty autopsy table. The frigid steel pressed into her lower back. She was cornered between the man's moral abyss and the metal of his laboratory.

The temptation to shoot was overwhelming. A single movement of her finger and the brain that conceived those horrors would cease to function. But something stopped her. It wasn't compassion. It was the need to know where Lucas was. If she shot now, and Lucas was trapped in one of those capsules, dying of cold, she would never find him in time. The laboratory was a complex of tunnels; she needed information.

"Where is he?" she asked, changing the subject, trying to regain control of the interrogation.

The Sculptor tilted his head, like a curious dog.

"The policeman. The rough and sentimental Chief Riera." He clicked his tongue. "A discordant element. Too... organic. Too chaotic. He doesn't fit the aesthetic."

"Where is he?!" Elena shouted. The echo of her voice bounced off the polished rock walls.

"Close." He gestured vaguely towards a metallic door at the back of the laboratory, shrouded in gloom. "He is experiencing the process. The thermal transition. It's fascinating, you know? The body struggles, shivers, burns its last glucose reserves. It's biology's final dance resisting eternity."

Elena's heart beat against her ribs like a caged bird. 'Thermal transition.' He was freezing. Lucas was dying right now, just a few meters from her.

Urgency replaced fear. She had to get past this man.

Discreetly, with a movement she hoped would be imperceptible under the overhead light, Elena slid her thumb over the gun's safety. It was already off; she'd checked it upon entering, but she needed to feel the mechanism. She readjusted her grip. She couldn't fail. At this distance, a shot to the chest would take him down, but perhaps not kill him instantly if he wore some kind of protection under his pristine coat. The head. It had to be the head.

The Sculptor saw the micro-movement of her hand. His blue eyes shone with predatory intelligence.

"You're going to try," he said, not as a question, but as a statement of fact. "You're going to try to kill me to save a man already marked by decay. He's sick, Elena. You've seen it in his gait, in the tremor of his hands. His own body is betraying him. I'm just accelerating the inevitable."

Sick? Elena's mind flashed to Lucas's limp, the pills he took in secret. The Sculptor knew too much. He had dissected their lives before dissecting their bodies.

"Philosophy class is over," Elena said, tensing her arm muscles. The decision was made. Lucas was worth more than any ethical answer.

But before she could pull the trigger, the sound changed.

The generator's constant hum coughed. The laboratory's white lights flickered once, twice. The intensity dropped, plunging the room into a grayish gloom for a second, before recovering its full power.

That brief instant of electrical instability was enough.

The Sculptor moved. Not with superhuman speed, but with calculated efficiency. He exploited the flicker of light that had distracted Elena for a fraction of a second. He took two long, fluid steps, closing the distance between them.

Elena reacted, but time had strangely stretched and contracted. She raised the weapon, seeking her target, but he was already too close. He didn't hit her. He stopped just at the limit of her personal space, invading her sphere of safety with his scent of mint and death.

She could see the pores of his skin, the almost synthetic texture of his epidermis. She could see that his pupils hadn't dilated even at the imminent threat of the shot.

"Shoot," he whispered. His voice was an intimate, seductive, and terrible hiss. "Shoot and break the silence. But if you do, the location of the freezing chamber dies with me. And your policeman will become an ice statue before you find the key."

Elena froze. Her finger on the trigger trembled, applying pounds of pressure that didn't quite release the firing pin. The gun barrel was centimeters from the chest of his pristine coat.

They were caught in a stalemate. She had the firepower; he held control of the information. And both knew that time was running out for Lucas, tick-tock, tick-tock, like the progressive cooling of a cell.

"You're a monster," she whispered, feeling tears of frustration burn her eyes.

"I am a visionary, Elena." The Sculptor slowly raised a hand and, with a boldness that defied all logic of survival, gently pushed the gun barrel aside, millimeter by millimeter. Elena resisted, but doubt weakened her arms. "And you are about to become my masterpiece."

They were face to face. His breath didn't mist, as if his interior was as cold as the cave. Hers came out in ragged clouds. Intimate enemies, united by a perverse understanding of ice and death, separated only by an impossible decision.

The generator coughed again, louder this time, and the light flickered, casting long, dancing shadows that seemed to applaud the scene.

## Chapter 33: Laberinto de Espejos

**Aigüestortes, December 26, 2018**
**Karstic System Tunnels**

The shot thundered in the stone cavity, a dull blast that found no flesh, but the millennia-old hardness of the rock. The bullet ricocheted, sparking orange flames that died before touching the ground, and the echo multiplied like a geological guffaw, mocking my aim.

The Sculptor didn't flinch. He didn't even blink. With a fluidity that contradicted the violence of the moment, he spun on his heels, his white coat billowing like a spectral shroud, and slipped into the black maw of the east tunnel.

"Stop!" I cried, but my voice sounded fragile, swallowed by the cavern's acoustics.

I couldn't let him go. He held the key, the only one who knew the code. Lucas was turning into an ice statue a few feet away, and every second I hesitated, his cells broke, crystallizing irreversibly. The image of his lungs collapsing spurred me forward, forcing my legs to run after the trail of mint and formaldehyde scent the killer left in his wake.

I exited the makeshift laboratory and ventured into the mountain's gullet.

The change was immediate. If the operating room possessed a clinical, artificial coldness, the tunnels exhaled the damp, rotten breath of the deep earth. The floor was a trap of uneven calcite, slippery from centuries of seepage. My flashlight beam danced chaotically, cutting through the shadows with a trembling ray that barely managed to penetrate the thick air.

I saw him at the end of a bend, a pale blur moving with an irritating grace. He wasn't running; he flowed. He knew every crack, every stalagmite that sprouted from the ground like a shark's tooth waiting to tear an unwary ankle. I, on the other hand, was an intruder, a foreign body the cave tried to expel.

My mountain boots, designed for the snow outside, found no traction in the damp slime of the inner rock. As I tried to gain speed on a sharp turn, my right foot slipped on a patch of subterranean moss.

The world spun.

There was no time to put out my hands. My side violently collided with a sharp stone edge protruding from the wall. The air escaped my lungs in a choked groan. I felt the tearing of thermal clothing and, an instant later, the sharp, hot, liquid burning sensation of my skin splitting open at rib-height.

I lay gasping against the rock, the metallic taste of bile in my mouth. Lucas's pistol, heavy and cold, was still in my right hand, but my fingers had lost sensation. I touched my side with my left; the dampness I collected wasn't water.

I looked up. He had stopped ten meters ahead, just at the threshold where the tunnel widened into a gallery full of natural columns. He wasn't fleeing. He was waiting for me.

A terrible understanding settled in my stomach, heavier than fear: he wasn't running blindly. Every step of his was a calculated choreography. I stumbled over the cave's geography; he, on the other hand, used it as an extension of his body.

I stood up, ignoring the stabbing pain that drilled through my torso with each inhalation. I limped forward, aiming the weapon at his silhouette.

"It's over," I said, trying to steady my aim.

He cocked his head, an almost childish gesture of curiosity. He stood next to a rustic control panel, a rusted fuse box attached to the rock wall, probably installed by the smugglers who used these caves decades ago.

"Elena," his voice was clear, without the panting of physical exertion, "you're trying to apply surface logic in the depths. Down here, sight is a luxury, not a right."

I looked around. The gallery was a forest of stone. Gigantic stalactites hung from the ceiling like spears pointing at my head. I needed cover. If he decided to attack, I was exposed in the middle of the corridor.

I lunged towards a limestone rock column, seeking shelter. In the movement, I struck a thin formation, a young stalactite that broke with a dry crack. The fragment, long and conical like a bone dagger, fell into my free hand. I gripped it tightly. A pistol with few bullets and a sharp stone. These were my tools against a man who had turned death into an aesthetic discipline.

"Where's the key?" I demanded, peeking just an eye from the edge of my refuge.

He smiled. It was a sad, disappointed grimace.

"You still don't understand. It's not about opening doors, but about closing your eyes."

His gloved hand moved towards the electrical panel. It wasn't a quick movement, but deliberate, giving me time to understand what was about to happen, allowing me to savor my helplessness.

"No..." I whispered.

He pulled the lever.

The distant generator's hum died instantly. The work lights hanging from the ceiling sputtered one last time and went out.

Darkness didn't fall; it lunged.

It was an absolute, solid blackness that swallowed my hands, the weapon, the ground, and my very existence. It was a blindness so total that my eyes ached from dilating uselessly, searching for a photon, a flicker, anything. The silence that followed was sepulchral, broken only by the rhythmic dripping of water, which now sounded like a clock counting down the time I had left to live.

I backed away, my spine pressed against the cold column. Panic, that creature I had kept at bay with analysis and logic, scratched at the door of my mind. I was blind, wounded, and completely lost in his labyrinth.

Then, I heard the sound.

A mechanical *click*, followed by a faint, high-pitched hum, almost imperceptible, like an electrical insect.

*Nzzzzzzzzzt.*

The sound came from where he had been. Military technology. Night vision goggles. I had seen it in inventories, in manuals, but I had never felt the weight of its implication until now.

For me, the world had ceased to exist. For him, I had just lit up in phosphorescent green, bright and vulnerable, a radiant prey in the middle of nowhere.

I heard the scuff of a rubber sole on the stone. He was approaching. And I didn't know where to aim.

---

## Author Style Guide

### Javier Castillo

### The Master of Contemporary Psychological Thrillers

Javier Castillo has revolutionized the Spanish suspense landscape with a formula that combines the dizzying pace of American bestsellers with emotional depth and a fragmented puzzle structure. This guide details the pillars of his style.

#### 1. Narrative Architecture: The Temporal Puzzle

Castillo's most recognizable characteristic is the rupture of linearity. His stories are not told from beginning to end but are assembled.

##### A. Multilayered Structure

*   **Intertwined Timelines:** The plot usually divides into at least two or three timeframes (e.g., the distant past of trauma, the recent past of disappearance, and the present of the investigation).
*   **Precise Headings:** Each chapter obligatorily begins with a location and a date.
    *   **Example:** New York, 1998 / Mississippi, 2020.
*   **Puzzle Effect:** Information is dispensed in such a way that a revelation in the past explains strange behavior in the present, keeping the reader in a constant state of "reconstruction."

##### B. Rhythm and the Cliffhanger

*   **Brief Chapters:** Facilitate fast, compulsive reading ("just one more page").
*   **Cliffhanger Endings:** Practically all chapters end with an impactful sentence, an unanswered question, or a revelation that changes the perception of what came before.

#### 2. Tone and Atmosphere

Castillo constructs an atmosphere of melancholic tension. It's not just suspense; it's the weight of accumulated pain.

*   **Textual Cinematography:** Descriptions are conceived as camera shots. The focus is on what the character sees and physically feels (the cold, the racing pulse, the dim light).
*   **Aesthetic Americanization:** Setting his works in the U.S. (especially the East Coast or the deep South) allows him to use archetypes of the classic thriller (the FBI, major newspapers, towns with secrets) that are familiar and universal.
*   **Emotional Darkness:** The environment often reflects the internal state of the characters: rain, basements, dense forests, or empty rooms that emphasize solitude.

#### 3. Language and Stylistic Devices

His prose is direct but sprinkled with very potent and visceral lyrical images.

##### A. Sensory and Abstract Metaphors

Castillo has a unique way of describing feelings. He avoids clichés and seeks physical images for pain or fear.

*   **Device:** Comparing an emotion to a sharp object or a destructive natural phenomenon.
    *   **Example:** "Guilt was a hungry animal nibbling at my nerve endings every time I closed my eyes."

##### B. Diction and Recurring Vocabulary

He uses words that evoke fragility and mystery:

*   Pieces, glass, broken, abyss, darkness, heartbeat, destiny, chance, truth, lie.

##### C. Introspective First Person

He usually gives voice to the protagonist through a very intimate first person. This allows for:

*   **Unreliable Narrator:** The reader only knows what the character remembers or wants to tell.
*   **Emotional Immersion:** Feeling the character's confusion and anxiety as one's own.

#### 4. Character Construction: "Broken People"

In Castillo's universe, no one is "fine." Everyone carries an emotional backpack.

*   **Trauma as a Driving Force:** The central conflict always arises from an unhealed wound from the past.
*   **Professions of Truth:** His protagonists are often journalists (like Miren Triggs), writers, or investigators. People whose social function is to ask questions, which justifies the search for clues.
*   **Moral Duality:** He explores the boundary between good and evil. His "heroes" often commit morally questionable acts to achieve an end.

#### 5. Central Themes

To write with Castillo's hallmark, the plot must pivot on these axes:

*   **Disappearance:** The void left by someone who vanishes without a trace.
*   **Destiny vs. Chance:** The idea that an insignificant decision can destroy an entire life years later.
*   **Family Secrets:** The family as a place of love, but also as the repository of the darkest lies.
*   **Identity:** People who discover they are not who they thought they were.

### Castillo Style Checklist

[ ] Have I started the chapter with a Date and Place?

[ ] Have I ended the chapter with a shocking twist or revelation?

[ ] Are there at least two intersecting timelines?

[ ] Have I used a visceral metaphor to describe fear or sadness?

[ ] Is my protagonist a person "marked" by a past event?

[ ] Is the pace fast enough to keep the reader from stopping?

## Chapter 34: Fuego Frío

The mist of breath froze before touching the ground. It was not a natural cave, but an inverted cathedral carved into the glacier's entrails, where stalactites hung like the fangs of a sleeping beast. The hum of diesel generators, a constant, mechanical heartbeat, desecrated the mountain's millennia-old silence.

Elena Vives stopped dead in her tracks, her boots embedded in the bare rock. Her left hand instinctively sought the cold face of her father's watch on her wrist, a nervous tic that not even therapy had managed to eradicate. Before her, illuminated by surgical spotlights that cast long, violent shadows, rose the cylinders.

There were six of them. Transparent polymer columns filled with a viscous liquid, in which those Aigüestortes had given up for lost floated suspended. They weren't dead, or at least they didn't appear to be; the 'preservation' the psychological profile spoke of was there, manifested in an obscene stillness. Static beauty, free from the decay of time.

“Cold is the only absolute truth, Elena,” the voice echoed from the gloom, bouncing off the bluish ice walls. “It stops entropy. It stops pain.”

The smell came first. A chemical slap of antiseptic mixed with the artificial sweetness of mint. The aroma of Dr. Arnald Soler.

Lucas Riera emerged to her right, his service pistol trembling slightly in his calloused hands. The limp in his right leg was more pronounced than ever; the damp cold of the cavern must have been gnawing at his joints like a rabid dog.

“Hands where I can see them!” Lucas shouted. His voice cracked, devoid of its usual authority, eroded by the horror of seeing familiar faces behind the glass.

Arnald stepped out from behind the last cylinder. He wasn't wearing a lab coat, but an immaculate gray thermal suit and blue nitrile gloves that looked like a corpse's hands. His eyes, shards of ice identical to the environment, showed no fear of the weapon. Only paternal disappointment.

“Lucas, always so predictable,” the doctor whispered, adjusting his gloves. “The disease is eating you from the inside, boy. I can see it in your posture. I could stop it, you know? I could stop it all right now.”

“Shut up!” Lucas took a step forward, but his bad knee gave way under the weight of the tension.

A dry crack echoed in the grotto. It wasn't a gunshot, but the sound of Lucas's boot slipping on an invisible patch of black ice. The police chief fell heavily onto his side, and the weapon slid across the polished stone floor, away into the darkness.

Arnald didn't run. He moved with the fluidity of one who knows every crevice of his sanctuary. In his right hand, surgical steel glinted: a long-bladed scalpel.

Elena felt the world narrow. Emotional claustrophobia, that old friend that constricted her throat, threatened to paralyze her. *Don't analyze*, she ordered herself. *Act*. Her green eyes scanned the space, searching for an advantage. Cylinders, cables, metal tables with instruments... and her own backpack, fallen two meters away.

The doctor lunged at Lucas, who was fruitlessly trying to get up. Arnald wasn't seeking to kill with fury, but with precision; he raised the scalpel, aiming for the femoral artery, a clean cut to bleed the officer dry in minutes.

“No!” The scream tore from Elena's throat.

She lunged for her backpack, ignoring the sharp pain as her knees slammed against the rock. Her fingers, numb from the sub-zero temperature, torpedoed into the fabric's interior. *Rope, ice axe... no time...* Her hand closed around a cold, rough metal cylinder. The emergency flare.

She heard a grunt from Lucas and the wet sound of tearing fabric. Arnald was on top of him.

Elena ripped off the safety cap with her teeth, tasting metal on her tongue, and pulled the firing pin.

A furious hiss filled the cave.

The red light exploded with the violence of a dying sun. The magnesium burned at a thousand degrees, devouring oxygen and staining the blue ice an infernal crimson. Shadows danced wildly, turning the stalactites into bloodied claws.

Elena didn't throw the flare. She brandished it like a sword of fire, charging at Arnald.

The doctor looked up, and for the first time, his mask of control shattered. His pupils, dilated by the perpetual gloom of the cave, couldn't adapt. The glare blinded him instantly. He dropped the scalpel and brought his hands to his face with a shriek that sounded not human, but animal.

“My eyes! I can't see the work!”

Elena seized the confusion. With a movement born of the most primitive desperation, she rammed Arnald with her shoulder, keeping him away from Lucas. The impact sent the doctor stumbling backward, away from the safe zone of the platform.

Arnald stumbled, disoriented by the white blindness burning his retinas. His feet sought purchase, but found only the uneven edge of a slope of compacted snow that descended towards the lower natural ventilation tunnels.

“Elena!” Lucas warned from the ground, pressing his hand against the superficial cut on his thigh.

The doctor faltered one last time. His heel stepped into emptiness. There was no dramatic fall into a chemical pit, no definitive end. His body rolled down the hard snow slope, disappearing into the blackness of a side tunnel, swallowed by the entrails of the mountain he believed he controlled. The thuds of his body against the ice walls faded, replaced by the echo of his distant curses.

The heat from the flare, still crackling in Elena's hand, had done more than just blind the enemy. The brutal thermal shift, fire against the ancient icy mass, triggered a chain reaction in the unstable vault.

*Crrrreeeacck.*

The sound was geological. A spiderweb crack appeared in the ceiling, directly above the preservation tanks. A block of ice the size of a car broke off, crashing to the ground just meters from where Lucas lay.

The ground vibrated. The cave, violated by heat and violence, began to reject its invaders.

Elena threw the flare, which continued to burn on the rock, and ran to Lucas. She grabbed him by the armpits, pulling him with a strength she didn't know she possessed.

“Get up! We have to go!” she cried, her voice barely audible over the roar of fracturing ice.

Lucas gritted his teeth, cold sweat mixing with the blood on his leg. He leaned on her, using her as a human crutch.

“He got away…” he gasped, looking toward the dark tunnel where Arnald had disappeared.

“If we stay, we'll become part of his collection,” she retorted, pushing him toward the exit, toward the main tunnel they had entered.

Behind them, the Sculptor's sanctuary began to collapse. The ice was reclaiming its own, burying the cylinders, the evidence, and the secrets under tons of solid water. But Arnald Soler was not among the rubble. The monster had fled into the storm, wounded, blind, but alive. And the mountain, outside, roared with the promise of an endless night.

## Chapter 35: El Derrumbe

THE SCULPTOR'S CAVE, VALL DE BOÍ
DECEMBER 26, 2018

The silence that followed the Sculptor's fall was not one of peace, but of inhalation. The mountain, wounded by the detonations and the unnatural violence it had harbored in its bowels, took a breath an instant before roaring.

Elena Vives felt the vibration in the soles of her boots before she heard the sound. It was not a deep geological tremor, but something more fragile and terrifying: the structure of ice and rock, weakened by years of maniacal excavations and the recent heat of the flare, was yielding to gravity. The gallery ceiling, that vault which had been a cathedral of horrors, let out a sharp groan, similar to metal twisting under hydraulic pressure.

“Move,” she ordered herself, though her voice failed her. It was a mental command, an electrical impulse sent to legs that felt filled with molten lead.

Lucas's pistol weighed in her right hand like an anchor, but her fingers refused to release it. The instinct to cling to the only tool of power she had left was superior to the logic of survival.

A block of schist the size of a coffin detached from the ceiling and crashed three meters from her, pulverizing one of the empty display cases. The roar was physical, a slap of air and dust that filled her mouth with the coppery taste of old earth. Elena spun on her heels, turning her back on the abyss where her executioner lay, and ran.

It wasn't an athletic run, like those she practiced on the trails of Aigüestortes to quiet the noise in her mind. It was a clumsy, animalistic flight. Her boots slipped on the uneven ground, covered in frost and debris. Her breath scraped her throat, each inhalation a mix of stale air and limestone particles.

She reached the bifurcation that led to the main exit, the wide tunnel through which they had entered.

She stopped dead, skidding on the gravel.

Before her eyes, the architecture of the cave changed in real time. A zigzagging crack, like a black lightning bolt drawn on the stone, ran across the tunnel's lintel. With a dry, painful crunch, tons of rock collapsed, sealing the passage with absolute finality. The resulting cloud of dust struck her, forcing her to cover her face with her forearm, protecting her moss-green eyes from the sandy blindness.

She coughed, bending at the waist. Panic, that old shadow that had lived in her chest since the failed case, tried to close its claws around her lungs. *Claustrophobia*. The idea of being buried alive, of becoming yet another fossil in the mountain's collection, threatened to paralyze her.

*Analyze. Don't feel, analyze.*

She forced herself to look at her father's watch on her left wrist. The second hand advanced with mechanical indifference. Time existed. She existed.

The main gallery was blocked. The ceiling above her groaned, releasing a fine rain of pebbles that clattered against her shoulders like hail. She needed another way out. Elena's mind, trained to see patterns where others only saw chaos, scanned the gloom.

The air.

If the cave collapsed, the displaced air had to escape somewhere. Or, even better, if the storm outside roared with the force she remembered, the pressure difference must be creating a current.

She closed her eyes for a second, blocking out the visual terror to enhance her sense of touch.

There it was. An icy caress on her right cheek. A tongue of frozen air that did not belong to the stagnant atmosphere of the underground.

She turned towards the side wall. At first glance, it appeared to be a solid wall of sedimentary rock, but as she approached, the residual light allowed her to distinguish a vertical fissure, hidden behind a broken stalagmite. It wasn't a tunnel, barely a scar in the rock, probably a natural drainage channel for spring meltwater.

The air whistled through it.

Elena shed the imaginary backpack of fear and assessed the opening. It was narrow. Terribly narrow. Her slender build was her only advantage, but even so, it would be like returning to the earth's womb.

The ground beneath her feet shook violently. Behind her, in the gallery of horrors, the ceiling began to collapse in a chain reaction. The roar became a continuous rumble, an avalanche of stone devouring the empty space, drawing closer to her position.

There was no choice.

She tucked the pistol into her waistband, feeling the cold metal against her hip, and squeezed into the fissure.

The world narrowed to friction. The rock pressed against her chest and back simultaneously. She moved sideways, crawling like a worm. The rock tore at her technical jacket, scratching the skin underneath. Every centimeter gained was a negotiation with the mountain.

Her breathing quickened, short and shallow. The space was so narrow that she couldn't fully expand her rib cage. The darkness was absolute now, thick as tar. Only the sound of destruction behind her and the howl of the wind ahead gave her spatial coordinates.

*I'm going to die trapped here. The rock will shift and crush my ribs.*

The voice in her head was seductive, inviting her to surrender.

“Shut up,” she growled, and the word was lost, absorbed by the stone.

She followed the air current. The cold intensified, transforming from an annoyance to a sharp pain that bit at her face. Her gloved, clumsy hands searched for handholds on the uneven surface. A sharp edge cut her cheek, but the pain was irrelevant.

Suddenly, the incline sloped upwards. Elena jammed her boots into the walls, using them as wedges, and climbed. Her muscles burned, lactic acid flooding her limbs, but adrenaline was a powerful fuel.

She saw a diffuse clarity above. Not sunlight, not even moonlight, but the milky gray of nocturnal snow reflecting nothingness.

With one last effort, driven by the primal terror of what was happening below, she hoisted herself up. Her hands found powdery snow, soft and treacherous, and then firm rock covered in ice. She pulled herself up, feeling her hips get momentarily stuck before breaking free with a yank that nearly dislocated her shoulder.

Elena rolled onto the surface, moving away from the opening.

The outside air hit her like a sledgehammer. It wasn't simply cold; it was atmospheric violence. The blizzard howled with a fury that made the cave's collapse seem like child's play. The snow fell horizontally, white needles stabbing into every exposed millimeter of skin.

She had barely moved three meters away when the earth beneath the snow shuddered.

A dull, deep sound, like a drum beaten underwater, rumbled through the valley. Elena turned, covering her face with her hands.

Where the fissure from which she had just escaped had been, the ground sank. The mountainside seemed to sigh and then imploded. Snow, rock, and ice were swallowed by the earth in a cloud of white dust that merged with the storm. The secret entrance, the gallery of display cases, the Sculptor's body... everything vanished, sealed beneath tons of eternal debris.

The mountain had closed its scar.

Elena lay in the snow, panting, her heart pounding against the frozen ground. The cold immediately began to penetrate her layers of clothing, reminding her that the escape wasn't over. It had only changed scenery.

She looked out into the white nothingness surrounding her. She was out, yes. But she was alone, in the middle of the worst storm of the century, and the night had no intention of being merciful.

She touched her pocket, verifying that the weapon was still there, a talisman of steel. Then she looked at her watch. The face was cracked, but the hands were still moving.

She had to find Lucas.

## Chapter 36: Capítulo 36

**Slopes of Peguera Peak, Aigüestortes**
**December 26, 2018**

The silence after the catastrophe weighed more than the rock itself.

Elena Vives emerged from the snow like someone reborn, but without the innocence of birth; only with the grime and guilt clinging to her skin. The roar that had swallowed the cave entrance, burying the Sculptor and his gallery of horrors under tons of granite, had ceased. Instead, the wind howled, claiming the acoustic void with a ferocity that chilled the blood even before it touched the skin.

She rose, coughing stone dust and ice crystals. The world had shrunk to a scale of violent grays. The storm, that white beast christened with technical names by meteorologists and feared by locals as an ancient deity, had swallowed the mountain. There was no up or down, only a vortex of icy needles piercing her exposed cheeks.

She looked at her hands. In her right, she still clutched Lucas's service pistol. The metal bit into her palm, a frigid reminder of the violence she had just enacted. She felt no remorse for the man crushed in the earth's bowels; her analytical mind, the one that usually dissected criminal profiles with surgical precision, now only processed basic survival data.

“Lucas?” The word emerged from her throat like a shard of glass, tearing at her vocal cords.

The blizzard carried the name away, scattering it into the vast blackness.

Elena forced herself to her feet. Her legs, numb from the forced position and the cold, reacted with sharp cramps and deep pain. Her technical high-mountain gear, torn during the escape and collapse, no longer retained body heat. She felt the temperature stealing the sensation from her toes, a slow, narcotic invasion promising a sleep from which she wouldn't awaken.

She spun around, searching for landmarks. The mine entrance had vanished. Where a black hole had once gaped in the hillside, there was now only a slope of rubble and disturbed snow, a fresh scar on the face of Peguera. Lucas had stayed outside, protecting the perimeter, battling his own limp and exhaustion. He had to be nearby.

“Lucas!” she shouted again, this time injecting all the air her aching lungs could muster.

She took a step and sank knee-deep. The fresh snow concealed the terrain's traps: loose rocks, crevices, treacherous roots. Every movement was a negotiation with gravity. Her father's watch, its face cracked by some recent impact, marked an irrelevant time. Seconds didn't matter; only heartbeats. And hers galloped wildly against her ribs, searching for an escape.

She advanced blindly, guided only by the spatial memory of where he had been before the world came crashing down. Visibility was nil. She extended her left arm, groping at the void, while with her right, she kept the weapon pressed to her chest, a useless talisman against the elements.

She stumbled. She fell face down, and the metallic taste of blood filled her mouth as she bit her tongue. She wanted to stay there. The snow suddenly seemed comfortable, a soft bed offering rest. “Just a moment,” a seductive voice whispered at the base of her skull. “Close your eyes. Stop fighting.”

The image of Lucas’s brown eyes, with that tired gaze that always seemed to understand too much, flashed in her mind. It wasn't a hallucination; it was an anchor. He hadn't given up on her when she was a hostile stranger invading his precinct. She couldn't leave him to die alone in that vast blackness.

She got to her feet with an animalistic growl. Determination replaced warmth.

About 10 meters ahead, a dark shape broke the monotony of the white. It didn't have the hard edges of a rock or the verticality of a pine. It was an irregular lump, half-covered by the mantle the storm wove with industrial speed.

Elena lunged towards it, ignoring the stab of pain in her quadriceps. She dropped to her knees beside the shape and began digging with her bare hands, frantic. She pushed the snow aside as if it were grave dirt.

The synthetic fabric of a parka appeared beneath her fingers. Navy blue. The local police uniform.

“Lucas, please, no…” she pleaded, her voice broken by the tears she couldn't allow herself to shed.

She pulled at the body, turning it with an effort that wrenched a groan from her. Lucas Riera’s face was pale, with a bluish tinge to his lips that made Elena’s stomach clench into a tight knot. His eyes were closed, and a deep cut on his forehead, above his left eyebrow, had a black, glistening crust of frozen blood.

Elena pulled off her right glove with her teeth and pressed her fingers against his neck, searching for the carotid artery. Her own hands trembled so much that at first, she felt nothing. Panic, a black, viscous tide, threatened to drown her.

“Focus. Search for life.”

There it was. Weak. Erratic. Like the flutter of a moth trapped in a jar. But it was there.

“You’re alive,” she whispered, and the vapor of her breath struck the inert face of the officer. “You hear me, Riera. Don’t you dare leave me here alone! I haven’t given you permission!”

Lucas didn’t respond. His breathing was shallow, barely a whistle between his parted lips. Hypothermia was already doing its work, extinguishing vital systems one by one, like someone turning off the lights in an empty house.

Elena assessed the situation with terrifying clarity. They were exposed to the elements, in the midst of the decade’s worst cyclogenesis, without shelter, without communication, and with one of the two incapacitated. The valley was isolated. No one was coming. Hope wasn't a strategy; it was a luxury they couldn't afford.

She looked around, searching for natural shelter, a ledge, anything. Nothing. Only the night and the snow that fell with the intent of erasing them from the map. She had to move him. She had to find warmth. Or they would both die before dawn.

She grabbed Lucas by his jacket lapels and pulled. He weighed a ton of inert flesh and wet clothes. Elena gritted her teeth until her jaw ached.

“Come on,” she growled, dragging him inch by inch up the slope. “Come on, damn it!”

That’s when she saw it. About 10 meters away, half-hidden by a boulder that had deflected part of the avalanche, something glowed with a faint, artificial light. It wasn't the reflection of the moon, which was non-existent that night. It was a red indicator light. Flashing.

A snowmobile.

Probably the vehicle the Sculptor had used to get there, or perhaps a forgotten emergency cache. The origin didn’t matter; the promise did.

Elena felt a surge of pure adrenaline. It was the difference between the end and opportunity. She pulled Lucas again, her boots slipping, her muscles burning with the fire of supreme effort.

They reached the machine. It was semi-overturned, but the engine still radiated residual heat. Elena checked the panel. It had fuel.

She hoisted Lucas onto the back with a strength she didn't know she possessed, securing him with the safety straps meant for cargo. He groaned faintly, a sound of pain that was music to her ears. It meant he still felt.

She climbed into the driver’s seat. Her hands, stiff as claws, fumbled for the ignition. She turned the key. The engine coughed, sputtered, and died.

“Don’t do this to me,” Elena hissed, pounding the handlebars. “Work! You have to work!”

She tried again. The engine’s roar sprang to life, rough and noisy, competing with the storm. The headlight flickered on, cutting through the profound darkness with a beam of yellow light that illuminated a suicidal downhill path.

Elena looked back, towards the pile of rocks that buried the cave. Evil was sealed in there. But the mountain wasn't done with them yet.

She accelerated, and the snowmobile launched itself into the white abyss, descending towards the valley where shadows awaited, and where the truth, like ice, was beginning to crack.

## Chapter 37: Capítulo 37

**Western slope of Peguera Peak, December 26, 2018**

The roar of the engine was the only constant in a universe that had turned white and hostile. Elena Vives gripped the snowmobile's handlebars with such force that her knuckles had turned the color of old wax, visually merging with the tempest devouring the world. There was no up or down, only the frantic advance through a milky void and the dead weight of Lucas Riera against her back, tied to her waist with a climbing rope she had salvaged from the emergency kit.

The machine's vibration climbed her arms, shaking the crescent-shaped scar on her left palm, a pulsating reminder of past mistakes that now served as an anchor to keep her from losing her sanity.

The wind wasn't blowing; it was pushing with the solidity of an invisible concrete wall. The snow wasn't falling; it was shooting horizontally, thousands of microscopic needles seeking any exposed inch of skin to plunge in their icy venom. Elena squinted her moss-green eyes, barely protected by scratched snow goggles she had found in the cargo compartment. Each jolt from the uneven terrain sent an electric shock up her spine, but the real terror wasn't capsizing, but the stillness she felt behind her.

Lucas wasn't moving. His head rested heavily on her right shoulder, an inert burden that threatened to drag them both into oblivion.

“Hang on!” she yelled, though the sound was torn from her lips before she could hear it, swallowed by the storm's bellowing throat.

The descent was a calculated suicide. Without visual references, Elena navigated, trusting gravity and a primitive instinct that screamed at her that the valley was below. Peguera Peak's slope was a labyrinth of sharp rocks hidden beneath the pristine blanket, deadly traps waiting to shatter the vehicle's skis.

A sudden lurch almost unseated her. The rear track skidded on black ice. The vehicle fishtailed, roaring in protest. Elena compensated with her body weight, leaning to the left, feeling Lucas's inert torso pendulum dangerously. Fear, that viscous entity that usually paralyzed her in enclosed spaces, here expanded to fill the horizon. There were no walls, but the claustrophobia was absolute; the sky had collapsed onto their heads.

She looked at her father's watch on her wrist. The hands glowed faintly in the daytime gloom. Twenty minutes had passed since the mountain swallowed the Sculptor. Twenty minutes in which Lucas's body temperature must have dropped to levels incompatible with life.

Suddenly, a rectangular shadow emerged from the whiteness, solid and geometric amidst the organic chaos.

A stone hut. A dry-stone construction, a vestige of ancient shepherds, crouching against a rock overhang that protected it from avalanches.

Elena released the accelerator. The snowmobile slowed, sinking into the accumulated powder snow. She stopped the engine a few meters from the entrance. The silence that followed was more deafening than the mechanical roar; an auditory void that hummed in her ears.

She unfastened the knot at her waist with clumsy, numb fingers that seemed to belong to a corpse. Turning, she saw Lucas's face. Frost had covered his eyelashes and the gray hairs at his temples, giving him the appearance of an abandoned marble statue. The blood from the wound on his head had solidified into a dark, almost black, maroon trail.

“Lucas,” she whispered.

Nothing. Not a twitch.

She dismounted the vehicle, sinking knee-deep. The wind exploited her vulnerability to buffet her, almost knocking her down. She walked towards the wooden door, worm-eaten by decades of winters, and pulled on the rusty iron latch. It was stuck.

Desperation transformed into mechanical fury. Elena picked up a stone from the ground, a granite shard with sharp edges, and struck the metal. Once, twice, three times. The sound of the impact was dry, pathetic against the storm's majesty. On the fourth strike, the rust yielded.

Now came the impossible part.

She returned to the snowmobile. Lucas was a large man, broad-shouldered and with dense bones. She, with her athletic but slender build, was an ant trying to move a fallen giant. She grabbed him under the armpits, feeling the stiffness of the police parka beneath her gloves.

“Help me, damn it,” she growled, pulling with her back muscles, using her legs as a lever.

The body slid from the seat and fell onto the snow with a dull thud that pained her soul. She dragged him. Every meter was a battle won against entropy. Her boots slipped, her breath was a torn bellows, vaporizing before her eyes. Lucas's weight was the weight of all the guilt she carried: the victim she hadn't saved in the past, the lie of her retirement, the attraction she never dared confess to him.

They reached the threshold. With one last inhuman effort, a tug that made her see stars and strained every fiber of her neck, she pulled him into the gloom of the shelter.

She kicked the door shut and dropped the crossbar.

The interior darkness smelled of old firewood, dust, and rodent droppings. But there was no wind. The outdoor howl reduced to a distant wail.

Elena didn't allow herself to rest. Her hands flew over his body. She sought the carotid pulse. Her fingers, still numb, pressed against his cold neck.

One second. Two.

There it was. A weak, erratic beat, like the flutter of a dying bird trapped in a too-large ribcage.

“You're here,” she exhaled, and the accumulated tension broke into a dry, tearless sob.

The place was austere. A stone fireplace in one corner, a rotten wooden cot, and a wobbly table. Elena pulled a tactical flashlight from Lucas's pocket and swept the room with its beam. There were remnants of firewood piled next to the hearth. Dry. Miraculous.
