She worked with the efficiency of an automaton. She tore pieces of her own thermal shirt to use as tinder. She took Lucas's lighter from his pants pocket. The small flame danced, fragile, before igniting the fabric and licking the wood. Smoke began to rise, and with it, a promise of warmth.

She returned to the officer. She needed to see the wound. With surgical care, she parted his matted black hair. The blow was ugly, an irregular gash on his parietal bone, but the clotted blood had stopped the bleeding. The real enemy wasn't the cranial trauma, but hypothermia.

She had to remove his wet clothes. The parka was stiff with ice. Elena struggled with the zipper, cursing the manufacturer, the weather, and fate. When she finally managed to open it, she removed his vest, flannel shirt, and inner t-shirt soaked with cold sweat.

Lucas's skin had a bluish tint under the light of the nascent fire. The old scars and calluses on his hands told the story of a man who had lived to protect others, forgetting himself.

Elena shed her own jacket and lay down beside him on the stone floor, ignoring the dirt. There weren't enough blankets, no medicine. She only had her own body heat. She pressed against his back, wrapping her arms around his broad chest, intertwining her legs with his.

It was an intimacy forced by survival, but one that detonated something deep within her psyche. She felt the slowness of his breathing against her own chest.

“Don't you dare leave me alone in this, Riera,” she whispered against the back of his neck.

The fire crackled, casting dancing shadows on the bare stone walls. Elena looked at her watch. The face was cracked, a diagonal line splitting time in two: before and after the shelter.

Suddenly, Lucas moved. It was barely a spasm, an involuntary muscle contraction.

“Elena?” The name came from his throat like the sound of sandpaper scraping stone.

She sat up slightly, propping herself on an elbow to look at his face. Lucas's brown eyes were half-open, glazed, unfocused, searching for something in the gloom that wasn't there.

“I'm here. We're safe,” she lied. No one was safe while the White Beast roared outside.

Lucas blinked slowly, fighting a narcotic fog. His large, cold hand sought hers, finding the scar on her palm. His fingers closed weakly around Elena's hand.

“I saw him...” he murmured, his voice thick and slurred. “Before the roof fell in...”

Elena felt a chill that had nothing to do with the temperature.

“Who did you see, Lucas? The Sculptor is dead.”

Lucas shook his head, an infinitesimal movement that seemed to cost him all his remaining energy. His gaze cleared for a second, lucid and terrified, fixing on her green eyes.

“Not the Sculptor...” he coughed, and a bubble of pink blood appeared on his lips. “I saw the satellite phone in his hand. The light was on, Elena.”

He paused to draw breath, a sibilant, painful sound.

“He wasn't calling for help... he was confirming the dispatch.”

The phrase hung in the stale air of the hut. Elena felt her blood run cold in her veins. If the Sculptor had sent something before dying—coordinates, files, an order—then his death wasn't the end. It was just the first movement of a mechanism that had just been set in motion.

The fire crackled, oblivious to the new abyss opening beneath their feet. They were isolated, wounded, and trapped by the storm of the century, but what truly ought to terrify them wasn't outside, in the snow, but traveling invisibly through data networks to an unknown recipient.

## Chapter 38: Capítulo 38

The cold was not a guest; it had become the absolute master of the cabin. It didn't bite, but gnawed, a slow, constant erosion that stripped the rotting wood of any illusion of refuge. Elena Vives felt her own body temperature plummeting, a biological countdown marked not by seconds, but by shivers.

They were huddled in the furthest corner from the door, on a musty straw mattress that smelled of ancient damp and forgotten sheep's wool. Elena kept her back against the stone wall, using her torso as a shield for Lucas Riera. The police chief lay with his head in her lap, his large, calloused hands inert on his chest, seeking a warmth the atmosphere denied him.

"Don't fall asleep," Elena whispered. Her voice emerged like a wisp of vapor, hoarse, scratching a parched throat.

Lucas blinked. His usually warm brown eyes were glazed, sunken in dark sockets. Hypothermia wasn't violent at this stage; it was a narcotic seduction, a promise of peace if one only closed their eyelids.

"I can't feel my leg," he murmured. The grimace of pain that had contorted his face for the past hour had softened, and that was what terrified Elena. Pain was life; its absence, the prelude to the end.

She slid her left hand under his jacket, searching for his heartbeat. The crescent-shaped scar on her palm brushed against the rough fabric of his uniform. Lucas's heart beat with the slowness of a funeral drum. *Thump... silence... thump.*

"Talk to me, Riera." Elena shook the man's shoulder, forcing an authoritative tone she didn't feel. Fear, that old shadow that had haunted her since her retirement, tried to close its claw around her lungs. Emotional claustrophobia. There, trapped by the storm of the century, there was no escape. "Tell me something real. No police procedures."

Lucas managed a half-smile, lopsided and weak.

"My grandmother... she used to say that snow has memory. That if you die in it... it keeps you forever."

"No one is going to die today." The assertion was sharp, but lacked foundation.

The wind howled outside, a bodiless beast scratching at the board joints, seeking entry. The 'White Beast' had not only isolated the valley; it was erasing it from the map. Elena looked towards the door, barricaded with a worm-eaten beam. The darkness was almost total, only broken by the grayish bioluminescence filtering through the cracks, a reflection of the snow piled against the walls.

Elena felt a cramp in her legs. She needed to move to generate warmth, but if she moved away from Lucas, he would lose the only thermal source keeping him tethered to consciousness. She settled for rubbing the policeman's arms, a frantic and useless friction against the frozen immensity.

Suddenly, the sound of the wind changed.

It wasn't a howl, but a dull thud. Something heavy had collided with the outer structure.

Elena froze. Her analytical mind, trained to dissect crime scenes, instantly activated, cutting through the fog of numbness. The sound was unnatural. It wasn't a branch broken by the gale. It was rhythmic.

*Crack. Crack. Crack.*

Footsteps. Someone was breaking the crust of ice accumulated in front of the entrance.

Lucas tried to sit up, but his muscles didn't respond. He only managed a choked moan.

"Elena?"

"Silence," she hissed.

She fumbled in the gloom. Her fingers closed around an old, rusty iron poker they had found next to the blind fireplace. The metal was so cold it burned her skin.

The blows ceased. The silence that followed was heavier than the storm. Elena held her breath, her moss-green eyes fixed on the door, trying to drill through the wood with her gaze. The Sculptor? Had he come to finish his work, to cure his 'art' by freezing the witnesses?

The doorknob turned. The rust protested with an agonized creak.

The beam they used as a bar vibrated, but held.

"Open up!" The voice came muffled, barely a human thread in the cacophony of the tempest.

Elena didn't move. She squeezed the poker until her knuckles turned white. If it was the killer, he would break in by force. If it was help... what kind of help could walk through that white hell?

A brutal blow shook the entire cabin. The wood groaned. Another blow. The beam jumped from its supports, falling to the floor with a dull thud.

The door burst open, pushed by a violent gust of wind that brought with it a swirl of snow and hail. The temperature inside plummeted instantly.

A figure was silhouetted in the doorway.

It was massive, distorted by layers of technical clothing and covered in frost from head to toe. It looked like a yeti, a creature born from the storm itself. It wore blizzard goggles that hid its eyes and a thick scarf covered its face.

Elena stood up, stumbling, raising the rusty iron like a spear.

"Stay back!" she cried, though her voice cracked.

The newcomer did not retreat. He took a step inside and slammed the door shut, fighting against the air pressure until he managed to snap the latch shut. Silence returned, but now there was a new presence, charged with urgency.

The man pulled down his scarf and took off his goggles.

Icy blue eyes, framed by frozen lashes, met hers. His ash-blond hair, usually neat, was flattened by his hat and damp with cold sweat.

"Put that down, Elena," said Dr. Arnald Soler. His breath formed dense clouds in the stale air.

Elena didn't lower the weapon. Her brain processed the information with painful slowness. Arnald. Her mentor. The man who had taught her to read the minds of monsters. And the man whose alibis were starting to show too many cracks.

"How...?" she began, but the trembling of her jaw prevented her from finishing.

Arnald didn't answer immediately. He took off the heavy backpack he was carrying and dropped it with a metallic clang. His movements were precise, clinical, ignoring the threat of the poker.

"Lucas needs immediate attention," Arnald said, ignoring the question. He knelt beside the policeman, who barely seemed aware of the new presence.

Elena watched the door, expecting to see someone else enter. Perhaps Roco, Lucas's dog, who had disappeared into the woods hours ago. But the threshold remained closed. Arnald had arrived alone. Absolutely alone in the middle of nowhere.

"Where's the rescue team?" Elena asked, lowering the iron a few inches, but without letting go.

"I am it." Arnald pulled a golden thermal blanket from his backpack and began to wrap Lucas with an efficiency that bordered on the mechanical. "No one else could come up. The roads are obliterated. I had to use snowshoes and the old smugglers' route."

"On foot?" Incredulity battled with relief. "It's suicide, Arnald."

"Or a necessity." The doctor looked up. His eyes were expressionless, pools of calm amidst the disaster. "I knew you would come here. It was the only logical refuge according to the geographical profile of the area. And I knew Lucas wouldn't last the night without medication."

Arnald took out a thermos and a vial. The smell that emanated from him when he opened his parka hit Elena. It wasn't just sweat and snow. There was something else. A subtle, almost imperceptible trace of mint and... surgical soap? The clean, sterile scent, amidst the cabin's grime, was disturbingly out of place.

Elena took a step back, her back hitting the cold wall.

"How did you know we were alive?"

Arnald was busy injecting something into Lucas's arm. The policeman groaned as the needle pierced skin hardened by the cold.

"I didn't know," the doctor replied without looking back. "But the alternative was to let you die. And you know I don't like losing my cases, Elena. Or my patients."

Elena watched Arnald's hands. They were steady, without the slightest tremor despite the physical exertion of the climb. Hands that healed. Hands that could manipulate bodies with an artist's precision.

Lucas coughed, a convulsion that shook his body under the thermal blanket.

"Arnald..." croaked the policeman, finally recognizing the man. "Roco... did you see Roco?"

The doctor shook his head, adjusting the blanket's collar around Lucas's chin.

"There was nothing out there, Lucas. Only ice and wind. If the dog got separated from you..."

He left the sentence hanging in the air, suspended like a death sentence. Elena felt a pang of pain for the animal, but it was eclipsed by the tension emanating from Arnald. He had arrived too quickly. He knew the location too precisely.

"Here." Arnald handed the thermos to Elena. "Broth with electrolytes. Drink. You're entering phase two of hypothermia. Your lips are blue."

Elena hesitated. She looked at the steaming liquid. Her body screamed for it, every cell demanding warmth. But her instinct, that internal alarm that had kept her alive in the face of human predators, hummed in her ears.

"You first," she said.

Arnald paused. A shadow of emotion crossed his pale face. Disappointment? Amusement? He raised the thermos and took a long drink, maintaining eye contact with her. Then he wiped his mouth with the back of his gloved hand.

"I'm not your enemy, Elena. Your mind sees patterns where there is only chaos. It's your gift and your curse." He extended the thermos to her again. "Drink. If you faint, I won't be able to carry both of you."

Elena took the container. The heat of the metal transferred to her numb fingers, a sensation so intense it almost hurt. She drank. The salty, hot liquid went down her throat like lava, awakening her constricted stomach.

"The storm will last at least forty-eight more hours," Arnald informed her, returning to massaging Lucas's bad leg to reactivate circulation. "We're trapped."

Elena slid down the wall until she was sitting, the poker still within reach.

"Trapped," she repeated.

The cabin, which had previously seemed like a solitary tomb, had transformed. Now it was a shared cage. Lucas dozed under the effect of the medicine and the warmth. And she was locked in with Arnald Soler, her mentor, her savior, and perhaps, just perhaps, the monster who froze people to preserve them eternally.

Arnald took off his parka, revealing an immaculate gray wool sweater underneath. He sat opposite them, crossing his legs, and pulled out a small camping lantern that illuminated the shelter with a white, clinical, unforgiving light.

"Rest, Elena," he said, his voice soft, almost hypnotic. "I'll take the first watch."

Elena gritted her teeth. Her eyelids weighed tons. The warmth of the broth was betraying her, pushing her towards sleep. But she knew one thing with absolute certainty: she would not close her eyes. Not while Arnald Soler was watching them with that icy gaze, evaluating their fragility as if they were unfinished sculptures.

Outside, the storm roared again, burying any hope of escape under meters of white silence.

## Chapter 39: Capítulo 39

The wind did not howl; it shrieked with the voice of a thousand torn throats. Outside the stone walls of the Ventosa i Calvell shelter, the storm known as “The White Beast” had ceased to be a meteorological phenomenon, transforming into an entity with a will of its own, a ravenous deity clawing at the wood, seeking any crevice to devour the warmth that still lingered inside.

Elena Vives adjusted her father’s watch on her wrist, a reflexive gesture, a nervous tic that sought to anchor her to a time that seemed to have dissolved. The hands glowed faintly in the gloom, indifferent to the chaos that reigned in the valley. Inside the cabin, the air was thick, laden with the smell of dampness, old wool, and the metallic tang of dried blood.

Lucas Riera lay on a straw mattress in the corner most sheltered from the drafts. His breathing was a wet, rhythmic, painful death rattle, competing with the roar outside. Elena approached him, her boots thudding dully against the floorboards. She knelt, ignoring the sharp pain in her own joints, remnants of the hellish trek that had brought them there.

“Lucas,” she whispered, though she knew the name would be lost in the gale’s roar.

The police chief's skin was the color of damp ash. Sweat beaded his forehead despite the cold that numbed Elena's fingers. It wasn't just the physical wound consuming him; it was the degenerative disease he had concealed so carefully, and which now, under the extreme pressure of exhaustion and hypothermia, was exacting its toll.

Elena placed the back of her hand on his forehead. It burned. A raging fever that contrasted with the frozen world trying to kill them.

“Hold on,” she ordered, transforming the plea into a military command. She couldn't afford the weakness of compassion, not now. She needed him to be a pillar, even if cracked.

She stood and walked to the small, barred window. Through the slats of the shutters, the daylight was a milky gray, indistinguishable from night. The snow wasn't falling; it flew horizontally, like glass shrapnel. The “claustrophobia of the soul” that always stalked her began to close its fingers around her throat. It wasn't the small space that terrified her, but the lack of control, the certainty that nature had decided to erase all traces of human order.

Suddenly, a different sound broke the storm's monotony.

It wasn't the creaking of yielding wood, nor the whistling of the wind. It was a dry knock. Rhythmic. Human.

*Knock. Knock. Knock.*

Elena froze. Her heart, which until that moment had beaten with the lethargy of fatigue, now slammed against her ribs like a trapped bird. She looked towards the main door, a slab of reinforced oak that vibrated under the wind's siege.

The Sculptor? Had he come to claim his final pieces?

Her eyes swept the room, searching for a weapon. Her pistol had been rendered useless, lost in the snow hours ago. Her fingers closed around the handle of a rusty ice axe that rested beside the cold fireplace. The metal was cold, but it offered a promise of violence that quelled her tremor.

The knocking repeated, more frantic.

“Open up!” a muffled voice, barely a spectral whisper filtered by the storm.

Elena pressed herself against the wall adjacent to the entrance, raising the ice axe. If it was him, if it was the monster who froze beauty to preserve it, she would split his skull before he could utter one of his phrases about the aesthetics of death.

“Elena!” The cry was clearer this time.

She recognized the voice. Or thought she did. She dropped her guard for a fraction of a second, just enough for doubt to settle in.

With a swift motion, she unbarred the door.

The door didn't open; it exploded inward. A gust of snow rushed in like a miniature avalanche, blinding Elena momentarily and stealing what little warmth remained in the room. A figure wrapped in layers of Gore-Tex and wool collapsed on the threshold, rolling inside.

Elena lunged against the door, using all her weight, digging her boots into the floor to overcome the wind's resistance that tried to keep it open. She cried out with effort, feeling the muscles in her back protest, until she finally managed to close the wooden leaf and re-bolt it.

Relative silence returned, broken only by the newcomer's gasps.

The figure on the floor was a heap of technical clothing covered in frost. He moved clumsily, removing a pair of ski goggles that had protected his eyes from the whiteout.

Arnald Soler looked up.

The forensic pathologist, her mentor, the man who had taught her to read the dead as if they were roadmaps, seemed to have aged ten years in the last few hours. His gray beard was a block of ice; his lips, a bluish, trembling line.

But what chilled Elena's blood was not the cold he brought with him, but the solitude that enveloped him.

Arnald was alone.

Elena kept the ice axe raised, pointing at the man's chest.

“Don’t move,” she warned. Her voice sounded strange in her own ears, metallic and devoid of emotion.

Arnald blinked, his eyes trying to focus on her through his exhaustion. He pulled off a glove with his teeth, revealing a bruised hand that he raised in a gesture of peace.

“Elena… thank God.”

“Where’s Roco?” she asked, ignoring his relief. The question was a test. Arnald’s German Shepherd never left his side. They were a single entity, mind and muscle.

Arnald’s expression broke. A shadow of genuine pain crossed his face, so visible and raw that Elena felt a pang of doubt about her suspicions. The man dropped his head, resting his forehead on the floorboards, breathing with difficulty.

“I lost him,” Arnald croaked. His voice was a broken thread. “On the ridge, before reaching the pass. Visibility was zero… the wind hit us sideways.”

Arnald coughed, a dry sound that shook his body.

“He slipped his leash, Elena. I tried to grab him, but the ground… the ice gave way beneath his paws. He fell down the north slope. I couldn’t… I couldn’t go down for him. The storm would have killed me.”

Elena studied his face. She searched for the lie, the micro-expression that would betray deceit. But she only found the absolute defeat of a man who had to choose between his life and that of his most loyal companion. The dog’s absence left a palpable void in the room, a confirmation that the mountain was unwilling to return anything it took.

“Get up,” she said, lowering the ice axe slightly, though without releasing it.

Arnald rose with difficulty, his joints creaking. His gaze traveled around the room until it stopped at the corner where Lucas lay.

“Is he alive?” he asked, and the clinical tone returned to his voice, displacing the grieving man to make way for the doctor.

“Barely,” Elena replied.

Arnald took off his backpack, letting it drop with a heavy thud, and limped towards Lucas. Elena stepped in his way, blocking his path with her body.

Their eyes met. Moss green against dark brown. History against suspicion.

“Why are you here, Arnald?” Elena’s question was a scalpel. “Last time we spoke, all circumstantial evidence pointed to you. Access to chemicals, knowledge of anatomy, obsession with preservation.”

Arnald didn’t back down. He held her gaze with a weary dignity.

“I’m here because I know how he thinks,” he said, and for the first time, Elena noticed real fear in his pupils. Not fear of her, nor of the storm, but of something worse. “And because I know what he’s going to do next. I’m not The Sculptor, Elena. I was his teacher, yes, at university, decades ago. But he… he has surpassed any theory I could teach.”

“That doesn’t explain why you’ve come here to die.”

“I came because I found this in his old basement lab, the one the police overlooked.”

Arnald reached into the inner pocket of his anorak and pulled out a folded paper, protected by a plastic sleeve. He held it out to her.

Elena took it with her left hand, feeling the scar on her palm tense. She unfolded the document. It was an architectural diagram. Not of a building, but of a hydraulic structure.

The Cavallers Dam.

But over the technical plan were handwritten annotations in elegant, disturbingly precise calligraphy. Red circles marked the lower inspection galleries, those submerged beneath the level of the icy water, where the temperature was constant and close to absolute freezing point.

“‘The Great Refrigerator’,” Elena read aloud, a marginal note written in blue ink.

“He wants to use the dam’s pressure and cold,” Arnald explained, his voice gaining urgency. “It’s not enough for him to freeze his victims in the snow. He wants to encapsulate them. To create an eternal mausoleum where the ice never melts, protected by millions of liters of water.”

Elena looked at the paper, then at Lucas. The connection was instant and terrifying.

“Lucas…” she murmured.

“Lucas is the obstacle,” Arnald said. “But you… you are the central piece, Elena. You always have been. The profiler who sees order in chaos. He wants you to witness his *Magnum Opus*.”

A groan from the straw mattress interrupted the revelation. Lucas stirred, his large, calloused hands clutching the blanket desperately.

“Don’t… go…” he stammered, eyes closed, trapped in the fever’s nightmare. “It’s a… trap.”

Arnald knelt beside him, and this time Elena didn’t stop him. She watched the doctor’s hands move with professional skill, taking his pulse, checking his dilated pupils.

“He has sepsis,” Arnald diagnosed bluntly. “The leg infection has spread. If he doesn’t receive intravenous antibiotics and real warmth in the next six hours, his heart will stop.”

The silence that followed the pronouncement was heavier than the storm.

“We can’t go down to the village,” Elena said. It was a fact, not an opinion. The snow outside was already a meter deep, and visibility was zero. Attempting the descent with a dying man would be suicide.

“No,” Arnald agreed, standing. “But the dam… the dam has maintenance facilities. They have an emergency infirmary for the operators. Generators. Heating.”

Elena looked at him incredulously.

“Are you suggesting we go directly into the wolf’s den?”

“I suggest it’s the only place with electricity and medicine within ten kilometers,” Arnald countered. “And it’s where he is. If we want to end this, if we want Lucas to live, we have to go there.”

Elena walked back to the window. The wind slammed against the shutters as if demanding entry. The idea was madness. Dragging a sick man through the white hell, straight towards a psychopathic killer who knew the terrain better than they did.

But the alternative was to wait. To wait for Lucas’s fever fire to extinguish, leaving him cold like the sculptures the killer left in his wake. To wait for the storm to bury the shelter.

She turned to Arnald. The man was rubbing his hands, trying to regain feeling. He looked fragile, far from the authoritative figure he had been to her years ago. And yet, he had climbed all the way here. He had lost his dog, his only companion, in the attempt.

“How do we take him?” Elena asked.

Arnald pointed towards a corner of the shelter where an old rescue sled lay, a fiberglass stretcher with runners, a relic from some past ski season.

“We tow him. You and I. We take turns.”

“The snow will swallow us,” she said, testing the argument’s soundness.

“The snow has already swallowed us, Elena. The question is whether we want to die still or moving.”

Elena looked at Lucas. His chest rose and fell with an irregular rhythm. The nascent beard on his face accentuated his pallor. She remembered the warmth of his hand when he had pulled her from the river days ago, the endearing clumsiness with which he tried to conceal his limp. There was an alliance between them, forged in initial mistrust and tempered in survival. She couldn’t let him die on that dirty wooden floor.

She approached Arnald’s backpack and began to rummage. She found ropes, carabiners, and a thermos.

“Drink,” she ordered Arnald, handing him the thermos containing the remains of lukewarm broth she had prepared earlier. “You need energy. We leave in ten minutes.”

Arnald nodded gratefully, his trembling hands cradling the container.

Elena knelt beside Lucas one last time. She stroked his cheek, feeling the feverish heat burn her fingertips.

“Lucas, listen to me,” she whispered in his ear. “We’re going for a walk. It’s going to hurt. It’s going to be cold. But I’m not leaving you here.”

Lucas half-opened his eyes. They were two dark wells, veiled by illness, but for an instant, there was a flash of recognition.

“Elena…” His voice was a rasp of sandpaper. “He… he’s waiting for you.”

“I know,” she replied, and felt a glacial calm settle in her chest, displacing fear. It was the same cold determination she felt before entering a crime scene, the necessary detachment to analyze horror without being consumed by it. “And I’m coming for him.”

Preparing the sled was mechanical work. Elena tied Lucas down firmly, wrapping him in all available blankets and covering him with a waterproof tarp secured with elastic cords. He barely protested; consciousness ebbed and flowed like the tide.

Arnald adjusted the dragging straps to his chest. Elena did the same with a second set of improvised ropes. They looked like two draft animals, two beasts of burden preparing for penance.

“The dam is two kilometers away, following the contour line,” Arnald said, checking his compass, though the needle danced erratically due to the storm’s magnetic disturbances. “If we maintain the pace, we’ll arrive in one hour.”

“Or we’ll die in twenty minutes,” Elena corrected.

They approached the door. The roar outside had diminished, but only slightly. It was a deception, a brief respite the storm offered to lure them into its trap.

Elena gripped her ice axe in her right hand, her left holding the towing rope. She looked at Arnald.

“If you fall behind, I won’t stop,” she warned him.

“I know,” he said. There was no reproach in his voice, only acceptance.

Elena opened the door.

The world had turned white. There was no sky or earth, only a vortex of frozen particles. The cold struck her like a physical mallet, stealing the air from her lungs in a single, agonizing instant.

They took the first step.

The sled scraped over the virgin snow. Lucas’s weight was an anchor pulling them back, but the terrain’s incline worked slightly in their favor.

They advanced into nothingness.

Behind them, the shelter door remained open, banging against the frame, a black mouth laughing at their departure. Ahead, the dam waited. And with it, the man who believed death was the only form of perfection.

Elena fixed her gaze on the white void in front of her. Roco was gone. Lucas was fading. Only she remained, the suspect beside her, and the mountain’s indifferent immensity.

She gritted her teeth and pulled.

## Chapter 40: Capítulo 40

The snow didn't fall; it assaulted. Each flake was a shard of glass propelled by a wind that had ceased to be air, transforming into a solid wall, a constant push against the chest that made the simple mechanics of breathing difficult. Elena Vives adjusted her ski goggles, though they were of little use in that white universe where sky and earth had merged into a milky, hostile void.

"Elena..." Lucas Riera's voice reached her fragmented, devoured by the mountain's roar.

She turned. She could barely make out the police officer's silhouette three paces away. Lucas advanced, dragging his right leg, sinking his boot into the deep snow with a grimace of pain that, though invisible beneath layers of thermal clothing, Elena could feel in her own bones. The limp, a constant reminder of his physical fragility, had worsened with the sudden drop in temperature.

"Not far now," she shouted, not to convince him, but to quiet the buzzing of her own anxiety.

She checked her left wrist. The crystal of her father's watch was fogged, but the second hand advanced with its usual mechanical indifference. Three o'clock in the afternoon. The light should have been gray, but the storm had stolen the day, imposing a premature and suffocating twilight.

They followed the GPS track, a blinking blue dot that intermittently lost signal, guiding them towards the old abandoned meteorological station on the northern ridge. According to the old cadastral maps Lucas had unearthed, a basement existed there, a natural permafrost chamber used decades ago for geological samples. The perfect place for someone looking to stop time.

The terrain leveled abruptly. Before them, a geometric, angular, dark shape emerged, breaking the organic anarchy of the snowfall. The concrete building looked like a forgotten bunker, its windows sealed by rotten planks and metal plates.

Lucas leaned against the outer wall, seeking a respite from the gale. His breath formed dense, rapid clouds.

"If he's not here..." he began, but a cough cut him off.

"He is," Elena stated.

It wasn't a logical deduction, but a visceral certainty. Her stomach had tightened into a knot, the same sensation she got before entering a crime scene, that electric vibration that made the hairs on the back of her neck stand on end.

They circled the structure, looking for an entrance. The main door was blocked by an avalanche of debris and old ice. Elena pointed towards a side, where a half-buried maintenance hatch showed signs of recent manipulation: the snow around it wasn't compacted by the wind, but disturbed by a shovel.

Lucas drew his service weapon. The black metal contrasted with the snowy clarity of the landscape. Elena took out her own tactical flashlight and nodded.

The police officer pulled the rusty handle. The metal groaned, a sharp complaint that echoed too loudly in the valley's isolation. They opened the gap just enough to slide inside.

The change was immediate. The howling of the wind died down, replaced by a dense, heavy silence, the kind that seems to press on your eardrums. But it wasn't the silence that stopped Elena dead in her tracks.

It was the smell.

It didn't smell of damp, or mold, or the confinement of forgotten places. The air was permeated with a clinical, pungent aroma: fresh mint and industrial disinfectant. It was a fragrance reminiscent of a hospital, of obsessive cleanliness, of an operating room before the first incision.

"My God," Lucas murmured, lowering his weapon but keeping his finger off the trigger guard.

Elena's beam of light cut through the darkness. They were in a service corridor, with frost-coated pipes running along the low ceiling. The walls had been scrubbed clean; the concrete gleamed, free of dust.

They advanced. The sound of their boots on the clean floor echoed with an obscene clarity. Elena noticed her hands sweating inside her gloves. She took them off, tucking them into her pockets, and ran her thumb over the crescent-shaped scar on her left palm, a nervous tic that sought to anchor her to reality.

At the end of the corridor, a double door of thick plastic, like those used in industrial cold rooms, blocked their way.

Elena looked at Lucas. The police officer's dark eyes reflected the fatigue of the trek and the weight of an illness that was eating away at his time, but in that moment, there was only determination. He pushed the plastic curtains aside.

They entered the gallery.

The space was vast, an old storage room converted. But there were no shelves or boxes. In the center of the room, illuminated by halogen spotlights powered by a silent generator vibrating in some corner, three rectangular blocks stood tall.

They were so transparent they seemed made of glass, but the mist emanating from Elena's mouth confirmed it was ice. Treated, polished ice, preserved at a precise temperature to prevent opaque crystallization.

They approached the first block.

Inside, there was no corpse in the forensic sense of the word. There was no decomposition, no laxity of death. The woman was suspended in the ice, dressed in a white silk tunic that floated around her as if she were underwater. Her hands were clasped over her chest in a gesture of serene prayer. Her blonde hair fanned out perfectly, each strand individualized.

"Marta," Lucas whispered, his voice breaking. He recognized the girl who had disappeared from the neighboring village three months ago.

But it wasn't just Marta. It was an idealized version of her. The makeup was subtle, correcting imperfections; the posture, studied to evoke artificial sanctity.

"He doesn't kill them," Elena said, her voice sounding strangely clinical in her own ears. She forced herself to analyze, not to feel, to raise the mental wall that separated her from the horror. "He... cures them. From his perspective. He stops the deterioration."

She moved towards the second block. A young man. The same imposed placidity. An identical glacial perfection.

And then, the third block.

It was empty.

It wasn't a solid block, but a mold. A coffin of crystal and half-frozen water, awaiting its occupant. Beside it, a metallic workbench, spotless, arranged with maniacal symmetry. Scalpels aligned by size, glass syringes, labeled vials with elegant, antique calligraphy.

Elena approached the table. Among the surgical instruments, there was an object that didn't fit the sterile environment. A leather-bound sketchbook.

She opened it with her fingertips, fearing to contaminate the evidence, or perhaps fearing what she would find.

The pages were filled with anatomical studies. Charcoal illustrations of Renaissance precision. Muscles, tendons, facial expressions. But as she turned the pages, the representations changed. They ceased to be generic.

An eye appeared. A detailed iris with golden flecks on a dark background.
A hand with a crescent-shaped scar.
A profile of a straight nose and thin lips.

Elena stopped breathing. It wasn't just any anatomical study. It was her.

There were portraits of her sleeping, of her walking through the village, of her looking at a glacier. The strokes were loving, devoted, sickening. The artist wasn't trying to capture her life, but to prepare her for eternity.

"Elena... you have to see this," Lucas called from the back of the room.

The police officer was standing in front of a wall that had remained in shadow. When he illuminated it with his flashlight, it revealed a collection of images pinned to the insulating cork. They were snapshots of Lucas. Telephoto lens photographs. Lucas limping. Lucas buying bread. Lucas talking with Elena.

But all the images of Lucas had a peculiarity: they were crossed out with a precise, surgical red X over the face. And beneath each one, a note written in the same elegant calligraphy: *IMPERFECTION. DETERIORATION. WASTE.*

"He knows we're here," Lucas said, turning to her. His hand gripped the pistol handle until his knuckles turned white. "No, wait... he knows *you* would come."

A heavy, metallic clang resonated behind them. The sound of steel pins sliding into place.

It came from the entrance. From the hatch door they had entered through.

Elena ran towards the corridor, her heart pounding against her ribs like a caged animal. She pushed through the plastic curtains and reached the reinforced main access door. She pulled the handle.

Immovable.

"It's locked from the outside!" she shouted, hitting the metal with her fist.

"The generator," Lucas warned from the main room.

The machine's constant hum changed tone. It became sharper, more strained. The halogen lights flickered once, twice, and then went out, plunging them into absolute darkness, broken only by the nervous beams of their flashlights.

And then, a hiss.

It came from the ventilation ducts in the ceiling. It wasn't air. It was a dense, white gas, beginning to fall like a slow cascade onto the gallery floor.

Elena recognized the smell instantly, beneath the mint layer.

"Nitrogen!" she roared, backing away towards the center of the room. "He's purging the oxygen!"

Lucas joined her, illuminating the gas that was accumulating on the floor like a ghostly tide.

"Is he going to suffocate us?"

"No," Elena replied, suddenly understanding the nature of the trap. Her profiler's mind clicked the last piece into place with a terrifying snap. "He's going to preserve us. He wants to lower the temperature until we can't move. He wants us still for the procedure."

The already intense, frigid environment began to bite with renewed ferocity. It wasn't the cold of the outside wind; it was a thermal stillness that penetrated clothes, skin, and flesh, seeking the body's core heat to extinguish it.

Elena looked at the frozen cell, the one that was waiting.
She understood it wasn't for her. She was the muse, the final masterpiece that would require preparation.
The empty receptacle, the waste one, the practice one... was for Lucas.

"We have to get out," Lucas said, his voice weakening. The gas was rising past his ankles.

"There's another way out," Elena said, forcing herself to focus. She remembered the blueprints, the structure of old refrigerators. "The drain. The meltwater channel."

She threw herself to the floor, searching under the tables, ignoring the gas burning her exposed facial skin. There, in the farthest corner, under a cast-iron grate.

"Help me!" she shouted.

Lucas limped to her. Together, they struggled with the grate. It was rusted, fused by years of inactivity and ice. Elena pulled with all her strength until the muscles in her back screamed, until the scar on her hand burned.

"On three," Lucas grunted.

One. Two. Three.

The grate gave way with a dry creak, raising a cloud of rust. Beneath, a dark, narrow, cylindrical tunnel, descending into the bowels of the mountain. It smelled of wet earth and abyss.

"You first," Lucas ordered, pushing her.

"We can't both fit. Your leg..."

"Go!" He pushed her with a force that brooked no argument.

Elena slid into the hole. The concrete tube was barely wider than her shoulders. Darkness swallowed her. She crawled, feeling claustrophobia close her throat, that ancient panic living in her chest awakening violently. *Don't think. Move.*

She heard Lucas enter behind her, the rustling of his body dragging, and then the screech of the grate being put back in place, or perhaps falling.

Above, in the gallery they left behind, a voice distorted by a PA system came to life, filling the air with a soft, cultured, and terrible tone.

"*Impatience is the enemy of perfection, Elena. But I appreciate the enthusiasm. The session has begun.*"

## Chapter 41: Capítulo 41

**West slope of Peguera Peak, Lleida Pyrenees**
**December 26, 2018**

The snow wasn't falling; it was attacking. It was a swarm of white wasps plunging their stingers into the little exposed skin they had left. There was no sky, no ground, no horizon. Only a milky void existed, an absolute nothingness that devoured the world in its wake, erasing their tracks seconds after they were made, as if the mountain refused to admit they had ever been there.

She took one step. Then another. The mechanics of movement had become the only possible religion.

She stopped and turned, shielding her eyes with her gloved hand. Three meters away, Lucas's figure was a dark stain silhouetted against the perpetual gray. His limp, which he usually tried to conceal with masculine pride, was now an agonizing drag. His right leg didn't respond; he dragged it as if it were deadwood, an inert weight anchored to his hip.

"Don't stop," he growled, his voice barely a torn whisper that the wind instantly carried away. He had frost on his eyelashes and skin of a bluish hue that turned her stomach.

Behind her, Doctor Arnald Soler walked hunched over, clutching the straps of his medical backpack with white knuckles. His orange parka was the only color in that monochromatic universe. He looked at her, and in his eyes, she saw the exact reflection of her own terror. He was no longer the mentor, nor the paternal figure who had guided her through the academy's halls; he was a man reduced to instinct, and that made him unpredictable.

"How much further, Elena?" Arnald shouted to be heard over the storm's howl.

She looked at her wrist. The crystal of her father's watch was shattered, a web of fractures distorting the hands. Three forty in the morning. They had been descending for twenty-five minutes that felt like three decades.

"We're close," she lied. Or perhaps not. Spatial orientation was a fantasy in the midst of that cyclogenesis.

She turned back to the slope and continued to break trail. Lucas's pistol weighed in the pocket of her technical jacket, a cold lump against her ribs. Five bullets. Five chances to stop a ghost or to make an irreparable mistake.

The descent became steeper. The virgin snow reached their knees, turning each stride into a muscular battle. Her breath burned, a glacial fire descending her throat and settling in her lungs. 'Claustrophobia,' that insidious voice whispered at the back of her neck. It wasn't the confinement of four walls, but the confinement of the outdoors. The white immensity squeezed tighter than any cell.

Suddenly, the terrain changed. The soft snow gave way to a sheet of hard ice beneath the surface layer.

She heard the moan before the thud.

She turned in time to see Lucas lose his balance. His good leg slipped, and the bad one gave way without protest. He fell heavily onto his side, letting out a choked cry that pierced her chest.

"Lucas!"

She lunged toward him, ignoring the burning in her own thighs. Arnald stood paralyzed for a second before reacting, his movements clumsy from the layers of clothing.

She reached Lucas and grabbed him by the lapels of his coat. His eyes were closed, and his teeth were clenched so tightly that she feared they would break.

"I'm fine," he panted, a lie so obvious it was insulting. He tried to get up, but his body refused to obey.

"Don't move," Arnald ordered, kneeling beside him in the snow. He searched for Lucas's pulse in his neck with trembling fingers. "He's at his limit, Elena. Hypothermia is gaining ground on adrenaline. If we don't get him to shelter in twenty minutes, his heart will start to fail."

"I'm not going to die up here," Lucas spat, opening his eyes. His usually warm brown irises were glazed over. "Help me up."

They helped him up, the two of them. Arnald on the left, Elena on the right. She felt Lucas's dead weight, his body heat escaping through the layers of fabric. He was a big, solid man, but at that moment he seemed made of glass about to shatter.

"Lean on me," she told him in his ear, shouting to overcome the gale. "We're going to find Marc. We're going to get him out of there."

The mention of the child acted like dirty fuel. Lucas nodded, clenched his jaw, and took a step.

They continued. The world narrowed to the rhythm of their forced march. One, two, drag. One, two, drag.

The wind changed direction, bringing a distinct smell with it. It no longer smelled only of ozone and ice. There was something else. A smell of stagnant damp, wet stone, and static electricity.

And then, the concrete beast emerged from the darkness.

First it was just a shadow, an unnatural density in the snow flurry. Then, the straight lines defined themselves. The Cavallers Dam. It wasn't just a retaining wall; it was an inverted cathedral, an obscene curve of human engineering defying the mountain's brutality. It rose before them like a giant tombstone, its concrete walls disappearing into the blackness above and plunging into the abyss below.

She stopped dead, her heart pounding against her ribs like a caged bird. There it was. The final stage.

"My God," Arnald murmured. He had stood petrified, staring at the structure.

The dam seemed to vibrate. She could feel the hum of the turbines, or perhaps it was just the blood pounding in her ears. They knew, thanks to the call, that Marc was in the foundations, in the inspection gallery. They knew the place was connected to the child's pulse. A tomb that would explode if the little one's heart stopped beating.

"The maintenance entrance," she said, pointing toward the eastern end of the crest, where a small cubic building broke the wall's symmetry. "It must be that way."

They dragged Lucas the last 100 meters. The ground leveled out, becoming the asphalt service road, now covered by a treacherous layer of black ice.

Upon reaching the metallic door of the access building, she released Lucas, leaving him leaning against the cold wall. She took out the pistol. The metal stuck to her glove. She disengaged the safety with a click that sounded like a gunshot in the relative silence offered by the wall's shelter.

"Arnald, stay with Lucas," she ordered, without looking at them. Her attention was fixed on the lock.

"Elena, wait..." Lucas began, trying to straighten up, but his legs were like rags.

She approached the door. She expected to find it closed, locked, welded shut. She expected to have to shoot the hinges or look for a window.

But the knob turned in her hand with an oily smoothness.

The door opened inward, revealing a wolf's maw that exhaled a current of air slightly warmer than the outside.

The relief lasted a fraction of a second. It was immediately replaced by a terrifying certainty.

The air emanating from inside didn't smell of machinery, nor grease, nor the dust of places closed for decades.

It smelled of mint.

A chemical, pungent, clean aroma. Antiseptic.

The Sculptor's scent.

He hadn't forced entry. He was waiting for them. The open door wasn't an oversight; it was an invitation.

"What's wrong?" Arnald asked, noticing her hesitation.

She turned toward them. The light of her tactical flashlight illuminated their haggard faces.

"He's here," she whispered, and the words seemed to freeze in the air before reaching them. "He's already inside."

She entered the darkness, pistol first, crossing the threshold into the belly of the beast, knowing that they had just ceased to be hunters to officially become guests.

## Chapter 42: Capítulo 42

**Cavallers Dam, Vall de Boí**
**December 26, 2018**

The metallic click of the latch echoed like a gunshot in an empty cathedral. As we pushed the heavy steel door shut behind us, the storm's howl was abruptly severed, replaced by a silence heavier than the snow itself. It wasn't merely the absence of noise, but a dense, oily presence that clung to wet clothes and seeped into one's ears.

I raised my tactical flashlight. The beam of white light cut through the blackness, revealing a raw concrete corridor that stretched into the bowels of the mountain. The walls wept. Threads of icy water slipped down the gray stone, feeding dark puddles on the ground that reflected our distorted intrusion.

"Elena?" Lucas's voice sounded hoarse, amplified by the tunnel's tubular acoustics.

I turned. The police officer leaned against the doorway, his cerulean face under the artificial light. His right leg bore no weight; he held it at an odd angle, and his knuckles were white from the force with which he clutched his side. Beside him, Doctor Arnald Soler breathed with difficulty, adjusting the straps of his medical pack with nervous, almost spasmodic movements. His eyes darted from the shadows to my gun.

"We're inside," I whispered, checking the weapon's safety for the umpteenth time. "Don't make a sound. Concrete carries sound better than air."

"It's an inspection gallery," Soler murmured, running a gloved finger along the damp wall. "These dams have miles of tunnels to monitor for leaks. He could be anywhere."

"Not anywhere," Lucas corrected, limping towards me. Pain was etched in the corners of his lips, taut as violin strings. "He'll look for the coldest spot. Where the temperature is constant."

I nodded, forcing my lungs to accept the stale air. It smelled of stale damp, ancient mold, and something else. Something chemical. Sharp.

I moved forward. My military boots, soaked through to the socks, made a dull, sticky sound on the soaked ground. The flashlight was my only defense against the absolute darkness that swallowed us. Every few meters, rusted pipes crossed the low ceiling like varicose veins of a sleeping giant.

My mind, trained to dissect crime scenes, began to catalog the environment. There was no dust. The floor was clean in the center, recently swept. Someone had been here, dragging something heavy that had left a subtle groove in the accumulated slime.

"Look at the floor!" I said, lowering the beam of light.

Two parallel lines furrowed the dirt, disappearing into the shroud of gloom that stretched ahead.

"Drag marks," Lucas stated, drawing his useless radio purely by police instinct before putting it away with a gesture of frustration. "Recent. The water hasn't erased them yet."

"Marc!" The word escaped Soler's throat, laden with a fear that sounded too genuine.

"Doctor, stay behind me," I ordered, without looking at him. Distrust was an animal scratching at the back of my neck. Soler knew this place too well, and his insistence on accompanying us bordered on the pathological. "Lucas, you cover the rear. If you can't walk, tell me now. I can't carry you."

"I can run if I have to," he lied. I knew it was a lie, he knew I knew it, but in that cement hell, pious lies were the only fuel we had left.

We delved deeper into the gallery. The temperature dropped with every step. It wasn't the sharp cold of the outer wind, but a frigid stillness that penetrated to the bone marrow, slowing the pulse. My breath formed ephemeral clouds before the light.

Suddenly, I smelled it.

I stopped dead, raising my left hand to stop the men.

"What is it?" Arnald whispered, almost colliding with Lucas's back.

I inhaled through my nose, seeking to confirm the alarm signal my reptilian brain had just sent. There it was. Beneath the stench of stagnant water and rust. A clean, surgical fragrance that did not belong in this godforsaken place.

Mint. And industrial disinfectant.

The scent of preserved death.

"He's here," I said, and my voice sounded strange and distant. "The Sculptor is here."

The tension in the tunnel skyrocketed. Lucas tensed his shoulders, momentarily forgetting his injured leg. Soler brought a hand to his mouth.

"How do you know?" the doctor asked, his eyes wide with fear.

"Because it smells like an operating room," I replied, resuming the advance, this time with my weapon raised and my finger caressing the trigger guard.

The tunnel opened into a wider room, a valve chamber where the ceiling rose several meters. In the center, a huge steel pipe descended vertically, disappearing into the floor. The roar of pressurized water circulating inside the conduit was a deep vibration felt in the molars more than heard.

But it wasn't the machinery that stopped my heart for a beat.

At the back of the room, illuminated by a construction light connected to a portable generator that hummed softly, was a staged scene.

It wasn't Marc.

It was a sewing mannequin, one of those old, headless ones, with a stained beige cloth torso. It was placed on a hand-carved ice pedestal. The block was translucent, perfect, without a single air bubble inside. A feat of thermal engineering impossible under these rudimentary conditions.

On the mannequin's torso, pinned with a scalpel, was a Polaroid photograph.

I approached, feeling how gravity seemed to increase, pulling my limbs towards the floor. Lucas limped to my side, his breathing a painful wheeze.

My flashlight's beam illuminated the photo.

It was me.

A photo taken from a distance, through a window. I was in my city apartment, sitting at my desk full of case files, my head in my hands. The date written in black marker on the bottom white border read: *Three days before coming here*.

"He was waiting for you," Lucas whispered. The understanding in his tone was devastating. "Elena, this isn't about Marc. Marc is the bait."

"The dam..." Arnald's voice trembled. "He's not trying to hide anything. He's exhibiting."

I moved closer to the ice pedestal. The cold emanating from it bit at my face. Inside the frozen block, suspended like an insect in amber, there was something. It wasn't pure water.

I brought the flashlight closer to the ice.

In the center of the block, perfectly preserved, was a wristwatch. A Rolex Submariner with the strap unclasped.

"It's Marc's," Arnald said, choking back a cry. "I gave it to him when he finished his residency."

The watch read 03:55.

I looked at my own wrist, at my father's old watch. The hands showed 03:50.

Five minutes left.

"It's a countdown," I said, and panic, cold and sharp, tried to take control of my hands. "It's not the time of death. It's showtime."

A metallic sound, strident and rhythmic, began to echo from the opposite tunnel, the one that plunged even deeper into the dam's darkness.

*Clang. Clang. Clang.*

Someone was hitting a metal railing with a tool. The rhythm was slow, deliberate. An invitation.

"Welcome, Elena," a voice distorted by the echo, devoid of humanity, bounced off the concrete walls, coming from everywhere and nowhere. "The canvas is prepared."

Lucas stepped between the darkness and me, raising his trembling flashlight.

"Police!" he yelled, but his voice cracked. "Come out with your hands up!"

We only got a soft, dry laugh, like dead leaves rustling in the wind.

"Beauty requires sacrifice, Agent Riera," replied the voice, now closer, coming from the shadows behind the immense pipe. "And the cold... the cold is the only one that never lies."

The generator's hum abruptly stopped. The construction light went out.

Darkness fell upon us like a granite slab, absolute and total, only broken by the trembling beam of my flashlight that now pointed at nothing. And in that blackness, the scent of mint became unbearably strong, right behind me.

## Chapter 43: Capítulo 43

The air inside the subterranean chamber bore no resemblance to the storm raging on the surface. If outside the “White Beast” was a chaos of wind and snow, down here an unnatural silence reigned, broken only by the rhythmic hum of generators.

Elena Vives crossed the reinforced metal threshold. The change in pressure momentarily plugged her ears. Her fingers instinctively sought the face of her father's watch on her wrist, a tactile anchor against the unreality unfolding before her green eyes. This was no ordinary basement. The walls were covered with immaculate white insulating panels, reflecting a bluish light that emanated from the center of the room.

"Tell me I'm not seeing what I think I'm seeing," Lucas Riera murmured behind her.

The police officer's voice was hoarse, laden with a fatigue that went beyond the physical pain in his right leg. Elena heard the characteristic drag of his boot against the clinical linoleum floor; his limp had worsened with the humidity and the effort of the descent.

"Don't look at the floor, Lucas," she warned, her own voice devoid of inflection, in a purely analytical mode. "Look up."

The "Sculptor's Workshop" was not designed to hide corpses, but to display them.

In the center of the room, dominating the space like a pagan altar, stood a two-meter-high reinforced glass cylinder. It was connected to thick hoses that pumped a viscous, transparent liquid, maintaining a temperature Elena could feel on her skin even several steps away. An absolute, chemical cold.

Inside the tank floated the figure.

Elena approached, forcing her legs to ignore the instinct to flee. The crescent-shaped scar on her left palm throbbed, a phantom reminder of old wounds, but her profiler's mind took control. There was no decomposition. No lividity. The preservation was so perfect it was obscene.

It was a young girl. Barely a teenager. Her ash-blonde hair floated around her head like a weightless halo, and her eyes were closed with a disturbing serenity. She wore an immaculate antique lace white dress.

"The technique is... different," Elena whispered, approaching the glass. Her breath did not fog the surface; the ventilation system absorbed any moisture. "It's not standard cryogenization. It's chemical suspension. He has vitrified her."

Lucas stood beside her. The large man, broad-shouldered and with calloused hands accustomed to dealing with drunks and lost tourists, seemed to shrink before the sight. His face, marked by exhaustion and the shadow of a three-day stubble, paled under the cyanotic lighting.

"It can't be," Lucas's whisper was a strangled thread of a voice.

Elena turned to him. She detected the trembling in the police officer's hands, the way his jaw tensed to suppress a retch.

"Do you know her?" she asked. It wasn't an accusation, but a need for data.

Lucas leaned heavily on a nearby metal table, covered with surgical instruments aligned with meticulous precision. The smell of mint and antiseptic, the olfactory signature they had traced to this place, was overwhelming in that corner.

"It's Julia," he said, and the name came out laden with an ancient pain. "Arnald's daughter."

Elena looked back at the tank. The piece clicked into place with a deafening mental snap. The obsession with purity. The rejection of the corruption of flesh.

"She disappeared fifteen years ago," Lucas continued, his gaze fixed on the girl's suspended face. "It was said she had eloped with a boyfriend to the coast. Arnald... he played the role of the devastated father. He organized search parties. He cried in my office."

Elena observed the girl's features. She had the same bone structure as Doctor Soler, that aristocratic and cold beauty. But where Arnald had an icy gaze, this girl retained an eternal innocence, frozen in time.

"She didn't elope," Elena deduced, her mind working at full speed. "He couldn't allow her to grow. To change."

"She was sixteen years old," Lucas ran a hand through his dark hair, where premature gray strands gleamed under the artificial light. "She was the living image of goodness in this valley. Arnald always said the world didn't deserve her. That life would eventually taint her."

"And he decided to save her," she concluded, feeling an icy nausea in her stomach. "To preserve her before time could touch her. This is his 'Masterpiece', Lucas. Everything else... the other victims... they were rehearsals. Tests to perfect the formula that would keep her like this forever."

Elena circled the tank slowly. At the base of the cylinder, an engraved metal plate bore a single Latin word: *AETERNITAS*.

The profiler then understood the magnitude of the monster they faced. Arnald Soler didn't kill out of sadism, nor a conventional sexual impulse. He killed to stop the clock. His psychopathy was a rebellion against mortality itself, a desperate and bloody attempt to freeze a moment of perceived perfection.

"If she's here..." Lucas straightened up, his hand descending to the holster of his weapon, although the gesture seemed futile against the horror before them, "it means he never left. That he's been coming down here every day, for fifteen years, to look at her."

"To care for her," Elena corrected. She pointed to the monitors flashing with constant readings. "The maintenance of this equipment requires daily attention. It's a sanctuary, Lucas. And we have desecrated it."

A metallic sound, heavy and dry, echoed from the chamber's entrance, behind them. It wasn't the wind. It was the locking mechanism of the security door.

The hum of the generators changed tone, becoming sharper, more urgent.

Elena and Lucas turned in unison. The figure silhouetted in the doorway wore no mask. He didn't need one down here, in his domain. Arnald Soler watched them with those ice-blue eyes that now seemed to reflect the same emptiness as his daughter's tank. He wore a rubber apron over his impeccable clothes, and the smell of mint seemed to intensify, flooding the room until it was difficult to breathe.

"Julia is a light sleeper," the doctor said, his voice soft, paternal, freezing Elena's blood more than any shouted threat. "And we don't like uninvited guests."

Lucas drew his weapon, but his movement was slow, clumsy due to the pain in his leg and the weight of the truth he had just discovered. Arnald didn't even blink. He held a remote device in his right hand, a small control with a single red button that pulsed rhythmically.

"Beauty is fragile, Agent Riera," Arnald continued, taking a step inside as the door finished sealing behind him with a hydraulic hiss. "It requires sacrifices to remain intact."

Elena looked at the tank, then at the father, and finally at the control in his hand. She understood the implicit threat. He wasn't going to kill them directly. He was going to incorporate them into the collection, or worse, he would destroy the sanctuary with them inside to protect his secret.

"It's not love, Arnald," Elena said, stepping forward, interposing herself between Lucas's weapon and the doctor. She needed time, she needed him to talk. "It's taxidermy. You've turned her into an object."

"I have made her eternal, dear Elena. And now, you will be the witnesses to her immortality... or the foundations of her new temple."

## Chapter 44: Capítulo 44

CAPÍTULO: 44 - Chapter 44

# Chapter 44

The metallic echo of the bolt sliding home was definitive, a dry sound that decapitated any hope of immediate return. Lucas Riera slammed his shoulder against the door, a dull thud that barely made the reinforced steel panel vibrate. The police chief let out a grunt, a mix of pain and frustration, as the vibration of the impact traveled up his arm to his neck.

“It’s sealed,” he said, his hoarse voice bouncing off the bare concrete walls. “Hydraulic. Arnald has guided us exactly where he wanted us.”

Elena Vives didn’t respond immediately. Her attention was captive to the oppressive atmosphere of the maintenance corridor. The air down there wasn't just cold; it had a mineral density, laden with stagnant moisture and static electricity. The emergency lights flickered erratically, casting shadows that seemed to stretch like deformed fingers over the rusted pipes running across the ceiling.

The former profiler closed her eyes for an instant, fighting against the pressure that was beginning to build in her chest. The walls seemed to lean inward. *It’s not the place, it’s your mind. Categorize. Analyze.* She opened her eyelids, revealing the moss-green of her irises, now darkened by the gloom.

“He hasn’t locked us in to starve us to death, Lucas,” she whispered, forcing her breathing to maintain a slow rhythm. “If he wanted to kill us, he would have done it upstairs. This is an antechamber. He wants us to see something before the final act.”

Lucas stepped away from the door, limping visibly. The tunnel’s dampness was seeping into his joints, and his right knee, that old enemy that predicted storms, now howled with every step.

“See what?” he asked, shining his flashlight into the depths of the corridor. “All I see is rust and cement. We’re below the lake level, Elena. Millions of liters of water pushing down on our heads.”

“The process,” she replied, advancing into the darkness. “A narcissist of his caliber needs witnesses who understand his ‘genius.’ The act isn’t enough; he needs the critique.”

They walked in single file. The floor was a metal grate over an unfathomable void where the constant drip of distant leaks could be heard. The roar of the turbines was a background vibration, a tectonic heartbeat felt more in the teeth than in the ears.

About fifty meters ahead, the corridor widened. It wasn’t an exit, but an old spare parts storage, a cavity dug into the living rock and lined with industrial brick. But something clashed violently with the decrepitude of the surroundings.

In the center of the chamber, illuminated by a white, almost surgical light emanating from portable halogen lamps, was an immaculate workspace.

Elena stopped dead. The smell hit her before the sight. It didn't smell of engine grease or mold. It smelled of peppermint and antiseptic solutions. A clean, sharp aroma that stung the nostrils.

“My God,” Lucas murmured, lowering the beam of light.

They had found the Sculptor’s workshop.

There were no corpses, but death was present in every detail. A stainless steel table, polished to a mirror sheen, dominated the center. On it, aligned with obsessive geometric precision, rested instruments that did not belong in a hydroelectric dam: scalpels, retractors, drainage cannulas, and rolls of thermal bandages.

Elena approached, her gloved hands hovering over the objects without touching them. Her mind began to reconstruct Arnald’s movements. *Order. Control. Symmetry.*

“Look at this,” said Lucas, pointing to a corkboard on the back wall.

It was covered in sketches. They weren’t a madman’s scribbles, but anatomical drawings of superb technical quality, rendered in charcoal. They depicted postures, angles of repose, facial expressions of absolute serenity. Elena recognized the faces. They were the victims of the past ten years. But in the drawings, they didn’t look dead; they looked asleep, eternal, liberated from the deterioration of time.

In the center of the panel, the largest and most detailed sketch showed Julia. Below it, a note in elegant, old-fashioned cursive calligraphy: *The Ice Pietà. Opus Magnum.*

“These aren’t murders to him,” Elena said, feeling a chill that had nothing to do with the ambient temperature. She rubbed the scar on her left palm with her thumb, a nervous tic that betrayed her agitation. “It’s curation. Arnald believes he’s rescuing beauty from biological corruption.”

Lucas approached a side desk where a thick, leather-bound notebook lay. He opened it cautiously. His large, calloused hands seemed clumsy against the delicacy of the paper.

“It’s a journal,” Lucas read, squinting under the harsh light. “There are chemical formulas. Refrigerant mixtures… Freezing times. He speaks of ‘cellular crystallization without rupture.’”

“He’s perfecting the technique,” Elena said, standing beside him and reading over his shoulder. “The first victims were trials. Errors. That’s why he abandoned them when the ice burned the skin or deformed the features. But with Julia… he believes he has succeeded.”

“’The subject must be alive for the transition,’” Lucas read aloud, and the phrase hung in the frigid air like a sentence. “’Fear preserves the expression. Adrenaline acts as a fixative before the cold.’”

Lucas slammed the book shut, a dry crack that echoed like a gunshot.

“She’s alive, Elena. He has her ready for the… ‘transition.’”

“The turbine hall,” Elena deduced, turning towards the only other door in the chamber, a heavy bulkhead with an opening wheel. “He needs massive energy for the industrial cooling systems. He’s connected his equipment to the dam’s main grid.”

Elena walked towards the bulkhead. She could feel the vibration on the other side, much stronger now. It was the heart of the beast, the place where the brute force of water was transformed into electricity. Arnald Soler was waiting for them there.

She adjusted her father’s watch on her wrist, checking the time purely out of habit, seeking an anchor of reality. The hands advanced inexorably.

“Lucas,” she said without turning, “when we cross this door, there will be no negotiation. He doesn’t see people; he sees raw material. If we try to reason with him, Julia will die.”

The police officer drew his service weapon. The black metal seemed to absorb the aseptic light of the workshop. His face, marked by fatigue and physical pain, hardened until it seemed carved from the very rock of the mountain.

“I’m not going to reason with him,” Lucas assured her.

Elena gripped the bulkhead’s wheel. The metal was icy, biting her skin through the fabric. She strained. The mechanism groaned, a protest of rust and disuse, but it yielded. She turned the wheel once, twice. The hermetic seal broke with a hiss of pressurized air.

The sound that entered was deafening. The mechanical din of the generators drowned out any other sound. Elena pushed the door open, and the amber light of the machine room spilled over them, bathing the immaculate workshop in a sickly hue.

They stepped towards the threshold, leaving behind the theory, the sketches, and the ordered madness, to enter the chaos.

## Chapter 45: Capítulo 45

The concrete of the Cavallers dam wasn't merely cold; it exhaled a mineral chill, ancient, like the breath of a hibernating beast. Elena Vives descended the next flight of metal stairs, her gloved hand brushing against the rusted railing. The metal vibrated beneath her fingers, a deep, constant resonance born from the structure's bowels, where the turbines battled against the pressure of millions of liters of water.

Behind her, Lucas Riera's footsteps were irregular. The drag of his right boot against the grated floor broke the hypnotic cadence of the electric hum. Elena paused for a moment on the landing, then turned. The emergency light, an agonizing red that flickered every four seconds, bathed the policeman's face. Lucas's skin was the color of damp ash, beaded with a sweat that didn't match the tunnel's freezing temperature.

"I'm fine," he grunted before she could ask the question. His voice was sandpaper on stone. "Keep going."

Elena nodded, though her profiler's eyes registered the tension in his jaw, the way his knuckles whitened as he gripped his service weapon. He didn't have much time left. Neither did either of them.

They continued their descent into the lower inspection gallery. The air down there was dense, heavy with the smell of ozone and stagnant water that clung to the palate. The dam's architecture, brutalist and oppressive, seemed designed to crush the human spirit. For Elena, every meter they descended was a turn of the screw in her chest. The walls seemed to close in. The immensity of the water contained on the other side of the concrete wall weighed on the back of her neck like a physical sentence. *Claustrophobia*, her mind whispered, but she pushed it into a dark corner of her consciousness, locking the door. Not now.

At the end of the corridor, a reinforced steel door stood out from the others. It lacked the rust and neglect that characterized the rest of the maintenance facilities. Its bolt gleamed, freshly greased, and a strip of white, clinical light escaped from the gap at the bottom, slicing through the red gloom of the corridor.

Elena raised a fist, signaling them to stop. Lucas pressed himself against the wall, ignoring the pain such a movement must have caused his injured leg. With a synchronization born of desperate necessity, she pushed the handle. The door yielded without a creak.

They entered with weapons raised, sweeping the space in a tense arc.

No one was there. But what they found made Elena slowly lower her pistol, feeling the bile rise in her throat.

It wasn't a machine room. It was a sanctuary.

The space, originally intended for storing generator spare parts, had been transformed with a sickening meticulousness. The concrete walls were covered with immaculate white tarpaulins, creating a sterile chamber within the industrial monster. In the center, a stainless steel table, clean to a surgical gleam, held a series of instruments aligned by size: scalpels, retractors, thick-gauge needles, and rolls of gauze.

But it wasn't the medical equipment that stopped Elena's breath. It was the drawings.

Dozens of charcoal sketches were taped to the tarpaulins. Anatomical studies of Renaissance precision. A hand relaxed in death. The curve of an exposed neck. A closed eye, every lash detailed with maniacal obsession. All showed the same face.

"Julia," Lucas whispered. The name left his mouth like a broken prayer.

The policeman limped toward the side workbench, where a laptop rested beside a digital voice recorder and several black leather notebooks.

Elena approached the drawings. The technique was superb, but it revealed a soulless vision. To its creator, this was not a person, but a composition of flesh and bone to be perfected. The "Sculptor." The nickname now took on a literal and terrifying meaning.

"Look at this," Lucas said, his voice trembling with barely contained fury.

Elena joined him at the table. A topographical map of the dam was spread out, with red ink annotations marking the main turbine hall, the "Atrium." But what captured her attention was the recorder. Beside it, a bandage stained with dried blood and an empty bottle of potent painkillers.

"He's injured," Elena deduced, touching the bandage. "He survived the cave-in, but he didn't come out unscathed."

She pressed the play button, and the tape spun with a soft hiss.

'Entry 412. The subject shows signs of accelerated cellular deterioration after the last cooling phase. The imperfection of the flesh is... disappointing. Beauty is ephemeral only if we allow entropy to win.'

Arnald Soler's voice filled the room, calm, cultured, with that pedagogical cadence Elena had heard so many times when he analyzed a case with her.

'I've had to adjust the nitrogen mixture. The process must be absolute. I'm not seeking death, Julia. I'm seeking the pause. Static eternity.'

There was a pause in the recording, then a metallic sound, as if Arnald had struck the table.

'The intrusion of Vives and the local policeman has accelerated the timeline. They don't understand the work. They only see the crime, not the preservation. I had to abandon the home laboratory, but the Atrium will provide the necessary energy for the final immersion. If I cannot keep you in this world under my conditions... I will take you to the next in a state of unalterable perfection.'

Elena stopped the recording. The silence returned, heavier than before.

"He wasn't talking to anyone in the ventilation shaft that time," Elena murmured, as the pieces of the puzzle clicked into place in her mind. "He was recording himself. Documenting his "Great Work." His narcissism is such that he needs an audience, even a future one."

"He's insane," Lucas spat, striking the table with his uninjured fist. "He has her down there. He's going to kill her."

"No," Elena corrected. The moss-green of her irises darkened as her eyelids opened again. "In his mind, he's saving her. He believes biological life is a corruption. He wants to freeze her at the exact moment he considers perfect. It's worse than murder, Lucas. It's total objectification."

Elena picked up one of the notebooks. She opened it at random. They weren't just medical notes; they were philosophical delusions mixed with hydraulic engineering calculations. Arnald had planned this for months, perhaps years. The dam wasn't an emergency hideout; it was his chosen cathedral from the start. The hydroelectric power provided him with the massive energy needed for his improvised cryogenization units.

Lucas pushed away from the table, breathing heavily. He put a hand to his chest, rubbing his sternum.

"Where is she?" he asked, looking towards the room's only other door, a watertight bulkhead at the far end.

Elena followed his gaze. That door led deeper. Towards the roar.

"In the Atrium, the turbine room," she replied, slipping the recorder into her pocket. "They'll need the proof if they get out of this. That's where the cold is most intense and the energy purest."

She checked her pistol's magazine. Full. She had 14 bullets to stop a man who knew every corner of her psyche, a man who had been her mentor and who now threatened to turn a girl into an ice statue.

"Lucas," she said, approaching him. She placed a hand on his shoulder. The fabric of his coat was damp. "If we go in there, there's no turning back. He knows this place. He's had time to prepare it."

The police chief looked at her. The pain in his brown eyes had been replaced by a granite-like determination. Loyalty, his greatest virtue and his greatest weakness, had now been forged into a weapon.

Elena nodded. There was nothing more to say.

They left the false sanctuary, leaving behind the white light and the drawings of the dead girl. The outer corridor seemed even darker in contrast. The sound of the turbines grew as they advanced, transforming from a hum into a physical roar that made their teeth rattle.

They reached a circular gallery that surrounded the dam's central shaft. Below, in the darkness, the black water churned. A grated bridge crossed the void towards a suspended central structure: the sluice gate control room, the antechamber to the Atrium.

Elena advanced first, her weapon held close to her body. The air was freezing, sharp as razor blades. Her breath formed white clouds that quickly dissipated in the forced airflow.

Halfway across the bridge, something caught her attention. On the grated floor, a drop of fresh blood gleamed under the dim light, red and viscous. Arnald had passed this way recently. The wound he had tended in the sanctuary must have reopened from the exertion. He was human. He bled. He could die.

That thought was the anchor Elena clung to as a glacial fear struggled to immobilize her legs. Arnald Soler was not a ghost, nor a supernatural monster, nor a force of nature. He was an arrogant, wounded, and desperate man.

They reached the end of the walkway. Before them stood the entrance to the Atrium, two colossal doors of riveted metal. There was no electronic lock, only a manual opening wheel, heavy and cold.
