Lucas positioned himself on one side of the frame, Elena on the other. Their gazes met one last time. In that silent exchange, everything they couldn't express with words condensed: fear, trust, and the certainty that one or perhaps both of them would not see the dawn.

Elena gripped the wheel with both hands. The metal bit into her palms through her gloves. She pulled hard. The mechanism groaned, a protest of rusted steel that echoed through the concrete cavern. She turned it once. Twice.

The door unlatched with a dull *clank* that reverberated like a gunshot.

Elena pushed. The metal leaf swung inward, revealing a vast darkness, broken only by the bluish glow of control panels and steam rising from the floor like fog in a graveyard.

The cold that poured from the room hit their faces, so intense it burned.

"Let's go," Elena said, and took the first step towards the end of everything.

## Chapter 46: Capítulo 46

The roar of the turbines in the bowels of the Cavallers dam was not a sound, but a tectonic vibration that ascended through the soles of their boots, making their teeth chatter. Elena Vives forced herself to ignore the pressure of thousands of tons of water looming above their heads, separated from them by mere meters of reinforced concrete. Her attention, sharpened by the panic she struggled to tame, converged on a single point: the reinforced polycarbonate cylinder situated in the center of the engine room.

Inside, suspended in a viscous, translucent solution, Julia floated.

She didn't look dead. She looked like an offering to a winter god. Her hair formed a static halo around her pale face, and the bluish illumination from the control panels lent her skin the gleam of antique porcelain.

“Don't touch her,” Doctor Arnald Soler's voice cut through the glacial air with the precision of a scalpel.

Lucas Riera, standing three paces from the doctor, adjusted his grip on his service weapon. His knuckles, white with tension, contrasted sharply with the dark metal. The police officer's right leg trembled imperceptibly, the phantom pain of an old injury awakened by the tunnel's aggressive humidity, but his stance remained as firm as the mountain's bedrock.

“Step away from the console, Arnald,” Lucas ordered. His tone lacked the usual warmth of the local police chief; it was gravel and warning.

The Sculptor, now stripped of his mask as an affable mentor, did not flinch. His ice-blue eyes, inexpressive and devoid of any human remorse, shifted from Lucas's pistol to Elena's face. There was an obscene neatness about him; even in that industrial basement, he exuded that clinical scent of mint and antiseptic that Elena now recognized as the aroma of madness.

“If I step away, she dies,” Arnald said with didactic calm, as if explaining a routine diagnosis. “The vitrification process is unstable. If you abruptly interrupt the cryoprotectant flow, the water in her cells will expand. Ice crystals will tear her apart from within. It will be... biologically disastrous. And aesthetically unforgivable.”

Elena felt an acidic nausea rise in her throat. She glanced at her father's watch on her left wrist. The hands advanced relentlessly. The storm outside, the “White Beast,” howled so loudly that its lament seeped through the ventilation shafts, competing with the turbines.

“You put her in there,” Elena's whisper was more terrifying than a scream. Her moss-green eyes, usually analytical, burned with a primitive fury. “You're the one who knows how to get her out. Do it. Now.”

She moved closer to the tank, searching for any sign of life in the girl's chest. The crescent-shaped scar on her left palm itched, a somatic sign that always preceded disaster.

“Elena, my dear, you were always my brightest student, but you lack vision,” Arnald typed a sequence into the panel. An amber light flickered at the base of the cylinder. “I didn't put her in there. I preserved her. The outside world is decay, entropy. Here, in the ice, beauty is absolute. Eternal. Didn't you seek order in the chaos of glaciers? This is the supreme order.”

“Defrost, Arnald!” Lucas bellowed, stepping forward, dragging his heavy boot across the metal grating. The metallic echo resonated like a misfired shot.

The doctor sighed, a theatrical gesture of disappointment.

“Very well. But it requires precision. A single degree of error, and thermal shock will stop her heart before it can beat again.”

Arnald began to manipulate the hydraulic valves. The liquid inside the tank began to bubble, its level slowly descending. The scent of ozone and chemicals intensified, mingling with the stale humidity of the subsoil.

Elena observed every movement of the doctor's hands. A surgeon's hands. A killer's hands. Her profiler's mind, which she had tried to silence for years under layers of snow and geological study, now worked at full throttle. *Narcissist. Complete control. He feels superior to us. He won't yield his masterpiece without one last act of arrogance.*

“Lucas, don't take your eyes off him,” she warned, circling the tank to position herself on the other side, searching for a manual emergency valve. “He's enjoying this.”

“I've got him, Elena. One false move and I'll blow his knee out.”

The liquid drained, leaving Julia exposed to the cold air of the room. Her body slumped gently against the curved glass, inert. Her skin had an unnatural bluish tint.

“Phase one complete,” Arnald announced without looking at them. “Now, thermal resuscitation. I need access to the secondary panel. It's behind you, Lucas.”

The police officer didn't move.

“Tell me what to touch. You're not moving from there.”

Arnald smiled, a thin grimace that didn't reach his eyes.

“It's a biometric security system, Lucas. Fingerprint. Unless you want to cut off my hand, you'll have to let me through.”

Lucas hesitated for a fraction of a second. His eyes sought Elena's. She nodded slightly, her stomach a knot of wire. They had no choice. Julia's life hung on the technical vanity of a monster.

“Slowly,” Lucas growled, pivoting on his good leg to let him pass, but keeping the barrel of his pistol pressed against the doctor's ribs.

Arnald advanced. As he passed Lucas, the entire building shook.

It wasn't a metaphor. A violent jolt ran through the concrete structure. The room's lights flickered and died for two eternal seconds before the emergency generators kicked in, bathing the space in a nightmarish red light.

“The power grid is down,” Elena said, her voice trembling for the first time. Claustrophobia, that old enemy, clawed at the walls of her mind. The darkness, the weight of the mountain, the confinement...

“Life support systems will fail in three minutes,” Arnald informed, his voice strangely animated by the chaos. “If we don't manually stabilize the temperature, she will die.”

Capitalizing on the distraction of the blackout and the change in light, Arnald acted. He didn't go towards the biometric panel. With a fluid movement, uncharacteristic of his age, he spun on his heels and struck Lucas's bad knee with his elbow.

The crunch was audible. Lucas bellowed in pain, and his right leg gave way beneath his weight, sending him to his knees. The shot went wide, hitting a steam pipe that burst with a deafening hiss, filling the space between them with scorching white mist.

“Lucas!” Elena cried, lunging forward.

Arnald was no longer by the panel. He had merged with the cloud of steam, a shadow moving with purpose.

“The tank, Elena!” Lucas shouted from the floor, struggling to stand as he clutched his leg. “Forget about me, save the girl!”

Elena stopped dead, torn between the instinct to help her partner and the need to protect the victim. Through the mist, she saw Arnald's silhouette manipulating a red lever beside the emergency exit.

“Beauty is ephemeral if not cared for, Elena,” the doctor's voice came, distorted by the roar of the steam. “But tragedy... tragedy lasts forever.”

He pulled the lever.

A heavy, final mechanical sound resonated through the room. The tunnel's security gates began to descend. He wasn't just escaping; he was trapping them. And worse still, he had activated the tank's purge protocol.

The cylinder where Julia lay began to vibrate. It wasn't warming. It was cooling again, rapidly, seeking absolute zero.

Elena ran to the console, her hands flying over controls she barely understood, guided only by desperate logic and the technical manuals she had devoured nights ago.

“Lucas, the door!” she cried without looking back. “If it closes, we'll run out of oxygen!”

Lucas, his face beaded with sweat from the agonizing pain, crawled. He couldn't walk, but he could shoot. He aimed at the hydraulic control box that regulated the descending gate. His large, calloused hands, usually steady, trembled with the effort of maintaining consciousness.

*One shot. I only have one shot before the steam blinds me.*

The roar of the dam seemed to mock them. Elena fought against the machine; Lucas fought against his own body. And Arnald Soler, the Sculptor, disappeared into the darkness of the service tunnels, leaving his broken masterpiece behind.

Elena twisted a valve, burning the palm of her hand on the icy metal. Julia's temperature indicator stopped in the critical zone.

“Hold on, girl!” she pleaded with the inert body. “Don't you dare give up now!”

Lucas's shot thundered in the room, a dry sound that seemed to stop time.

## Chapter 47: Capítulo 47

**Cavallers Dam, Vall de Boí**
**December 26, 2018**
**04:32**

The sound wasn't heard; it was felt in the marrow of her bones. A low-pitched roar, a tectonic vibration born in the mountain's depths, ascending through the concrete to rattle Elena Vives's teeth.

She was in the Atrium.

It wasn't a cathedral, but the belly of an engineering beast. The metal catwalk beneath her boots vibrated with the fury of thousands of liters of water pressing against the floodgates. Elena raised her tactical flashlight, its beam slicing through the absolute darkness like a scalpel in an infected wound. The air smelled of ancient damp, of silt, and of that static electricity that precedes tragedies.

"Lucas?" Her voice broke, insignificant against the roar of the hydraulic machinery.

She pointed downwards. The abyss opened at her feet, a fifteen-meter drop into the Valve Chamber.

He was there.

The sight struck her chest with the force of a physical hammer. Lucas Riera was nothing more than a dark smudge struggling against a rising, relentless black tide. The water reached his neck. His face, bathed in Elena's circle of light, was the color of old wax. He wasn't swimming; he floated with the clumsiness of one who has lost the battle against the cold.

"Lucas!" she screamed again, this time tearing her throat.

He raised his head. The movement was slow, agonizing, as if the air weighed as much as the water. His eyes, usually warm and dark, were two wells of glassy confusion. Hypothermia was no longer a threat; it was a sentence being carried out.

Elena swept the perimeter with her flashlight, searching for a ladder, a control panel, anything that wasn't rusted steel and vertical walls. The light illuminated a silhouette at the opposite end of the catwalk, barely ten meters from her.

She froze. Elena's heart, which until that moment had been galloping wildly, skipped a beat and then hung suspended in icy stillness.

The Sculptor.

He stood there, still as a Greek statue. He wore no mask, but shadows sculpted his features so that only a tense jawline and the specular gleam of ice-blue eyes were discernible. He wore an immaculate gray wool coat, an anomaly of elegance in that industrial hell.

Elena raised her service weapon. The weight of the steel in her right hand was the only thing that anchored her to reality.

"Get away from the controls!" she ordered. Her police training tried to take over, but her hands trembled, not from fear, but from the fury burning in her blood.

The man didn't flinch. He didn't raise his hands. He didn't even blink. He simply tilted his head to one side, observing her with the clinical curiosity of an entomologist studying an insect trapped in amber.

"Beauty requires sacrifice, Agent Vives," his voice came clear, projected with perfect diction that cut through the ambient noise. "Cold preserves. Water purifies. He is about to become something eternal."

"Close the damn valve!"

"The choice is yours," he said, gesturing minimally towards the abyss with his gloved hand. "You can pursue me. I have the exit key. Or you can try to get him out before the water level reaches the ceiling. You have..." he glanced at an imaginary watch on his wrist "...less than three minutes before total immersion."

The water below roared, rising another foot. Lucas coughed, a wet, pitiful sound that crawled up the silo walls.

Elena looked at the man. She had a clear shot. She could end the nightmare that had ravaged the valley. She could avenge the frozen girls. But if she shot, if she ran towards him to take the key, Lucas would drown in the darkness.

The Sculptor knew. It was his masterpiece: not a corpse in ice, but Elena's living guilt.

"Son of a bitch," she whispered.

She holstered her pistol with a brusque movement, accepting moral defeat to win the battle for life. The Sculptor gave her one last look, a mix of disappointment and respect, then turned and disappeared through a service door that closed behind him with a definitive metallic *click*.

Elena ran to the railing.

"Hold on!" she roared down into the shaft.

She spotted a maintenance ladder, a structure of iron bars embedded in the concrete, slick with decades of mold. She didn't think twice. She launched herself into the void, clinging to the rungs with a desperation that ignored the pain.

The cold metal bit into the crescent-shaped scar on her left palm, sending a jolt of pain up to her elbow. She ignored it. She descended, jumping three rungs at a time, her boots slipping on the verdigris. The dampness clung to her clothes, heavy and suffocating.

When she reached the water level, the cold struck her like a physical slap. It wasn't just ice water; it was liquid pain, a thousand needles plunging simultaneously into every pore of her skin.

She let go of the ladder and plunged in up to her chest. The impact stole the air from her lungs. She gasped, searching for oxygen, and the taste of oil and metal filled her mouth.

"Lucas!"

He was a few meters away, clinging to a vertical pipe, his chin barely above the black surface. His lips were a bluish, trembling line.

"E-Elena..." The name emerged as a broken whisper.

She swam towards him. Her wet clothes weighed tons, dragging her downwards, but adrenaline was a powerful engine. She reached his side and slipped her arm under his armpits.

The contact was terrifying. Lucas was either burning with fever or so cold his skin scorched; her brain couldn't distinguish the thermal difference.

"I've got you. I've got you, do you hear me? Don't you dare close your eyes."

"My leg..." he mumbled, his head falling onto her shoulder. "Stuck..."

Elena submerged. The murky water was impenetrable to sight, so she had to use touch. Her fingers traced Lucas's thigh, feeling the soaked uniform fabric, until they reached his shin. His foot was wedged between two grates on the chamber floor.

The water continued to rise. It already brushed Elena's chin.

"I'm going to push," she said, surfacing and spitting out water. "On the count of three. You have to pull up. Lucas! Look at me!"

He opened his eyes. There was surrender in them. That stupid, deadly calm that precedes the end.

"No... leave. You won't make it..."

"Shut up!" she yelled at him, and slapped him. The sound was sharp, brutal. Lucas's head snapped back, and some life returned to his gaze, even if it was anger. "You are not leaving me alone with this! One!"

Elena submerged again.

She braced her feet on the grate, found a foothold, and grabbed Lucas's ankle with both hands.

"Two!"

She pulled. She pulled with everything she had, with the rage of the failed case from years ago, with the fear of losing the only person who anchored her to the world, with the brute force of survival.

"THREE!"

She felt Lucas's boot come free with a dull crunch. Perhaps she had broken something, but he was free.

They broke the surface, gasping, coughing water and bile. The water level already covered the lower ventilation grate. Little breathable air remained before the chamber would seal with liquid.

"The ladder..." Elena gasped, dragging Lucas towards the wall.

He tried to help, weakly kicking, but his right leg was dead weight. Elena had to carry almost his entire weight. Every stroke was torture; her muscles burned with lactic acid and the cold began to numb her fingers.

They reached the iron rungs.

"Climb. You have to climb."

"I can't... my hands... I can't feel them."

Elena positioned herself behind him. She pushed his backside, forcing him to grip the first rung.

"Climb or I'll shoot you in the ass myself, Riera. Move!"

Lucas let out a guttural grunt and began the ascent. It was slow, arduous. Elena climbed pressed against him, using her own head and shoulders to support him if he slipped. One step. Another. The sound of water filling the void beneath them transformed into a hungry gurgle, licking at the soles of their boots.

Fifteen meters. A vertical eternity.

When Lucas's hand touched the edge of the upper catwalk, his strength collapsed. He slipped.

Elena reacted purely by instinct. She let go of one hand from the ladder, hanging only by her right, and grabbed Lucas's belt in mid-air. The yank almost dislocated her shoulder. She screamed, a cry that echoed off the concrete dome, but she didn't let go.

Her fingers slipped on the wet leather.

"Up!" she shrieked, and with a final titanic effort, fueled by absolute terror, she hoisted Lucas high enough for him to heave his torso over the metal grate.

He rolled onto the safety of the catwalk, coughing violently. Elena scrambled after him and collapsed onto the cold metal, trembling uncontrollably.

For a minute, there was only the sound of their ragged breaths and the roar of the water below, which finally reached the level of the lower catwalk, engulfing it completely. If they had taken thirty more seconds, they would be dead.

Elena forced herself up. The world spun. She crawled to Lucas.

He was curled into a fetal position, shivering with violent spasms. Elena touched his face. He was icy. He needed urgent warmth, or his heart would go into fibrillation.

"Lucas..." She pulled off his soaked jacket with fumbling hands. He offered no resistance. "We have to move."

"He's gone..." he mumbled, his teeth chattering. "I saw him... up there."

"I know." Elena looked toward the door through which the Sculptor had vanished. It was closed. "I know."

She took off her own parka, which, though damp on the outside, retained some body heat in its inner lining, and threw it over him. Then she took out her multi-tool knife and cut Lucas's pant leg.

His ankle was swollen, purple, and deformed. Probably broken. But that was a problem for later. Now, the problem was hypothermia and the killer on the loose.

Elena stood, swaying. She picked up her pistol from the floor, where it had fallen when she collapsed. She checked the chamber. Dry. Ready.

She walked towards the exit door. She gripped the knob.

She turned it.

Locked.

Of course.

She hit the metal with the butt of her weapon, frustrated. The sound was hollow, mocking.

"Elena..." Lucas's voice was stronger now, fear lending it clarity. "Look at that."

He pointed toward the wall next to the door.

Elena focused her flashlight.

Something was taped to the concrete with industrial adhesive tape. A transparent plastic envelope, hermetically sealed. Inside, a sheet of thick, cream-colored paper.

Elena approached. She tore off the envelope and ripped the plastic.

The handwriting was antique calligraphy, elegant, fountain pen strokes in dark blue ink.

"The true work of art is not death, but transformation. Today you chose life, Agent Vives. You have altered the composition. But every action has an opposite reaction. While we played our game here, nature has taken its course outside. The storm has closed the valley. And I hold the only key to the dam's central heating. Enjoy the winter."

The flashlight flickered once. Twice.

And then, the entire electrical system of the Atrium died.

The roar of the turbines changed pitch. It stopped. The silence that followed was more terrifying than the noise. It wasn't peace; it was the sound of machinery grinding to a halt, of the dam's life support systems shutting down one by one. The darkness was total, dense as tar.

Elena turned her flashlight on again, but the beam seemed weaker now, swallowed by the immensity of the hall. The cold began to descend, no longer from the water, but from the air itself, an invisible claw tightening around her throat.

No heating. No electricity. Wet. Isolated.

Elena turned towards Lucas, illuminating his pale face.

"We haven't won," she whispered, feeling how the real game had just begun. "We've only changed cells."

## Chapter 48: Capítulo 48

**Cavallers Dam, Vall de Boí**
**December 26, 2018**

The sound of the bolt sliding was not a metallic click, but the definitive clang of a judgment.

Elena Vives stood motionless in front of the reinforced steel door, her palm still extended towards the knob that no longer turned. The echo of the lock reverberated against the bare concrete walls of the Atrium, multiplying into a mocking cacophony that took seconds to die. When it did, what remained was not silence. It was an absence of sound so absolute that her own ears began to hum, desperately searching for a frequency that no longer existed.

The darkness was almost total, barely rent by the trembling beam of her tactical flashlight. The air, which seconds before had smelled of stagnant damp, now carried a new, sharp, crystalline nuance: the scent of snow as it hardens.

"Elena!"

Lucas Riera's voice sounded watery, broken by an involuntary chattering of teeth.

She spun on her heels, sweeping the space with the light. The chief of police lay against the walkway railing, his right leg stretched at an unnatural angle. The parka she had lent him was soaked, heavy as a lead slab on his body, but it was the only barrier between his skin and the hypothermia that was already claiming its due.

Elena knelt beside him. There was no hesitation, no time for panic. Her mind, trained in the worst scenarios of human behavior, filed the fear away in a hermetic mental box and focused on the mechanics of survival.

"Don't move," she ordered. Her voice came out hoarse, a cloud of white vapor escaping her lips to dissolve into the darkness. "I need to see that ankle."

Lucas attempted a smile that ended in a grimace of pain. His skin was the color of old wax, and his dark eyes, usually warm, shone with the fever of *shock*.

Elena looked down at his hiking boot. The leather was deformed by internal swelling. She didn't dare unfasten it; the boot's compression was the only thing keeping the joint stable and, likely, containing the internal bleeding.

"The boot stays on," she dictated, running her gloved hands over his shin, searching for exposed fractures. There were none. Lucas let out a muffled groan when she pressed a sensitive spot above his malleolus. "Grade three sprain, or a clean break. Either way, you can't walk."

"I have to. He's out there. Or in here."

"He controls the board, Lucas. We're the pieces he just swept off the table." Elena stood up, illuminating the vast space that opened before them.

The Atrium was an industrial cathedral. Pipes the thickness of a human torso ran along the walls like metallic varicose veins, disappearing into the height of a vault that the flashlight couldn't reach to reveal. Below, in the darkness of the pit, the distant hum of the turbines could be sensed, an underground heartbeat that made the floor vibrate beneath their soles.

It was cold. An aggressive, physical cold that not only lowered skin temperature but seemed to slow thought.

"Do you have the weapon?" Lucas asked, trying to push himself up onto his elbows.

Elena patted her side. The Glock 19 was still in its holster, a comforting weight but useless against the invisible enemy that now stalked them: the sub-zero temperatures.

"I have the weapon. I have your phone, but down here it's an inert brick. And we have..." She looked at her wrist watch, her father's old analog one. The hands glowed faintly. "Perhaps three hours before your core temperature drops to critical levels. We need heat."

"The control room... there must be... an emergency panel."

"Stay here. And for heaven's sake, try not to fall asleep."

Elena walked away. Her footsteps echoed with an obscene clarity on the metal walkway. Every meter that separated her from Lucas increased the pressure in her chest, that old claw of anxiety that constricted her throat in confined spaces. *Breathe. Analyze. Execute.*

She followed the luminous path to a fuse box attached to a load-bearing pillar. The lid was open. As she illuminated the interior, Elena felt a nausea that had nothing to do with the cold.

It wasn't a short circuit. The wires hadn't been burned by an overload. They had been extracted. Not cut, but unscrewed with surgical precision and removed. The empty sockets where the heating relays should have been looked like empty eye sockets staring at her.

"Damn son of a bitch!" she whispered.

The Sculptor didn't improvise. This wasn't a reaction to their arrival; it was a prepared scenario. He had turned the dam into a giant refrigerator, patiently waiting for the meat to enter to be preserved.

She continued her walk along the platform, circling the Atrium's circular perimeter. The structure was labyrinthine. She passed rusted valves and pressure gauges whose glass was fogged with frost that was beginning to form inside.

Then she saw it.

At the end of the corridor, a half-open door cast a faint, almost imperceptible line of light onto the gridded floor. It wasn't conventional electric light. It was a bluish, chemical glow.

Elena drew her weapon. The metal of the pistol burned with cold. She advanced close to the wall, controlling her breathing so that her vapor wouldn't betray her position before her body did.

She peered into the doorway.

No one was there. The room was an old maintenance office, now stripped of any useful furniture. In the center, on a plastic folding chair, rested an LED camping lamp, the source of that spectral light.

And next to the lamp, a sheet of paper.

Elena entered, sweeping the corners with the barrel of her weapon. Clear. She approached the chair. The paper was a thick, high-quality sheet, artistic in its incongruity in that environment of grease and metal.

A sentence was written in impeccable, almost architectural calligraphy, in black ink:

"Beauty is only eternal if time stands still. Welcome to my museum."

Below the note, there was a small object. Elena illuminated it. It was an industrial digital thermometer, of the kind used to calibrate turbines. The red numbers blinked, descending with hypnotic speed.

*—8 °C.*
*—8.5 °C.*

Elena frowned. The outside temperature was low, but the dam's insulation should keep the interior above freezing thanks to the thermal inertia of the concrete and water. Unless...

A dull hum began to emanate from the ventilation grates in the ceiling. It wasn't the sound of the heating starting. It was a constant, dry blow. Elena took off a glove and raised her hand towards the duct.

The air coming out wasn't recirculating the environment. It was a direct injection of polar air, forced from outside and, possibly, cooled even further by some inverted heat exchange system.

The Sculptor hadn't just turned off the heating. He had turned the ventilation system into a rapid-freeze weapon.

"Elena!"

Lucas's urgent cry tore her from her analysis.

She ran back along the walkway, ignoring the burning in her lungs as she inhaled the icy air. Her flashlight's beam danced chaotically on the curved walls.

When she reached Lucas's position, she found him trying to drag himself backward, away from the railing.

"What's wrong?" Elena knelt, pointing towards where he was looking.

Lucas pointed down, into the dark pit of the Atrium.

"Listen!" he said, his voice trembling violently. "The water."

Elena strained her ears. The hum of the turbines had changed. It was no longer a constant drone. Now there was a gurgling, a liquid and rising sound.

She leaned over the railing, illuminating the void. The light beam was lost in the depth, but something reflected it back: a black, oily surface, agitated.

"He's opened the bottom valves," Elena realized, feeling the blood drain from her face. "He's flooding the lower chamber to increase humidity."

"Humidity and extreme cold," Lucas murmured, understanding the elemental physics of death. "He's going to turn us into ice."

Elena looked at her companion. Frost was already beginning to glisten on the police officer's eyelashes. The pain in his leg, the bone-chilling cold, the water level rising like a black tide... everything was designed to induce despair.

"Get up!" she said, pulling his good arm with a strength she didn't know she still had. "We have to get to the crest level. Now!"

"I can't..."

"I said get up!" she yelled, her voice breaking the deadly calm of the enclosure. Her green eyes, usually analytical and distant, burned with a primitive fury. "I'm not going to let you become part of his damned collection."

Lucas nodded, gritting his teeth until his jaw ached. He leaned on her, shifting his full weight onto Elena's left shoulder. She stumbled for an instant, readjusting her center of gravity, assuming both the physical and emotional burden.

Together, they formed a clumsy, limping silhouette under the bluish light filtering from the office, advancing towards the service stairs while the digital thermometer, forgotten on the chair, marked a new descent.

*—10 °C.*

And the water kept rising.

## Chapter 49: Capítulo 49

The concrete corridor descended into the mountain's depths, an artificial throat that swallowed the sound of the storm outside. Down here, the wind's howl had been replaced by an electrical hum, low and constant, like the heartbeat of a dying animal.

Elena Vives moved forward, pressed against the left wall, feeling the rough, cold cement through her gloves. Her breath condensed into ephemeral clouds that the flickering fluorescent light tinted gray. Behind her, the irregular rhythm of footsteps betrayed Lucas Riera's effort. The police chief dragged his right leg; the pain from his old injury, exacerbated by the humidity and drop in barometric pressure, must have been torture, but he hadn't uttered a single complaint.

"Not much further," Elena whispered, not so much to inform as to convince herself.

She checked her father's watch. The analog face showed three in the morning, though in that bunker, time seemed to have dissolved. The second hand advanced with cruel indifference.

"It smells like him," Lucas growled. His voice was raspy, laden with fatigue.

He was right. The air, until then stale with mold and subterranean dampness, began to permeate with a clinical scent. Menthol and industrial disinfectant. A pristine smell that, in that context of darkness and danger, was more nauseating than rot. It was the olfactory signature of the Sculptor.

They reached a fork. The main corridor ended at a reinforced steel door, hermetically sealed. To the right, a gutted control panel showed colorful wires like exposed veins.

Elena approached the mechanism. Her numb fingers traced the path of the wires.

"It's an airlock system," she declared, her profiler's mind switching to analytical mode to quell the apprehension crawling up her stomach. "It needs a code or a physical key."

Lucas leaned against the wall, taking weight off his bad leg. He clutched his service weapon with calloused hands, knuckles white with tension.

"Or a bit of brute persuasion," he said, pointing at the rusted hinges. "Arnald isn't an engineer. He adapted this old mine, but the structure is giving way."

Before they could decide on a method, a metallic click resonated above their heads. The speakers, hidden somewhere in the vaulted ceiling, came alive with a hiss of static.

"Impatience is a flaw of living flesh," Dr. Arnald Soler's voice filled the corridor, amplified and devoid of any human warmth. "Perfection requires pause. It requires... stillness."

Elena felt the hairs on the back of her neck bristle. It wasn't a recording. He was watching them.

"Soler!" Lucas shouted, pointing his weapon at the security camera that blinked with a red light in the upper corner. "It's over! Come out with your hands up."

A soft laugh, dry as dead leaves, emanated from the speakers.

"Lucas, always so predictable. So... dynamic. But your role in this play has concluded."

The floor beneath their feet vibrated. It wasn't an earthquake, but the activation of heavy machinery. A security barrier, a thick sheet of metal that had been hidden in the ceiling, began to descend rapidly between them, separating Elena and the police officer.

"Lucas!" Elena lunged forward, but instinct stopped her mere inches from the falling steel guillotine.

"Get back, Elena!" he roared.

The clang of metal hitting concrete sealed the corridor. The echo of the impact reverberated in Elena's chest, leaving her in utter and complete silence. She was alone on the isolated side. Lucas had been left behind.

"No..." she gasped, striking the cold surface with her open palm, the crescent-shaped scar on her left hand burning from the impact.

"Solitude sharpens the senses, doesn't it, Elena?" Arnald's voice returned, now more intimate, as if whispering directly into her ear. "He was a hindrance. A chaotic variable. You and I share the search for truth. Come. The Sanctuary is open for you."

The electronic lock on the door in front of her emitted a sharp beep and the red light changed to green. The mechanism released with a hydraulic sigh.

Elena looked back at the barrier separating her from her ally. A wave of panic, that old shadow that had haunted her since her last failed case, tried to close its grip around her throat. She felt the pressure in her chest, the lack of air, the certainty that she was trapped in a tomb of ice and metal. *Emotional claustrophobia*.

"Order in chaos," she repeated mentally. "Analyze. Execute."

Lucas was injured, yes. But Lucas Riera was the immovable force of Aigüestortes. If Arnald thought a metal door would stop the man who had carried the weight of an entire valley for years, he didn't know him.

Elena couldn't wait. If she entered the Sculptor's game, if she advanced alone into the trap, she would die. And Lucas would die trying to save her. She had to change the rules.

She ignored the open door that invited her into the wolf's den. Instead, she rummaged in her pockets. She pulled out the small tactical flashlight she had taken from the supply kit and, instead of lighting the way, used it as a hammer. She struck the exposed fuse box next to the airlock panel.

One, two, three dry strikes. The plastic shattered. Blue sparks flew, illuminating her moss-green eyes.

"Come on, Lucas," she whispered, her voice broken by tension. "I know you're there."

On the other side of the metal wall, a muffled thud was heard. Then another. A rhythmic, brutal sound. Metal against metal.

"Elena!" Lucas's voice, though muffled, resonated with firmness. "Get away from the door!"

She ran to the opposite end of the narrow corridor, covering her head.

There was a contained boom. Not an explosion, but the sound of physics yielding to brute force. Lucas must have shot at the hinges or used some heavy industrial object to pry open the damaged mechanism.

With a blood-curdling screech, the metal sheet buckled at the bottom. Large hands, fingers stained with grease and blood, appeared through the gap, grabbing the bottom edge.

Elena saw the muscles of Lucas's forearms tense beneath the fabric of his uniform. With a roar of effort that mingled pain and fury, the police officer lifted the barrier just enough, wedging it open with a piece of pipe he had ripped from the wall.

He squeezed through the opening, falling to his knees on the other side, beside her. He was breathing heavily, sweat beading on his forehead, mixing with the gray dust of the bunker. His right leg trembled visibly, unable to support him for a moment.

Elena knelt beside him, gripping his shoulders.

"I thought..." she began, but the words caught in her throat.

Lucas looked up. His dark, usually tired eyes now burned with fierce determination. He wiped the back of his hand across his mouth, cleaning away a trickle of blood.

"That bastard..." Lucas gasped, forcing himself to his feet, using Elena as momentary support until he regained his balance. "He underestimated me. He thinks I'm an old cripple."

"You *are* an old cripple," Elena said, and for the first time in hours, a fleeting, nervous smile crossed her face. "But you're my old cripple."

Lucas reloaded his weapon with a dry motion. The 'clack-clack' of the slide sounded like a death knell.

"Arnald made a mistake," he said, looking at the open door at the end of the corridor, that dark maw leading to the laboratory. "He wanted to divide us. Now he has both of us, and I'm really pissed off."

The Sculptor's voice was no longer coming from the speakers. Silence had returned, but now it was different. It wasn't the stillness of observation, but the tension before an assault. Arnald knew they had bypassed his first filter. The final match had begun.

Elena stood up, dusting off her pants. Her gaze hardened. Fear was still there, an unwanted passenger in her stomach, but Lucas's solid presence beside her acted as an anchor.

They advanced together towards the threshold. The smell of mint became more intense, almost unbearable, mixing with the low temperature emanating from the adjoining room. It was an unnatural, chemical cold, designed to halt life in a perfect instant.

They stopped in front of the door frame. Elena looked at Lucas and nodded once. Lucas returned the gesture, adjusting his grip on his pistol.

There was no need for words. They were going to enter the frozen hell, but they would enter standing and together. Elena extended her hand and pushed the steel sheet.

## Chapter 50: Capítulo 50

The air inside the Cavallers dam lacked the purity of the mountains; it tasted of rust, stale hydraulic oil, and an artificial cold born not of winter, but of chemistry. Elena Vives felt her breath freeze on her lips before she could even exhale. They were in the belly of the concrete beast, an abandoned auxiliary turbine room that Doctor Arnald Soler had transformed into his final operating theater.

There were no natural stalactites here, only pressure-burst pipes hanging from the steel girders like metallic intestines, dripping a coolant liquid that hissed upon touching the polished concrete floor. The fluorescent lights flickered, casting spasmodic shadows onto the gray walls, stained with dampness and years of structural neglect.

“Art requires permanence, Elena,” Arnald’s voice echoed in the metallic acoustics of the chamber.

The Sculptor stood by the floodgate controls, but his attention wasn't on the water contained behind the wall, but on the industrial cryogenic tank situated in the center of the room. His eyes, that inexpressive blue Elena had mistaken for clinical serenity, now glowed with the fever of culmination. The scent of antiseptic and mint emanating from him managed to overpower even the stench of old grease from the machinery.

Lucas Riera moved to Elena’s right. The police chief dragged his leg; the pain in his knee must have been a constant scream in that glacial environment, but his large, calloused hands held his service weapon with a firmness that belied his exhaustion.

“It’s over, Arnald,” Lucas growled. His voice was raspy, laden with the fatigue of three days of pursuit. “There’s no way out. The maintenance tunnels are blocked.”

“Way out?” Arnald smiled, a neat gesture that didn’t reach his eyes. “I’m not looking to flee, Lucas. I’m looking to preserve.”

The doctor raised a hand, revealing a remote detonator. It wasn’t connected to conventional explosives, but to the pressure valves of the massive refrigeration system feeding the tank.

Elena analyzed the surroundings with the speed of someone who had spent years studying ice fractures. But this wasn’t glacial ice; it was human engineering pushed to its limit. The concrete vault above their heads showed fine cracks, dark capillaries in the artificial stone caused by the vibrations of the external storm and internal thermal stress.

“If you open those valves abruptly,” Elena said, keeping her tone low, clinical, trying to appeal to the logic of the man of science Arnald believed himself to be, “thermal expansion will shatter the foundations. You won’t preserve anything. This room will become a tomb of rubble, not a museum.”

Arnald tilted his head, as if considering an interesting diagnosis.

“Destruction is merely a phase of transformation.”

The doctor’s finger tensed on the button.

“Now!” Lucas yelled.

The police officer lunged forward, ignoring the protest of his injured leg, trying to close the distance. Arnald reacted with surprising speed, retreating to the cover of the main tank as he pressed the detonator.

A deafening hiss filled the concrete chamber. There was no fire, only a dense, white cloud of liquid nitrogen that erupted from the auxiliary valves, devouring oxygen and temperature in micro-seconds.

Elena felt the bite of absolute cold on the exposed skin of her face. Her lungs constricted. She saw Lucas stumble as the floor beneath his boots instantly coated with a layer of slippery frost. Arnald, protected by his position behind the tank and a mask he had donned with practiced movements, prepared to fire at the fallen officer.

Time fractured in Elena’s mind. She couldn’t reach Lucas. She couldn’t disarm Arnald. Her moss-green gaze locked onto the industrial structure’s only vulnerable point: the main injector of the nitrogen tank, a chrome-steel pipe vibrating under unstable pressure.

*Order in chaos.*

She raised the weapon she had taken from the body of a fallen agent in the upper tunnels. Her hands didn’t tremble; fear had sublimated into a geometric clarity. She aimed not at the man, but at the machine.

The shot thundered in the confined space, sounding more like a hammer blow against an anvil than a detonation.

The bullet pierced the injector.

The result wasn’t an explosion, but an implosion of temperature. The tank tore open with the shriek of tortured metal. A geyser of white vapor, nearly two hundred degrees below zero, shot out with the force of a hydraulic cannon, striking directly against the load-bearing girders supporting the room’s ceiling.

The steel, subjected to an impossible thermal shock, screamed. The main girders snapped with a crash that vibrated Elena’s dental fillings.

“Lucas, take cover!” she roared, diving behind a rusted turbine.

The concrete vault, deprived of its support and cracked by the sudden contraction of the cold, gave way.

It wasn’t a gradual collapse. It was a total collapse. Vehicle-sized concrete blocks detached from the ceiling, dragging electrical wiring networks and ventilation ducts with them. Construction dust mixed with the nitrogen mist, creating a white and gray blindness.

Arnald Soler screamed. It wasn’t a scream of pain, but of fury at seeing his masterpiece interrupted. He tried to run towards the service exit, but the floor beneath his feet fractured. A section of the elevated walkway, made of metal grating and safety railings, twisted as if it were soft wire under the impact of a slab of reinforced concrete.

Elena curled into a fetal position, covering her head as the world came crashing down. She felt the impact of debris bouncing off her back, dust filling her mouth, the coppery taste of fear on her tongue. The noise was apocalyptic: the roar of stone breaking, the lament of metal, and, in the background, the distant but growing rumble of the dam's water pressing against walls that had just lost their internal structural integrity.

Then, silence. A dense, heavy stillness, broken only by the dripping of some surviving pipe and the settling of the debris.

Elena coughed, expelling plaster and cement dust from her airways. Her ears rang. She forced herself to open her eyes. Visibility was almost nil, a milky gloom barely illuminated by the sparks of a torn cable swinging like a macabre pendulum.

“Lucas?” Her voice came out broken, barely a whisper.

“I’m… here.”

The answer came from her left, muffled but alive. Elena crawled over the debris, ignoring the sharp pain in her knees and the cut on her cheek. She found Lucas half-buried under a sheet of thermal insulation and gravel, but the heavy concrete had fallen just inches from him, forming a triangle of survival thanks to the fallen turbine.

She helped him free himself, her fingers frantically searching for any sign of critical damage. Lucas grunted as he moved his injured leg, but nodded.

“I’m alright. Just bruised.”

Both turned towards where Arnald had been.

There was no longer a tank, no control platform, no floor. Instead, a mountain of twisted girders, fractured concrete slabs, and shattered pipes rose to the ruined ceiling. From amidst the debris, a cold smoke emanated, the last breath of the nitrogen dissipating.

At the base of the collapse, trapped beneath an I-beam and tons of masonry, something gleamed. It was a metallic reflection, pristine, out of place amidst so much dirty destruction.

They approached, limping, breathing with difficulty the rarefied air.

Beneath the mountain of industrial ruins, only one hand was visible. Pale, immaculate, with fingers outstretched as if trying to reach for something. Arnald’s wristwatch continued ticking away the seconds with indifferent precision. The rest of the Sculptor had become fused with the concrete and ice, buried forever in the foundations of the dam he had intended to use as a sanctuary.

Elena looked at the inert hand. She felt no triumph, only a sudden emptiness, as if adrenaline had been drained from her with a syringe. The crescent-shaped scar on her left palm throbbed with the rhythm of her heart.

“It’s over,” Lucas said, his grave voice echoing in the new artificial cavern they had created.

Elena nodded, but her green eyes continued to scan the structure above them.

“The dam is compromised,” she stated, observing a new crack seeping black water on the far wall. “We have to get out. Now.”

The distant roar of water seeking an exit confirmed her analysis. Arnald’s tomb would soon become an underwater grave.

Lucas leaned on Elena’s shoulder. She bore his weight, accepting the physical burden as the only tangible reality in that moment. Together, they turned their backs on the remains of the concrete operating theater and began the arduous ascent to the surface, leaving behind the man who had wanted to freeze time and who was now, ironically, a static part of the architecture.

## Epilogue: Epílogo

**Vielha Hospital, Aran Valley**
**February 14th, one month later**

The smell of disinfectant never quite managed to cover the stale aroma of illness. Elena Vives walked down the third-floor corridor, counting the white tiles, a nervous habit that had survived the storm. 112 steps from the elevator to room 304. Her boots, still caked with dried mud from the thaw, squeaked faintly on the polished linoleum, breaking the aseptic silence of the sanatorium.

She stopped in front of the half-open door. Her left hand, where the crescent-shaped scar throbbed with changes in atmospheric pressure, hesitated over the metal doorknob. Through the glass, the grayish light of a winter afternoon bathed the room.

She pushed the wooden door open.

Lucas Riera was not in bed. The police chief sat in a wheelchair by the window, his right leg in a cast and elevated on a footrest. The light silhouetted his broad frame, now somewhat thinner, accentuating the premature gray hairs at his temples. He looked like a statue weathered by the elements, a monument to exhaustion.

"You shouldn't be up," said Elena. Her voice sounded raspy, as if she hadn't used it in days.

Lucas slowly turned his head. His brown eyes, sunken in sockets darkened by lack of sleep, scrutinized her. There was no reproach in his gaze, only mutual recognition, the silent greeting of two shipwreck survivors.

"The nurses say if I don't move, I'll rust," he replied, managing a half-smile that didn't quite reach his eyes. "And the bed reminds me too much of a slab."

Elena closed the door behind her, shutting out the distant bustle from the nurses' station. She approached the window, keeping a respectful distance, honoring the magnetic field of pain that surrounded the police officer.

"The final forensic report came in this morning," she commented, looking outside. "The mountain peaks, once relentless devourers, now rose indifferently under a leaden sky. They've identified all the remains. Even those that had been under the permafrost for decades."

Lucas nodded, drumming his calloused fingers on the armrest of his chair.

"The thaw always gives back what we lend it," he murmured in a low voice. "The valley has a memory, Elena. More than we do."

"Arnald..." she began, but the word caught in her throat. Her mentor's name, now stained by the shadow of suspicion and tragedy, weighed like a stone in her stomach. "They confirmed his fall wasn't accidental. He knew what he was doing, aware it was the only way to stop the machinery."

"Sometimes, cutting the rope is the only way to keep others from falling into the void," Lucas stated. He rubbed his right thigh just above the cast, a reflex gesture against a phantom pain. "Down here, people are starting to forget. They clear the snow from the sidewalks, open the shops, prepare for the spring season. It's obscene how quickly life covers death."

Elena turned to face him. The fading light caught her asymmetrical bob cut, revealing new lines of expression around her moss-green eyes.

"It's not forgetting, Lucas. It's survival. The same survival that made you drag yourself two kilometers with a shattered tibia to call for help."

He averted his gaze, uncomfortable with the implicit praise. He fumbled in the pocket of his hospital gown, searching for something clumsily.

"I almost forgot. One of the rescue teams found this near the cave entrance, where the snow had receded."

He extended his hand. In his open, large, and rough palm rested a metallic object.

Elena's heart skipped a beat. It was her father's analog watch. The leather strap was darkened by moisture, and the crystal had a diagonal crack splitting the face in two, separating twelve from six.

She took it reverently, as if it were a sacred relic. The metal was cold, but as she brought it to her ear, she heard the faint, rhythmic *tick-tock*.

"It's still working," she whispered, feeling a knot in her throat that threatened to unravel her analytical composure.

"It's old machinery," said Lucas, observing her with an intensity that made her feel vulnerable. "The kind that was made to last, to withstand blows and keep ticking. Like you."

Elena buckled the watch onto her wrist. The familiar weight on her arm acted as an anchor, stabilizing the vertigo she'd felt since leaving the cave. The crack in the crystal didn't prevent her from seeing the time; it only reminded her that time, from now on, would be marked.

"I'm being discharged tomorrow," Lucas announced, breaking the spell. "I suppose you'll go back to Madrid. Your glaciology thesis must be waiting for you, and I doubt you'll want to see snow for a while."

There was a dense silence. Elena looked out the window again. Below, in the parking lot, cars were splashes of color on the gray asphalt. Beyond, the road snaked towards the tunnel, the exit mouth to civilization, to noisy cafes, incessant traffic, and the anonymity of the big city. Towards a life where ice only existed as cubes in a glass.

But then she looked up at the summits. The imposing peaks, which before had seemed like relentless devourers, now stood immutable under a leaden sky. She no longer saw monsters on their slopes. In her mind, tectonic plates, freezing cycles, cracks that needed to be measured and understood, took shape. She understood the natural chaos that had so terrified her and that, paradoxically, was the only thing that now felt honest to her.

"No," she said, with a clarity that surprised her.

"What?"

"I've requested my records be transferred to the Aran Valley monitoring station. They need someone to oversee the residual glaciers. Someone who understands how ice moves from within."

Lucas frowned, incredulous. "Elena, this place almost killed you. This is where your ghosts are."

"My ghosts go with me wherever I go, Lucas. In Madrid, they hide in the corners of my apartment, in the subway's noise. Here, at least, they have space to run." She leaned on the windowsill, crossing her arms. "Besides, someone has to make sure the snow doesn't cover what it shouldn't again."

The police chief watched her for a long time. He searched her green eyes for any trace of madness or unresolved trauma, but found only a quiet determination, solid as bedrock.

"You're staying," he repeated, not as a question, but tasting the affirmation.

"I'm staying."

Lucas nodded slowly. The tension in his shoulders seemed to dissipate, as if he had just released an invisible burden he hadn't known he was carrying.

"Then I suppose we'll see each other. Aigüestortes is a small town."

"I suppose so." Elena managed a faint smile, her first real one in a month. "And you're going to need someone to help you with rehabilitation if you want to patrol those trails again."

"I have a physio assigned," he countered, though the twinkle in his eyes belied his defensive tone.

"Your physio doesn't know a safe crevice from a death trap. I do."

They looked at each other, and in that exchange there were no words of love or eternal promises, only the understanding of two broken pieces that, when joined, formed something different, imperfect but functional. An alliance forged in absolute cold.

Elena stepped away from the window and walked towards the door. Before leaving, she stopped and looked at her father's watch. The hands kept moving, indifferent to the past, devouring the future second by second.

"Rest, Lucas," she said without turning. "Winter is ending, but the mountain is still there."

She stepped into the corridor. The hospital air no longer felt so suffocating. At the end of the hall, the automatic doors opened, letting in a gust of icy wind. Elena did not shiver. She inhaled deeply, filling her lungs with the sharp evening air, and walked outside.

The snow at the edges of the road was melting, turning into dirty water that ran towards the gutters. But high up, on the summits, the white remained pristine, eternal, waiting. There was no more fear. Only a deep and silent respect. Peace was not the absence of storm, she understood as she walked towards her car; peace was the certainty that, when the storm returned, she would be ready to read the signs in the ice.
